The Red Page
Grave-Clods from Thomas Hardy
GOD’B FUNERAL.
I saw a slowly-step ping train —
Lined on the brows, scoop-eyed and bent
and howr —
Following in files across a twilit plain
A strange and mystic form the foremost bore.
And by contagious throbs of thought
Or latent knowledge that within me lay
And had already stirred me, I was wrought
To consciousness of sorrow even as they.
The fore-borne shape, to my blurred eyes,
At first seemed man-lUce, and anon to change
To an amorphous cloud of marvellous size,
At times endowed with wings of glorious
range.
And this phantasmal variousness
Ever possessed it as they drew along:
Yet throughout all it symbolled none the less
Potency vast and loving-kindness strong.
Almost before I knew I bent
Towards the moving columns without a
word;
They, growing in bulk and numbers as they
went,
Struck out sick thoughts that could be over-
heard:
“O man-projected Figure, of late
Imaged as we, thy knell who shall survive?
Whence Game it we were tempted to create
One whom we can no longer keep alive?
“Framing him jealous, fierce, at first,
TF# gave him justice as the ages rolled,
Will to bless those by circumstance accurst,
And long-suffering, and mercies manifold.
“And tricked by our own early dream
And nfed of solace, we grew self-deceived,
Our making soon our maker did we deem,
And what we had imagined we believed.
“Till, in Time's stayless stealthy swing,
Uncompromising rude reality
Mangled the Monarch of our fashioning,
Who quavered, sank; and now has ceased to
be.
“So, toward our myth’s oblivion,
Darkling, and languid-lipped, we creep and
grope
Sadlier than those who wept in Babylon,
Whose Zion was a still abiding hope.
“How sweet it was in years far hied
To start the wheels of day with trustful
prayer,
To lie down liegely at the eventide
And feel a blest assurance he was there!
“And who or what shall fill his place?
Whither will wanderers turn distracted eyes
For some fixed star to stimulate their pace
Towards the goal of their enterprise?”....
Some in the background then I saw,
Sweet women, youths, men, all incredulous,
Who chimed as one: “This figure is of straw,
This requiem mockery! Still he lives to us!”
I could not prop their faith: and yet
Many I had known: with all I sympathised;
And though struck speechless, I did not for-
get
That what was mourned for, I, too, once
had prized.
Still, how to bear such loss I deemed
The insistent question for each animate mind,
And gazing, to my growing sight there seemed
A pale yet positive gleam low down behind,
Whereof, to lift the general night,
A certain feio who stood aloof had said,
“See you upon the horizon that small light —
Swelling somewhat?” Each mourner shook
his head.
And they composed a crowd of whom
Some were right good, and many nigh the
best ....
Thus dazed and puzzled 'tioixt the gleam and
gloom
Mechanically I followed with the rest.
LAMENT.
How she would have loved
A party to-day!—
Bright-hatted and gloved,
With table and tray
And chairs on the lawn
Her smiles would have shone
With welcomings... .But
She is shut, she is shut
From friendship's spell
In the jailing shell
Of her tiny cell.
Or she would have reigned
At a dinner to-night
With ardors unfeigned,
And a generous delight;
All in her abode
She'd have freely bestowed
On her guests... .But, alas,
She is shut under grass
Where no cups flow,
Powerless to know
That it might be so.
And she would have sought
With a child's eager glance
The slip snowdrops brought
By the new year's advance,
And peered in the rime
Of Candlemas-time
For crocuses... .chanced
It that she were not tranced
From sights she loved best;
Wholly possessed
By an infinite rest!
And we are here staying
Amid these stale things
Who care not for gaying,
And those junketings
That used so to joy her,
And never to cloy her
As us they c10y!... .But
She is shut, she is shut
From the cheer of them, dead
To all done and said
In a yew-arched bed.
EXEUNT OMNES.
Everybody else, then, going ,
And I still left where the fair was?....
Much have I seen of neighbor loungers
Making a lusty showing,
Each noio past all knowing.
There is an air of blankness
In the street and the littered spaces;
Thoroughfare, steeple, bridge and highway
Wizen themselves to lankness;
Kennels dribble dankness.
Folks all fade. And whither,
As I wait alone where the fair was?
Into the clammy and numbing night-fog
Whence they entered hither.
Soon do I follow thither!
A POET.
Attentive eyes, fantastic heed,
Assessing minds, he does not need,
.Nor urgent writs to sup or dine,
Nor pledges in the roseate wine.
For loud acclaim he does not care
By the august or rich or fair,
Nor for smart pilgrims from afar,
Curious on where his hauntings are.
But soon or later, tvhen you hear
That he has doffed his wrinkled gear,
Some evening, at the first star-ray,
Come to his graveside, pause and say:
“Whatever the message his to tell,
Two thoughtful ivomen loved him well."
Stand and say that amid the dim:
It will be praise enough for him.
—From “Satires of Circumstance” (Mac-
millan, per Sydney Boob Club).
©
Thomas Hardy has at least added a new
phrase to the English language. “Life’s
little ironies” is firmly fixed in our speech.
Satires of Circumstance, the title of his
latest book of verse, deserves a similar per-
manency. Hardy, the last of the Victorian
giants, survives, almost unwillingly. Yet
the last year has seen him married again,
and The Dynasts actually produced on the
London stage, an artistic and dramatic suc-
cess. And, having apparently nothing else
to do, he goes on writing verse—the one form
of literary labor for which he is least fitted.
The last infirmity of his mind is poetry,
jagged, crude, pessimistic poetry that is
nothing but closely and inconveniently packed
thought. Hardy’s beliefs have one by one
faded with the advance of age. He does not
believe any more in immortality—not even
in the immortality of God. Hardy is now chief
mourner at God’s funeral. And, even if
there were a God, Hardy has doubts whether
He would blow the last trump.
Ha, ha. It will be warmer when
I blow the trumpet (if indeed
I ever do; for you are men,
And rest eternal sorely need).
Hardy’s spirit will not haunt any grave
mound. Whether spirits haunt places
depends solely (and scientifically) on
whether you want to be haunted:—
And there you’ll find me, if a jot
You still should care
For me, and for my curious air;
If otherwise, then I shall not,
For you, be there.
You take your choice. Hardy leaves you
no other escape from his drab logic. Life
is full of little ironies, the inherent cussed-
ness of things, and circumstances are dis-
tinctly satirical.
As well cry over a neio-laid drain
As anything else, to ease your pain!
This reduction of all the beauty and all
the yearning of life to cemeteries and sani-
tary arrangments, this reduction to the
absurdity of a soulless logic, leads Hardy at
times almost to Bellerivean heights of stark-
ness and the heavy thud of bathos. Hearken
to this Bellerivean plaint, almost in the
mood of our great Australian master of the
drab:—
THE NEWCOMER’S WIFE.
He paused on the sill of a door ajar
That screened a lively liquor-bar,
For the name had reached him through the door
Of her he had married the week before.
“We called her the Hack of the Parade;
But she was discreet in the games she played;
If slightly worn, she’s pretty yet,
And gossips, after all, forget.
“And he knows nothing of her past;
I’m glad the girl’s in luck at last;
Such ones, though stale to native eyes,
Newcomers snatch at as a prize.
“Yes, being a stranger he sees her blent
Of all that’s fresh and innocent,
Nor dreams how many a love-campaign
She had enjoyed before his reign!’'
That night there was the splash of a fall
Over the slimy harbor-wall;
They searched, and at the deepest place
Found him with crabs upon his face.
Advisedly we say that that never in “Belle-
rive s most stagnant moments has he
achieved the bleak deadness of this. But it
is time Hardy stopped. If he writes any
more books of verse it will be impossible to
distinguish pupil from master, and “Belle-
rive will have a right to complain about
breaches of copyright. But how “Bellerive”'
would have gloried in those crabs!
The Mathematician’s Mind
The late Henri Poincarfi, .the French
mathematician, possessed, it is generally ad-
mitted, a remarkable scientific mind,
“marked by characteristics of swiftness, com-
prehensiveness, unexampled lucidity, and the
perception of recondite but fertile analogies.
His philosophical writings are not those of
a professional philosopher.” Science and
Method (Nelson; 65.) is a collection of his
essays that will appeal- especially to mathe-
maticians —indeed, there are chapters that
will appeal solely to that curious sub-species
of mankind; but there is much of general
interest to be gleaned from his delightful
pages, especially his analysis of the mind of
a mathematical genius such as Poincarg was.
Poincarg confesses that he was absolutely
incapable of doing an addition sum without
mistake; and he is convinced that he would
make a very bad chess-player. And this is
how he wrote his first treatise on “Fuchsian
functions”—whatever they are or aren’t.
“For a fortnight I had been attempting to
prove that there could not be any function
analogous to what I have since called Fuch-
sian functions. I was at that time very
ignorant. Every day I sat down at my table
and spent an hour or two trying a great
number of combinations, and I arrived at no
result. One night I took some black coffee,
contrary to my custom, and was unable to
sleep. A host of ideas kept surging in my
head; I could almost feel them jostling one
another, until two of them coalesced, so to
speak, to form a stable combination. When
morning came I had established the existence
of one class of Fuchsian functions. I had
only to verify the results, which only took a
few hours.” Further discoveries came sud-
denly, at inconvenient moments—not when
Poincare was deliberately and fruitlessly
seeking them, but spontaneously.
“One is struck by these appearances of
sudden illumination, obvious indications of a
long course of previous unconscious work.
The part played by this uncohscious work in
mathematical discovery seems to me indis-
putable. Often when a man is working at a
difficult question he accomplishes nothing the
first time he sets to work. Then he takes a
rest, and sits down again at his table. Dur-
ing the first half-hour he still finds nothing,
and then all at once the decisive idea pre-
sents _ itself to his mind.... The rest was
occupied with unconscious work.... the rev-
elation came during a period of conscious
work, but independently of that work, which
at most only performs the unlocking process.
.... These sudden inspirations are never pro-
duced except after some days of voluntary
efforts which appeared absolutely fruitless, in
which one thought one had accomplished
nothing. .. .These efforts, however, were not
so barren as one thought; they set the un-
conscious machine in motion, and without
them it would not have worked at all, and
would not have produced anything,.. .The
unconscious ego plays a most important part
m mathematical discovery The subliminal
ego is in no way inferior to the conscious
ego; it is not purely automatic; it is cap-
able of discernment; it has tact and lightness
of touch ; it can select and it can divine. In
a word, is not the subliminal ego superior to
the conscious ego?”
Poincarg suggests that the part played by
the preliminary conscious work is to set the
subliminal ego at work, to stir it up. Let us
add that the method for getting mathematical
inspiration is exactly the method used for
poetical inspiration. The ideas come as the
result of the poet brooding on his subject, as
the result of his attempting to write down
his ideas. The poet sets his unconscious
mind at work; _ and if he insists sternly
enough, steeps his mind in his subjecf long
enough, his subliminal ego will do the work.
And then all he has to do is to write it down
and polish it....and fatuously take credit
for it.
The Literary Agent
“Bil-1 Bowyang” has been stung:—
When I read the advts. and prospectus of the
American and English literary agent—and I have a
dozen different specimens before me as I write —I
am impressed equally by the omnipotence and benevo-
lence of the man. It is clear that he holds editors
and publishers in the hollow of his hand. He assures
me that manuscripts from unknown writers receive
little or no attention when submitted by their
authors, whereas they are promptly accepted
when submitted by him, because his judgment is
always relied upon! He tells me that he never
handles a manuscript unless he is practically sure
that he can place it; that all the leading editors
send to him for what they require, and that they
even accept from him the very manuscripts that
they have previously rejected when submitted by
the authors! He undertakes to guide authors and
to revise their work, though I have yet to learn
that his own name is known as a successful author
or writer. In some cases, he even announces that
he charges no fees; and I am wondering how he
manages to pay the rent of his office.
Now I want to say at once that there are agents
and agents. A few—they can be counted on the
fingers of one hand —are of the highest reputation,
and have among their clients many of the most suc-
cessful authors of the day. Such agents are of
genuine value to the literary worker, for they hon-
estly and efficiently promote his interests. They
not only relieve him of all business worries, but
they find increased openings for his work, and
obtain the best prices for it. It is not too much to
say that many well-known authors largely owe their
success to their literary agent. Albert Dorrington
once admitted that his literary agent put him on
the high road to success. But it is with the other
type of agent that I propose to deal.
When in London some 18 months back I noticed
the advt. of one of these gentry, which appeared
in several papers. He announced himself as a
“practical author,” whatever that may mean. As
a matter of fact I ascertained that he was the
author of one unsuccessful novel which he paid
the publisher to produce. In answer to an inquiry
he sent me a long letter, in which he boasted that
he placed MSS. with a long list of magazines men-
tioned. One of these was not in existence—but that
is a mere trifle. He further gave a long list of
publishers with whom he placed manuscripts. I
made inquiries of one of these, and learnt that he
had not even submitted a manuscript to them! His
fees were also interesting. For a novel he charged
a guinea for reading, two guineas for revision, and
one guinea for offering it to publishers—a total of
four guineas. He hastened to add that he charged
no commission in the sale of the work, and would
probably not concern himself about it. Another
agent, who has been established for many years,
charges a fee of five guineas before even looking at
the manuscript. Apparently it is a matter of
complete indifference to him whether it is good or
bad. In the latter ease, it is his method to wait
for some months, and then write to the author
saying that he has submitted it without success tc
a number of publishers, but that he has now found
one willing to take it if the author will contribute,
say, £5O towards the cost of the first edition. If
the author refuses, the manuscript is returned to
him. If, however, he swallows the bait, the agent
arranges with one of the smaller publishers to take
it on the terms mentioned, and he deducts a com-
mission of 10 per cent, when forwarding the author’s
cheque to the publisher.
One agent has struck out a line of his own. He
inserts advts. in various papers, somewhat to the
following effect: —
“TO AUTHORS —Publisher requires MSS. of all
kinds. Highest prices given. No fees. Address,
‘Publisher,’ care of, etc.”
I have made inquiries about this chap. When
the author writes, he receives a reply from a small
flat in the London suburbs, announcing that Mr.
, literary agent and publisher, will be happy
to arrange for the publication of his manuscript.
There will be no fee whatever, but a deposit must
be paid to cover out-of-pocket expenses, and the
manuscript will be revised if necessary. Strange
to say, it nearly always is necessary to revise the
MSS. that reaches this practitioner; and this is
where he comes in. He charges a pretty stiff price
for work which he has no qualifications for under-
taking. The next step is to let the manuscript rest
for a time—possibly to recover from the revision—
and then the proceedings follow those we have
described above. When I was in England a lady of
my acquaintance sent a story to this agent and
paid the “deposit.” Very shortly afterwards she
asked for its return. A letter came from the agent,
saying that he had already offered it to some ten pub-
lishers, who names were given. This was an obvious
lie on the face of it, for there had not been sufficient
time for ten publishers to consider the manuscript.
My lady friend was therefore not surprised, when she
made inquiry of one of the firms mentioned, to learn
that they had not even heard of the manuscript.
Another agent in London contrives to combine
the functions of both publisher and literary agent.
Manuscripts that reach his hands are rarely offered
elsewhere, but, after picking up any odd guineas
that may be available for reading and revising, he
offers to publish the work himself if the author
will pay half the cost of the first edition. Needless
to say the amount suggested as “half” covers the
entire cost of production and leaves him a handsome
margin of profit as well.
I could relate many more curious facts, but the
above will be sufficient to warn the Australian author
who intends to send his work abroad, to avoid all
literary agents who boast of their successes; those
who profess to charge no fees; and, above all,
those who do not rely mainly for their remuneration
upon a commission on the proceeds of the sale of
the work.
A. Safroni-Middleton, author of Bush
Songs and Oversea Voices (Long, 3s. 6d.),
judging from'certain press-critiques in Eng-
lish and U.S. publications, has a reputation.
For instance, he is “a really original Aus-
tralian poet,” and “promises to occupy a
place in the foremost rank of the few rising
British Colonial poets.” We learn, also,
that he was once a violinist at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney, and that for a time he
“became a boundary rider.” Possibly he was
a good violinist and an excellent boundary
rider; but he is certainly a poor poet. His
work is careless, uninspired, and full of the
out-of-date melodrama in verse that doubt-
less still appeals to overseas critics as the
real “colonial” thing. Thus he presents the
real Australia: —
Out on the wide domain we slept, as independents
do!
Beneath a big-leafed tree, all in a row!
Penniless seafaring men, the tigers in the Zoo
At midnight roared, oft woke the sleeping show.
We’d hear the ferry siren’s hoot, out o’er the silent
bay.
My pillow was an old cheese-cutter hat.
The baronets and M.B.A.’S in rags beside us lay,
With hungry, dreaming eyes in moonlight sat!
Good enough, perhaps, for England—Robert
Bridges has set on record his appreciation of
an earlier volume —but modestly we demand
something a little better, even a little more
grammatical, for inclusion in our Aus-
tralian fellowship.
“L.D.”: May I speak on the side of the
“heretics” in opposition to “Montalex”? No
good English poet, not even The Bulletin,
insists on a rime to the eye as well as to the
ear. I find that The Bulletin accepts with-
out comment such sets as “tried —cried —
wide,” “warn —morn,” “years—beers.” I hold
that these rimes are similar to the much-
talked-of “morn —dawn” pair ; if anything,
the ear is less offended by the last than by the
others. The ordinary educated Englishman
who is not conscious of his pronunciation
makes no difference between “awn” and
“orn.” If questioned, he would say that he
pronounced the “r” in “morn,” “farther,” etc.,
but he has long ceased to do so except in
theory. English has gradually dropped the
more difficult consonant sounds, such as the
“w” in “wrong,” the “gh” in “night,” and
now the “r” in “farther.” Poetry may resist
such changes and call them cockneyisms; but
it will soon be forced to recognise the last as
well as the two first.

RING ALBERT’S BOOK
A Tribute to the Belgian King and People from
Representative Men and Women throughout
the World. Entire Proceeds from Sale of the
Book go to the Belgian Relief Fund.
KING ALBERT’S BOOK contains perhaps more illustrious names than have
ever before been inscribed within the covers of a single volume. Statesmen
(beginning with the Prime Minister and the President of the French Republic),
eminent ecclesiastics, soldiers, sailors, scholars, preachers, teachers, authors, artists
and composers from nearly all the great countries of the world —Great Britain
and its wide Empire—have contributed to its pages, each giving of his own kind —
the statesman an inspiring message, the soldier and sailor a thrilling word of
cheer, the preacher a word of comfort, the scholar a word of wisdom, the poet, the
artist and the composer an uplifting and kindling song story, picture or piece of
music.
Rarely in the long history of hooks can it have occurred that some of the
highest representatives of so many great races have drawn so close together in a
common cause. Rarely has literature presented a more significant sign of world
brotherhood than KING ALBERT'S BOOK provides.
Merely as an autograph album KING ALBERT’S BOOK is, perhaps, one of
the most interesting volumes ever issued from the press, for every contribution
bears its author’s name at least in facsimile; but the value of its substance
transcends even the distinction of its form. Great events call forth great talents,
and many of the contributions to KING ALBERT’S BOOK are of an excellence
that has hardly ever before been attained by their illustrious authors.
KING ALBERT’S BOOK is a quarto volume of ISO pages, printed in the
best style, containing a signed Photogravure portrait of King Albert, many plates
in color as well as in black and white, and bound in substantial cream white
cloth.

DOCTOR’S ALARMING STATEMENTS.
Protect Yourself Against Ordinary
Dangers by using
NEV-A-BITE
“There is no question,” says a leading
specialist, “tjiat insect bites may lead to great
danger to health, and have been known to end
fatally.”
This alarming
statement, which
has appeared in
the influential
newspapers of this
country, should
arouse the public
to the fact that
bites from mos-
quitoes and other
insects are things
to worry about.
It is quite pos-
sible that if one’s
blood is in a poor
state an insect
bite might set up
such serious in-
flammation as to
cause blood-poi-
soning, and —-as everyone well knows —blood-
poisoning may lead to even the amputation of
a limb.
One has only to glance at the illustration
given here of the mosquito to realise what
terrifying poison-injecting parts this insect
has. It is absolutely necessary, not only for
the woman who desires to keep her complexion
in its natural condition, but also for the pro-
tection against more serious dangers, to secure
some reliable preparation which will prevent
insects from biting.
The best form of treatment of mosquito bites
is prophylactic, or preventive. The application
of a little of the Nev-a-bite lotion to the
forehead, neck and the instep (when openwork
stockings are worn) reduces the probability of
mosquito-bites to a. minimum.
Nev-a-bite is a perfectly harmless pre-
paration, and serves to whiten, soften and
beautify the skin.
Nev-a-bite can be obtained at 9<J. per
bottle. Postage 3d. extra from—
DUERDIN & SAINSBURY
Manufacturin’ Chemists. MELBOURNE.

whose bite is perhaps
the most dangerous of
all insects.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Saints and Sinners.
I love a sinner who loves his bath,
I doubt a saint if his cuffs be soiled;
No man need fear for the aftermath
Whose heart is sound and his linen boiled.
Though sinners be sinners and saints be
saints,
Some sinners are truthful, some saints
deceive,
And none is so black as his neighbor paints
And none so white as he makes-believe!
I love a sinner who owns to sin—
The gods themselves were a sinful race—
I doubt a saint when his blood runs thin;
A warm heart goes with a fearless face.
I dread the gaze of the faultless man
Who pricks my sins with a virtuous sword ;
Sin won sweet guerdon since sin began,
But virtue, I’m told, is its own reward.
I’ve loved true women and loved true men.
And loved them more for their splendid
faults;
If one died sinning we grieved—and then
We buried his sins in the churchyard vaults.
If all the world on its knees confessed
I’ll warrant the sinner were loved the most!
And who shall say whom the gods love best?
What man is saintly enough to boast?
The sinner’s creed is a kindly creed —
None is so black as the saintly paints;
The world were a dreary old place indeed
Were all men faultless and women saints!
N.S.W.
W. M. W.
The Black Cat’s Serenade.
Come away,
Tiger-lady
Wild and gay !
The night is shady.
Quick, when all the world is sleeping,
Over the hill the moon is creeping.
On your fence
Here I climb,
Untuneful rhyme,
Making tense
Like a minstrel masquerading—
Wo'n’t you hear my serenading?
Have you stirred
Unespied?
Yelvet-furred,
Onyx-eyed!
Claws of ivory, silken tresses.
Ears are rent for your caresses.
The watch-dog throttles ;
Children squall,
On the wall
There’s broken bottles.
Home-life’s fraught with apprehension,
In the bush we scout convention.
Milk is sweet,
Fine is fish ;
But what’s cooked meat
In a dish!
Bush cats tire of such tame feeding.
We’ll have morsels warm and bleeding.
Through the waste
Rabbits scud.
Soon we’ll taste
Living blood.
We’ll go a-hunting, px-owling, snaring—
Look! through the trees the moon is
staring.
Now the moon
Is shining o’er us,
Tiger, soon
The world’s before us.
Jungle beasts, we’ll snarl at sorrow,
Christian cats we’ll be to-morrow.
victoria.
LOUIS ESSON.
Not All the Dead are Buried.
Not all the dead are buried! I can see
Dead men all round me —sad and sorry
husks
Who breathe from force of habit wearily
And on good food ply artificial tusks.
In most of us there’s something dead, but
these
Are wholly dead, and should be put away ;
A Stone Age man the job would surely
please;
But they must linger on until their Day.
Some have themselves destroyed. Some Cir-
cumstance
Has slowly killed in his remorseless style;
For them life holds no roses or romance—
Joy wins from them at most a twisted smile.
The world belongs to Youth, and Youth
alone #
Should own the world —that is, the"surface
part.
Let each dead man retire beneath his stone
And hide away his dead and dusty heart.
Grey-bearded youths skip round with relics
that
In years are young and still appear to thrive;
But just because a body wears a hat
And walks, it is no proof that it’s alive.
Not all the dead are buried! When I see
Them walking round and putting up a bluff
That they’re alive, it fairly maddens me;
I hate such futile false pretence and guff.
Old custom binds. The dead and standing
weed
Must not be mowed, however great our
need.
How I would like to swing Death’s hissing
blade
Amongst these jobs! It would be good for
trade!
n.s.w.

THURSDAY, MARCH 11, 1915.
ON THE DISADVANTAGE OF BEING A
CENTENARIAN.
Every now and again some romantic maiden-
aunt, rummaging among the family archives,
comes upon her great-grandfather’s birth
certificate ; and, assured that she has found
the title deeds to life of her still garrulous
grandparent, supplies the correspondent of
the local newspaper with the information
that the old man is in his 107th year. The
old person concerned, who is really 81, feels
his ancient limbs totter under him ; and con-
vinced by his rheumatics that he ought to
be 107, identifies himself with his own father
and wears the blushing honors of his mythi-
cal longevitv before the admiring neighbors.
Then-the local newspaper stars the item and
prints a column article on John Smith,
the father of the district, incidentally setting
forth the health-promoting qualities of a
locality in which old men simply cannot
die from natural causes, but must, in
desperation, talk themselves into a belated
grave. So the crop of centenarians con-
tinues to ripen, and the four-score limit set
by Holy Writ is scoffed at by all but the
insurance companies. Curiously enough, no-
body who insures his life ever lives to be a
hundred.
In spite of the infirmities of the flesh and
the fact that Nature seldom provides a
third set of teeth, extreme longevity might
have some advantages. The man of science
wjho set out on some stupendous astronom-
ical calculation at the age of 30 might have
a chance of working out his sum if he had
70 good years in which to chase his eternal
logarithms. The man who determined to
save a million pounds out of his wages as a
grocer’s assistant (as the writers of.self-
help books who still have a tram-fare at
GO suggest he should) would be getting well
ou towards the realisation of a 500th
part of his ambition before the curtain was
rung down. With the assurance of a good
half-century in front of him, even a middle-
aged man might sit uncomplainingly in the
corner of an all-stop suburban train and
read the daily paper's opinion of the Labor
Party. Besides,. Dy living a full" ten decades
a man could attend in turn the funerals of
practically all the people who really ought
to be dead; and any legacies, peerages or
other matters in which he had a reversionary
interest would be certain to fall to him be-
fore the finish. As against these obvious
advantages, however, there are a number
of items in the debit column. Chief among
them is the disappointment which the future
alwavs holds for those who imagine that
human progress has its wheels oiled and
goes forward on a straight road. The man
born when the dying roar of the guns of
Waterloo seemed to proclaim the settled
peace of outworn Europe, would be feeling
sick and sorry indeed as his century of life
ebbed to the wail of ruined Belgium. The
man who had felt in his earlier days the
throb of the great Chartist movement, and
had seen afar the millennium for labor
which was to come with the equal franchise,
would wonder sadly as he gazed upon the
Holman Government and its works. Then
again, there is the point made in the old
Greek fable of Tithonus, for whom the
lovely Eos obtained the gift of going on
living without stipulating that he should
never feel more than 20. Tithonus had a
rough time of it after his last tooth dropped
from his immortal gums and fell clattering
to the marble floor; and when old age got
in its finest work on him, he was far too
busy trying to think out some way in which
he could achieve a coffin to bother about
politics or pugilism or poetry or any of the
other things that amuse the regular, dying
population.
These melancholy reflections are provoked
by a recent speech made by Andrew
Fisher, at Kyneton, Victoria. The Prime
Minister stated, without changing color or
showing any other sign of shame at his own
inefficiency:—
Ho had no hope of seeing a uniform railway gauge
within the next 10 or 15 years. A- strategic line
for military purposes could, however, be cheaply
built from the back of Adelaide to Brisbane. It
was for the people of Australia to say whether they
were prepared to make the sacrifice for the sake of
setting their house in order.
The bringing of the railway gauges of the
Commonwealth to one uniform standard has
been recognised for a very long time as
one of Australia’s greatest needs. Again
and again the matter has been reported on,
referred to committees of politicians, re-
ferred to State Premiers in conference, re-
ferred to commissions of experts for further
report, and faithfully promised by all sorts
of people in authority as a matter of
urgency. And now the Prime Minister
calmly assures us that we shall have another
10 or 15 years to wait before the thing can
have any hope of accomplishment. The
assurance is lamentable. Even if we were
content to wait the full term, what guaran-
tee have we' that another Prime Minister
will not then arise and tell us to keep
hoping? In 15 years time the Federation
will be nearly 30 years old, and the stand-
ardisation of the railway gauges was one of
the things which was regarded as a natural
corollary of Australian unity. Indeed,
while the broken gauges remain to remind
us of the old clumsy “colonial” methods and
absurd “colonial” boundary lines, the Federa-
tion is not complete. Would Andrew
Fisher have us believe that his grip is so
weak and his ability so small that he cannot
contrive to bring the State Governments
into line and have this comparatively simple
work carried out? In other countries, private
railway corporations have combined for
purely commercial reasons and carried out
the standardisation of their gauges in in-
credibly short time without any great fuss
or commotion. To secure railway uniformity
is one of the first serious steps towards an
effective system of defence and one of the
easiest; yet Andrew Fisher stands off and
looks at the proposal as one so stupendous
that it must be reported on and referred
back to commissions and committees at least
a dozen times more before a plate-layer can
possibly be set to work. Yet, almost in the
same breath as that in which he tells us of
his helplessness afid despair, he suggests
the construction of what he is pleased to call
a “strategic railway” from the back of
Adelaide to Brisbane! While the line to
connect Oodnadatta and Pine Creek is in
doubt and the simnle readjustment of the
existing railway gauges is still 15 years off,
in the name of all decency why suggest
making another railway? What go<->d pur-
pose could such a railway serve? Why not
make a railway from Broken Hill to Alice
Springs or run a chean line from Newcastle
to Westernport? not do any old thing
that doesn’t want doing as an excuse for
shirking those things that are necessary and
urgent? The present hour when State poli-
ticians are bursting with alleged patriotism
surely offers the best of all onnortunities for
coming to a final understanding about the
railway gauges. The State person who, when
half the world is on fire, would throw diffi-
culties in the way of Australian safety, re-
veals himself as a fool or a traitor to the
best interests of his country. Knowing this,
if he had any grit at all Andrew Fisher
would put on the screw now and secure a
system of uniform gauges, not in 15 years,
but in 15 active and purposeful months. ' A
statesman would, see and seize the oppor-
tunity. But Andrew Fisher is not a states-
man. He is not even a large politician. The
public believes him to be honest. But
something more than honesty is required of
the man who has the job of running Aus-
tralia. It was probably honesty on his
part—honesty of his peculiar brand—that
made him .practically admit that there was
no hope of dealing with the railway gauges
until he was superseded by a better man;
but it was the sort of honesty which makes
moderately hopeful people tired and robs us
of all regret that the span of life is com-
paratively short. While gangs are swelter-
ing to make that strategic railway to carry
war-goats from Port Augusta to Brisbane
the invader may land somewhere with an
axe, and make us regret that the span is
even as long ht it is.
* * * *
Yet witli anything like a fair deal Aus-
tralia should offer the centenarian more
than any other country. The possibilities
of this white man’s continent, complete
within iitself sand rich with a wealth that is
practically limitless, are such as to fire our
dreams. The man who is 50 to-day and who
could secure a tenancy of the house of life
for another half-century ought to see such
progress as would keep him young with
ever-growing expectation. He should see
population flowing north and west along the
lines «f great permanent railways, built at
once for the purposes of defence and the
strategy of trade. He should see new ports
thronged with the ships of all the seas ; vast
areas under cultivation yielding an ever-
increasing harvest; busy factories, clean
and sanitary, where well-paid workers
turned out all the wares that an advancing
civilisation demanded; cities wholesome and
sweet and gay as the sun and wind that
chased the pestilence from them; a strong
fleet at sea keeping touch with a strong
fleet in the air; a citizen army composed of
all the nation’s manhood, orderly, disci-
plined, resolute for peace. AH these things
he should see; but before he can see them
he must discover a leader whose imagination
will soar high enough to enable him to think
of putting two steel rails a few inches closer
together, or a few inches wider apart, in less
than 15 years. With such a man found, the
disadvantages of being a centenarian will
be greatly reduced.
TRADE AND THE DEBT AND THE
EVERLASTING LEAK.
Though vague noises about “collaring
German trade” are audible throughout the
land, something more than a suspicion exists
that the phrase has a meaning very different
from what it pretends to imply. The aver-
age citizen naturally enough assumes the
idea behind it all to be that Australia should
so direct her industrial affairs as to conserve
to herself the trade hitherto carried on
with an enemy. He imagines that the coun-
try, having learned through war the inex-
pressible folly of its casual methods in the
past, is determined to end the practice of
dragging nearly everything it produces and
the bulk of what it consumes across the
sea in ships; that the reason for this reso-
lution is not that the German is a Hun
and a disturber of the peace and a pirate in
a spiked helmet, but that the whole business
of foreign trade, as this country knows it,
has been shown to be economically unsound
—a source of loss, waste and embarrassment.
But the citizen who assumes this is assum-
ing too much; for behind all the clamor of
the moment can easily be detected the
characteristic note of the importer, the in-
dent agent and the drummer. What these
people want is not the conservation of trade
for the benefit of Australian workmen and
manufacturers, for the employment of Aus-
tralian capital and skill, but to collar on
behalf of other foreigners and oversea phil-
anthropists the commerce hitherto cornered
by the enterprising Teuton, and now lying
round in ruins for someone to seize upon.
Uncle Sam is after this all over the world;
John Bull is considering the matter from
the point of view of the world’s carrier and
an imposing creditor. The obvious duty of
Australians is to get busy on their own
territory. If they merely transfer to Chicago
the business which fell down dead at Ham-
burg, what will they have succeeded in
collaring? Yet it is just this futility
that the disguised Freetraders behind the
ambuscade of patriotic meetings want Aus-
tralians to perpetrate.
* -v* * •
Not only is the campaign for extension of
foreign trade in new directions being in-
dustriously prosecuted under the pretence
of a great friendliness for Australian indus-
try, but veiled arguments against the theory
of internal development are beginning to
find furtive expression. These are clothed
in varied raiment. For instance, while it is
difficult to unearth any direct denial of the
expediency of developing Australian manu-
factures —indeed, the attitude of the foreign
trader for the present towards this project
is one of detached, inactive benevolence —
easy discovery can be made of piebald sug-
gestions calculated to foster the idea that an
expansion of manufacturing at this stage
of proceedings would be an act of disloyalty
to our friends and relations —that when the
war is over Australia should take pride in
sending copious orders in certain directions
and laboriously flinging into steamers, for
transport to England and elsewhere, piles
of all the raw material it is possible to
gather together. In the Freetrade dailies pon-
derous sermons having neither beginning nor
end, but based upon a veiled text of this
character, may be read. An argument en-
countered the other day was a double-
barrelled one to the effect that if Australia
reduced her imports so as to have a balance
on exports, the credit so obtained would be
useless, as the money could not be spent;
further, a reduction of imports would cause
a fall in exports, since each was the comple-
ment of the other. This is probably meant
to be a Freetrade poser for Protectionists,
the point being that a country selling a
million pounds’ worth of material to an
over-sea customer and buying back only half
a million pounds’ worth must find itself with
a credit of half a million which cannot be
employed. Applied to Australia the theory
is as moth-eaten as the cerements of
Pharaoh. This country is heavily in debt
and has each year to pay by exports an
enormous interest bill. Now. the certain
effect of reducing imports would be to'cause
a transfer of this debt from the foreign bond-
holder to ourselves. In other words, the
accumulating margin in our own favor would
be employed by the holders of the credit to
buv back our debt, with two inevitable'and
■welcome results —(1) the interest, instead of
being exnended abroad, would be paid within
the country itself, and (2) each year an in-
creasing sum would be available for employ-
ment in internal development and for the
extension of local industries. That has
been the experience of Germany and the
United States. A reference to the move-
ments of Australia’s debt shows that during
the succession of fat years in which our ex-
ports exceeded imports a steady fall took
place in the volume of our debt to London ;
but when imports began to snow the
exports under, the debt went back to
Cohen. The country was in the latter
case realising its securities to meet the.cost
of the piles of imported merchandise our ex-
ports were inadequate to pay for. Great
Britain built up her financial supremacy
by selling more than she purchased, using
the capital sum thus realised for extending
her manufactures, reducing her debt, paying
for the national obligations with cash instead
of loans, and lending to other countries.
No reason exists why Australia should—not
do the same. There are a thousand reasons
why she should.
* * * *
The foreign trader, with bis eye always
on the beautiful spectacle of men tearing
boxes out of a ship and filling the conse-
quential hole up with things dug out of the
earth or with piles of dead animals, refuses
with a dogged, unreasoning persistency to
admit tha*t Australia can do what other
countries have done. He declines to regard
trade as anything but barter, and in his in-
most soul believes that the commerce of the
nation should foi* ever remain like that of
some bf those islands where the natives hand
over the local nuts and sucking pigs in re-
turn for brilliant-colored trousers and jew’s
harps. The notiop that these people might
use the nut and pig on the spot and make
their own pants and musical instruments
seems to him wholly absurd. But to the
reckless nigger there must assuredly come
reflection and repentance, when, after having
borrowed from the large-hearted trader the
material for laying down railways and roads
to bring the nut and pig to the seaboard, and
making racecourses and building a bridge
which has been washed away, he finds him-
self with a bill for interest, his pants worn
out, his jew’s harp in ruins, and only suffi-
cient surplus vegetation and pork to get rid
of the bailiff for another half-year. The
plain, commonsense plan for that afflicted
son of Ham to follow is to set honestly to
work to produce as much nut and pig as
his lands will yield for the sustenance of
people who desire these things and for him-
self ; to forswear the imported pants of
the foreigner, and make his own clothes, and
be satisfied with the musical instrument
turned -out • b.V'liis own hands. Then he will
begin, to see solvency ahead of him; to have
the wherewithal to redeem the faded p.n. of
painful* memory; to be able to. pay for his
railway and his bridge—and in course of
time have the cash to lend to some other
benighted ; ass who is squandering his patri-
mony, in the bad old way.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The First Lesson of the War.
Unless civilisation is prepared to strain its
resources to - the uttermost, Belgium as a
nation goes out, not because her big enemy
will not in the end be defeated, but because
the people of the small country will be all
dead. This means in plain language that
while Germany violated her trust, Britain
was untrue to hers. Having guaranteed the
integrity of the small nation, she should have
been ready to support that guarantee. Had
she established 'compulsory military service
even as late- as when she discovered with cer-
tainty ‘what the designs and intentions of
Germany* actually were, there can be very
little doubt that the Belgians would still
have bread ' to eat and roofs to cover them.
It is very probable that the Germans would
never haye invaded Belgium at all. It was Bri-
tain’s unreadiness and nothing else that gave
the German his opportunity; and but for
Belgium’s own pluck and resource there is
no telling how far he might have succeeded.
It is very little use saying that the war will
begin in May, and that Belgium will then
be avenged. The dead cannot be avenged.
The hideous carnage may go on until there
is as little left of Germany as there is to-
day of Belgium ; but still the horrible fact
will remain that Belgium has been ravaged
and ravished because it trusted Britain and
Britain was not ready. In matters of war
and defence let us learn the first lesson: the
thing that should have been done the year
before last can never be done the year after
next.
Andrew Fisher and the Democracy He
Doesn’t Serve.
When he was in Maoriland recently
Andrew Fisher said that “the democratic
platform must come from the people—not
from the leaders.” If the Prime Minister
really believes this, why not make some
attempt to act upon it? There is only one
way in which a legislative platform can
come from the people, and that is by the
Referendum with Initiative. The Refer-
endum with Initiative is devised to enable
any large section of the public to have a
vote taken upon any proposal by the general
body of electors ; ■ and when once a proposal
has been* carried, it is mandatory upon the
Government and>. Parliament to effectuate it
by legislation. 4 , The introduction of this
machine is one *of the steps towards the
abolition of party government, and the prin-
ciple of it has been endorsed by the Labor
Conference and placed upon its platform.
Now Andrew Fisher says that the people
must* have* the, framing of legislation ; but,
though he has been v in office on and off for
11 years, he has never made any attempt to
place the power in the hands of the demo-
cracy. Why? The simple answer appears
to be that" the Prime Minister does not
really believe in democracy, and that his
words spoken in Maoriland were only the
usual clap-trap with which Laborites are
kept jigging. This further illustrates the
remarkable fact that, while the party com-
pels the obedience of its rank and file, it
does not insist to any extent upon its leaders
doing the work they are appointed to do.
Labor leagues are declared bogus when they
take a line of their own, but the Prime
Minister is not declared bogus when he
neglects the direct orders of the organisation
he professes to serve. The Referendum with
Initiative would formulate a platform for
all Australia exactly as the Labor Confer-
ences do at present, but with a difference.
When the Labor Conference commands, men
like Andrew Fisher can shirk and shuffle
and get out of doing the things they are told
to do; when the Referendum issued the order,
prompt obedience to that order would be
compulsory. That is why the Prime Min-
ister and' his colleagues persistently forget
that the proposal is on the Labor platform.
They don’t want to obey the democracy.
Choking Justice with a Tangle of Tape.
A really shocking illustration of the law s
delavs and ofpthe manner in which a point
of procedure, elementarily simple, can be
smothered a _zareba, of seals, parcn-
ments and hocus-pocus, has been afforded
the world at large and Australia particularly
in the case of the steamer Birkenfels, Th
vessel, a German, sailed from New York-
prior to the outbreak of war carrying cargo


for Australia. Coal aboard running short
on the voyage, the Birkenfels put into Cape-
town for supplies. War had then been
declared, and the steamer was promptly
seized. There was not a pennyworth, of
material aboard for anyone in South Africa;
so the obviously common-sense course was
to send the vessel on in continuance of her
voyage, under whatever surveillance or
control might have been considered
expedient. Afterwards, the cargo having
been delivered to consignees in the ordinary
way, the vessel would be available for dis-
cussion by the Admiralty alone or in con-
junction with the Commonwealth and
Union Governments. But, instead of send-
ing the vessel on, the official known as the
Admiralty Proctor at the Cape insisted that
all Australian owners must prove their
claims before the Courts there, acting
through attorneys. Costly cable communi-
cations were made with London and Aus-
tralia; and after Capetown had spent three
months writing and reading correspondence,
waiting for more news, and generally behav-
ing with expeditious leisure, the Admiralty
got the hang of things and ordered that the
Birkenfels was to go on. But the .Proctor,
representing the Red Tape of the situation,
was not done with yet: he filed notice in
the Courts of intention to apply to have
condemned all the cargo on which claims
had not been made. More delay ensued,
and after a month the Courts approached
the weary old problem again, and Wigs
paraded in great force with their attendant
attorneys, and Bills of Costs expanded like
Zeppelins preparing for flight. After pro-
longed argument the Court came to the con-
clusion that it had no power to do what the
Admiralty wanted — i.e., to send the ship and
cargo on to Australia- —but had authority
to do what the Admiralty Proctor asked,
viz., to condemu certain cargo. Then the
Court adjourned, leaving everyone concerned,
except perhaps the Proctor, with a mournful
feeling that things were so mixed that it
would be better to drop the whole business
and scuttle the ship in the dead of night.
What has happened since is only a matter
for speculation ; but possibly the Admiralty
finally got to grips with the Proctor, or
called him off, or rolled him up in a man-
damus. Anyhow, the latest information is
that the Birkenfels is coming on with her
eight-months-old cargo and a blister for
costs big enough to be mistaken for a hay-
stack. When this kind of thing is possible
it looks as if the Admiralty would be all the
better were somebody to stir the Prize
department with some very pointed remarks.
The spectacle of cargo from a neutral port,
consigned to part of the Empire, being held
up at another part with the persistence
shown by a second-hand merchant affecting
the seizure of a washerwoman’s time-pay-
ment mangle, would be wildly humorous were
it not the cause of great loss and
embarrassment.
The Hero: A Daily Tragedy.
[For The Bulletin.]
Scene: The drawing-room at Kitchener-
ville, home of Henry Smythe, President of
the Shell Cove Empire League. Afternoon
tea is just over. Mrs. Smythe gazes across
the harbor in a stricken manner, and fur-
tively mops her eyes. Miss Alma Smythe
weeps near the piano. Mr. Smythe, with
lips compressed, and an air at once sad and
proud, lights a cigar.
Mrs. Smythe : And you think, Henky,
there is no hope—he has really done it?
Mr. Smythe : I can only tell you, my dear,
what Blogson told me on the tram. It was
common talk in the warehouse after lunch, I
believe. A portion of the staff rendered the
National Anthem when the news came
through... .They agreed that it was just like
him.
Mrs. S.: Ah, Henby, they knew him bet-
ter than we did. And yet—why not? My
father was in the Volunteers for a time, and
uncle Herbert really got his first start, as
you may say, as an army contractor. In a
sense, we have always been a military
family.
.Miss Smythe (indignantly tearful) :
Mother, I’m surprised at you! Absolutely
encouraging it. As if there weren’t thou-
sands of working-men whom nobody would
miss, without taking what the dear Bishop
called only last Sunday “the flower of the
nation.” Oh! it is croo-ooel. (Breaks
down.)
Mr. S. (with a pathetic attempt at cheer-
fulness) : Come, come, girl! Bear up ! Bear
up ! No tears when he comes back ! That
is not the welcome that the boys who are
saving the Empire exnect.
Mrs. S. (infected by her daughter's grief):
It’s all very well for you to talk, Henry.
You’re a man. But for us women —Oh! it’s
awful. And him an only son, too. (Weeps
tempestuously against a life-size statue of a
negro in Jamaican planting costume.)
The sun sinks behind the hills. Mr.
Smythe continues to smoke. The ladies
sniffle. A maid enters to clear away the tea-
things. She picks up a cup and saucer,
stands for a moment with distorted features,
drops the crockery on the floor, bursts into
loud, hiccoughing sobs, and exit hastily.
Mrs. S. (miserably): She has a good
heart, Henry.
Mr. S.: She understands! Would you
iexpect other than sympathy? Noblesse may
not oblige in her station in life, but these
people understand in their poor way the
case of others. (He smokes with more
furious pride than ever. A dog howls
ominously outside.)
Mrs. S.: Fido !
Miss S. (lachrymosely): Poor little Fido.
Mrs. S.: I wonder if he, too, feels.. .. ?
Mr. S. (looking out of window): Those in-
fernal boys next door have been throwing
stones at him again. If I get hold of the
young hounds
A ring is heard. The inmates of the draw-
ing-room start, and gather together.
Mrs. S.: It is him.
Mr. S. (gently): He, my dear.
Miss S.: He’s forgotten his key again.
A shuffling noise is heard without, indicat-
ing that the crockery-smashing maid is ap-
proaching once more.
Miss S.: It’s Muriel going to open the
door.
Mrs. S. (suddenly inspired): Hold! (To
Muriel, who appears in a dishevelled con-
dition) : Stop ! I, his mother, will open the
door for him. It is a duty to him and to his
country. Exit.
A hand- outside strikes up “Sons of the
Sea." Mr. Smythe stands for an instant to
the salute. His daughter watches him with
shining eyes. Simultaneously enter Mrs.
Smythe and her son Cyril. Cyril is a
well-set-up youth of 25. He is a second-
grade cricketer, and a budding politician,
with strong views concerning the Labor
party's disloyalty to the Empire.
Mr. S. (advancing and clasping the boy's
large and capable right hand) : Well, mother,
is the news true?
Miss S. (wildly) : Speak, mother, speak!
Mrs. S. (with the cold fortitude of the
Roman matron) : Let Cyril tell his own
story.
Cyril: Father, mother, Alma, it is true.
This morning at 9.30 I joined the Shell Cove
Rifle Club —three drills per week and find
your own uniform.
Mr. S.: f My brave son!
Alma : ■! Cyril !
Mrs. S.: t. My gallant boy !
The maid, Muriel, who has been standing
tip stage with the tea-things, drops the entire
tray and exit hastily, sobbing. Mr. S. seizes
his son's right hand; Mrs. S. casts herself on
his right shoulder; Miss S. hurls herself on
his left shoulder.
Curtain.
J. B. D.
Socialism and Soft Jobs.
“10-2-4’s” discourse :
Socialism is a great institution —when it is run
in the right spirit. The Socialism of Germany, for
example, has done more to make that nation the
formidable machine it is than all Essen. Australia
suffers from Socialism run on individualistic lines;
just as a bank would suffer if it were placed under
the direction of an I.W.W. enthusiast. The chief
trouble is that States and municipalities alike regard
the public purse as a substitute for the almshouse.
The Bondi (Sydney) Surfing Sheds, which are in
the hands of the. Waverley Council, exemplify the way
a municipality conducts its Socialism. On Saturday
afternoons and Sundays the fringe of the ocean re-
sembles a disturbed ant-heap. Surfing sheds are
not expensive to run. They don’t consume corn or
petrol. The wear and tear is inconsiderable. They
don’t' take much cleaning, and the repairs bill is
small. Hence there should be big money in them.
Yet they are not sensationally profitable. One reason
is that they are run on the almshouse principle. I
used them on a recent week-day. In and about the
receipt of cash were a number of well-set-up youths,
of fighting age, whose absurd collective job it was
to take tuppences and hand oyer towels. On this
day, as on the countless others when I have used
the sheds, an aged man could have done all the work.
As a matter of fact, at the Private Enterprise baths
on the south side, a white-whiskered old concessionaire
performs the feat, and apparently prospers on it. But
municipal councils don’t do things on those lines, and
so long as the public remains apathetic they never
will.
A principle which taxpayers should insist on in
Socialistic enterprises is that no able-bodied men
under middle age should be employed on dead-end
jobs. It is bad national economy to have youths
who are sound in wind and limb opening gates or
handing out bathing costumes or issuing railway
tickets for a living. Each person so occupied is
one more producer lost to the country, and one
more obstacle in the way of the elderly man, the
injured ex-soldier, and others whose job-range is
small. Socialism would do a lot to justify itself
if its enterprises paid its owners better and more
frequently than it does. It would do still more if
it figured, where possible, as a refuge for those
whom it is the State’s duty to support.
Blither.
From a recent issue of Sydney Worker :
When this war is over another shall begin. It
will be a war, not for the dominance of a caste,
but the sovereignty of a whole people; not for the
gratification of hate, but the realisation of fraternal-
ism. And it won’t be fought with the weapons of
violence. The workers don’t need bullets when
they’ve got brains. “This country belongs to us,”
they say when they come back from the war.
“Haven’t we just saved it from destruction? Get off the
land which is ours by all that is sacred to humanity.
Down with these monopoly fences! Away with
these insolent notices—‘Trespassers will be prose-
cuted I’ ”
It is fairly serviceable industrial guff when
poured out hot from a platform, but it
doesn’t stand the cold test of print. In the
first place- the workers alluded to haven’t
saved Australia. A lot of them haven’t even
tried to. Australia has been saved by the
natives of Belgium, France, Russia, Servia
and Britain. If the principle of spoils to
the victors applies, the Commonwealth should
be divided up, not amongst the gallant lads
who are now roosting beneath the shadow of
the Pyramids, but amongst a horde of mostly
low-wage foreigners for whom the unions
have little use. The downing of the mono-
poly fences and the abolition of the law of
trespass is another glowing impracticability.
It is the sort of reform that cuts both ways,
and kicks from either end. It raises the
problem, not merely of Cecil’s sheep-run
and Ronald’s cattle-ranch, but of Bill’s
front garden and Jim’s bull. Also Herb’s
goat. If, as an outcome of the war, Parlia-
ment decides that there shall be no more
sheep-stations, there will logically be no
more backyards. The householder will be
liable to find poor men slumbering in his
fowl-house and rich men using his shed as
a garage. His poultry will have to live in
the dining-room by night, and be shepherded
vigilantly by day. That is to say, these
things would happen if the insane situation
anticipated by the Worker ever came to
pass. Of course it never will. Meanwhile,
the expression of such flagrant Ben
TiLLETT-isms by the press mouthpiece of the
workers renders the cause ludicrous and dis-
creditable among sober-minded and intelli-
gent people, which is the class from which
Labor derives its best recruits.

Sweet Lavender.
One of the many neglected industries of
Victoria —not to mention the rest of Aus-
tralia —is flower-growing for the production
of essential oils; and because it is neglected
the local manufacturers of “toilette re-
quisites” have a grievance against the Law
which, in this particular case, seems honestly
desirous of doing its best for the local
gardener. Rectified spirits of wine, costing
about 335. per gallon in the open market, is
available to manufacturers of scents, etc., at
10s. per gallon, provided at least one-third
of the essential oils used by them are dis-
tilled from Australian-grown flowers. The
manufacturers’ trouble is that they can’t
obtain the necessary vegetable supply. Obvi-
ously they might grow perfume plants them-
selves sufficient to yield all the essential oils
they require; but the fact remains that they
have left the gardening job to other people,
and the other people do not worry about it.
So the local manufacturers of “toilette re-
quisites,” being stuck-up for lack of local
products, reckon themselves hardly treated
by the Law in being forbidden to use more
than two-thirds of imported oils, seeing that
their goods must compete against artfully-
composed and profusely-advertised brands
from abroad. A certain quantity of lavender
flower still is raised for commercial pur-
poses, but it barely suffices to supply the
perfume-makers with their quota of native
oil, so there is none left for the other manu-
facturer. Whatever humor there is in the
situation depends upon the recent movement
for brightening the lives of Victorian felons.
Formerly the governor of Pentridge Gaol
devoted three or four acres of the prison
grounds to the cultivation of sweet lavender.
With that supply there was enough essential
oil to go round. His successor, however,
eradicated the odorous plantation, and con-
verted the acres into a football ground. The
“jugged” citizens thus enjoy their recrea-
tions on the site of a blighted industry, as
it were. Again, no doubt, the men whose
supply of essential oils was thus stopped
l should have taken measures to ensure a
larger production. Yet. even so, from their
point of view thev have a grievance: their
manufacturing business—and manufacture
is really their business —is at present para-
lysed by a law which under existing con-
ditions benefits nobody.
+.
As a fresh cause of trouble descends upon
the N.S.W. Ministry every morning, the
sudden resignation of Carmichael, who
only lately returned from a luxurious
European tour, has attracted little more
than passing notice. It would require the
enlistment of the whole weary outfit for
service in Turkey to cause anything like
real astonishment. Carmichael’s nominal
reason for throwing up his job is resentment
at not being nominated by Holman as
acting-Premier during the absence from Syd-
ney of both that young man and deputy-
leader Cann. This is only part of the story.
Carmichael has resigned mainly in conse-
quence of a hankering after the limelight
and partly in order to dodge the falling
brick. Since last election he has been easily
the most garrulous politician in Australia.
He has talked morning, noon and night—
about education, the price of apples, how
to cook cheap meals, the fructifying influ-
ence of loans; of unemployment, the Ger-
mans, music, painting, literature; about
anything and everything under the sun. He
even burst into the arena of defence and
started forming rifle clubs. All this
has, of course, been gall and wormwood to
other members of the Cabinet. Exactly
what Carmichael was after no one seemed
able to fathom, except those discerning
cynics who understand how sweet a morsel
notoriety may be to men of a certain tem-
perament. Like jealousy it feeds upon itself.
Carmichael, of course, had visions of
achieving speedy greatness or having it
thrust upon him; but these became rather
clouded lately. Reversionary interests were
obviously being acquired by others; so a
partly-disgruntied Carmichael, to cover a
deep chagrin, and anxious to get farther
into the front of a rather confused picture,
decided to make his dramatic bolt. The
situation thus created is, in view of the
Caucus vote at last meeting, when George
Black was selected as Minister for Agricul-
ture, one of particular interest for Holman,
since it may place the discontented section
of the party in a majority. The certain
thing, however, is that, whether this be the
case or not, the house of cards cannot be ex-
pected to stand up much longer.

When the N.S.W. Unnecessary Commodities
Commission was appointed, the Holman
Government announced that the capitalist
had been scotched at last, and for the future
he would have to ask the Commission’s leave
before advancing prices. To back this up,
the gullible public was told that the Com-
mission had issued a circular informing pur-
veyors of foodstuffs that at least seven days’
clear notice of any intended increase in
prices must be given, so that the validity
of the reasons advanced might be considered.
All hot air! Any day almost you can still
read things like this: —
One of the leading Sussex-street houses yesterday-
advanced the price of cheese -Jd. per lb. For special
brands they wanted Bd., and for ordinary prime
loaf from the South Coast 7j|d. per lb. They justi-
fied the movement by stating that prices in both
Melbourne and Brisbane were higher than they were
in Sydney, and that production in the cheese manu-
facturing districts of New South Wales was rapidly
declining owing to the dry weather.
No application to increase the price of
cheese was made to the Necessary Com-
modities Commission by these Sussex-street
people; yet neither the Government nor the
Commission has taken any action. It looks
as if the Young-Man-in-a-Hurry, knowing
that he has annoyed some wheat farmers by
his Acquisition Act, throws a sop to another
lot of farmers by allowing the people in the
child-sweating cow country to squeeze as
hard as they like.
One of the results of our muddled process
of fixing wages is showing itself in a disin-
clination amongst young Australians to take
to skilled trades, or to continue in them
when the journeyman stage has been reached.
Awards have been so made that workmen
engaged (say) as laborers can in many
cases command wages nearly as high as
those of a tradesman, along with greater
privileges and, as a rule, more em-
ployment. The unskilled laborers can also,
if they are physically well developed, get
the higher wages straight away instead of
having to accept something much poorer
during a period of apprenticeship. Nearly
all the Unions dealing with the affairs of
workmen in the more skilled branches of
industry possess a disheartening record of
men who have broken their indentures or
abandoned, the trade after working for years
at it, in order to seek the wages and con-
ditions of the unskilled laborer —a deplor-
able business, because it means that the
country is losing the benefit of these men’s
skill, and is thus handicapped in its struggle
to be something more than a wood-and-water
joey for the foreign manufacturer.
If ever there was a case of accepting
“respectability” as an excuse for crime, it
seems to be that in which a foreman printer
was tried at Millicent (S.A.) for “larceny
of the sum of £lO 3s. 9d., moneys belonging
to” the local racing club. According to the
undisputed facts —which this paper takes
from Adelaide Register (l/3/’ls) —having
the job of printing the totalisator tickets,
the accused simply printed and pocketed
extra tickets on every horse in each race,
and then collected dividends on the winners.
When called to account, he remarked : “I do
not know why I did it, as I was not in need
of money,” and offered to refund. On which
the two amazing Justices
commented severely upon the offence. Accused was
a young man, .and for several years had been receiv-
ing a salary that more than sufficed for the needs of
himself and his wife and family. Yet without rhyme
or reason he had elected to raise money by mean
and despicable underhand work. They could not
understand the man’s action at all. In view of the
fact that restitution was to be made, and that
accused had been dismissed from his employment,
they inflicted a fine of £lO, in default three months
imprisonment. The fine was paid.
From which it appears that, in Millicent,
to have no excuse for a crime is the best of
all excuses; also that a deliberate fraud is
fairly safe if you are rich enough to part
up a similar sum to that involved —in other
words, to lay level money against being
found out. The Bulletin lias no wish to
see any first offender of the kind in gaol:
but in this case the first offender escaoed
gaol because he had money in his pocket.
He escaped, not because gaol would be a
bad experience, but because he was rich
enough to buy his freedom. The Attorney-
General of South Aus. might do worse than
have a word or two with the Justices who
dispense such “justice” at Millicent.
Extracts from the sworn evidence of eye-
witnesses :
Paul Verlinden, wine merchant, of Aerscnot.
Towards seven o’clock in the evening we were
herded together four by four. Four men had to
come forward and the soldiers shot the group,
feigned death and escaped by hiding in a potato
field. I noticed that they afterwards visited the dead
and gave the finishing stroke to one who still lived.
I counted 41 corpses next day.
Mrs. Vansteenwinkel, of Hofstade: I saw a woman
dead on the high road. She was about 50 years old.
A bayonet had been thrust through her body. Her
name was Lisa, but I cannot produce the corpse.
Harold de Hemptinne, Count, of Ghent: At three
o’clock in the morning of the 26th August I saw in
Sempst, near Malines, a man from whom two legs
had been cut off up to the knees by the Germans;
he was charred. I also found another one whose arms
and legs were cut off; he was charred. I came
across a boy of less than 14 whose body was riddled
with bayonet thrusts.
Xavier de Ribaucourt, Military Chaplain (6th
Army Division) : On the 25th day of August I ascer-
tained that in the village of Hofstade, near Malines,
an old woman of about 60 years of age had been
killed by a dozen bayonet thrusts as the Germans
drew back before the carabineers. She still held a
needle and a cotton in her hand with which she had
been sewing. We found her in her house.
A British Consul: In a small village which has
been entirely destroyed by fire, situated between the
railway viaduct of Antwerp and Elewyt, I witnessed
the following during the last attack which waS made
on the 26th August, 1914. In a small farm an old
man was attached by the arms to the rafter of the
roof of his house. The body was quite charred.
Only the head, arms and feet were intact. Farther
on, a little boy of about 15 years of age had his
hands tied behind his back, and the body was rid-
dled with stabs of German bayonets.
Joseph Rymen, of Schaffen-lez-Diest: We discov-
ered the feet of a person whom they had buried head
downward. In this way we found Gustave Loots
and Jean Macken, already mentioned. According to
the declaration of Mrs. Loots, these people must have
been buried alive.
Incidentally a preposterous Gerhog doctor
named Moll, with some reputation as an
alienist, calls these and worse atrocities the
“collective illusion” of a nation united in a
savage desire to injure Teuton prestige!

In the bad old days the army in the field
was considered fair game for every soulless
commercial thief, and many a poor wretch
of a soldier was done to death by the
contractor at home whose life and income he
was trying to preserve. Now it is no longer
safe to rob and cheat the army —at least,
not on the grand scale that made fortunes
within, the space of a few months. Not
only! has the attitude of the stay-at-home
citizen changed to one of real solicitude, but
a more efficient age lias produced the
Kitchener type of army officer, and with
him ,an organisation which makes systematic
swindling a very dangerous game to
play, The men in the field having been
preserved at last from the very potent enemy
who used to attack them with rotten food,
rotten boots, rotten ammunition and all the
rest of it, the ghouls have been forced to
look for another unprotected victim. And
where did they find him? Why, right there
in the safe, law-hedged cities —the patient,
bovine citizen! There was no black-browed,
souare-jawed Kitchener to look after the
citizen —only a rabble of parrot politicians
and a tangle of laws that any dishonest fool
could dodge round; so, as we have seen
within the past few months, the screw has
been applied in a hundred ways to the stay-
at-home by mean-spirited traders who, hav-
ing been forced to pay an extra ha’penny,
snatch another penny from their helpless
customer and, being challenged, impudently
plead that they are only getting their own
back. These are the slinking jackals of
trade; but there are also the arrogant un-
nameables who. at a time when thousands
of brave-hearted people are dying of starva-
tion, would see the fat stock they are unable
to export driven down a steep place into the
sea rather than that the price should fall.
The exact epithet for that type of huckster
has yet to be found.

With the allied warships battering in the
front door, Russians hovering rqund in Ar-
menia, Greeks clamoring for action, and Bul-
gars moving mysteriously about in the rear of
things, the days of the Turk become more
painful and the nights more tightly filled with
apprehension. What may be in store for the
other combatants in the immediate future js
at best a subject for speculation, but it seems
impossible to doubt that tlje misguided alien
who covers the back of his head with a red
hat has got through the early doors to a place
of woe and tribulation. A circumstantial
story that the wretched Sultan, on applying
to the Austrian for assistance to repel the
Unbeliever, was curtly told to look after him-
self probably contains the truth; for the
ancient Hapsburg has about as much on his
own hands just now as he cares to think
about, and will find a still larger helping of
unpleasantness if the allied fleets join hands
across the Bosphorus. Such a happening as
this would, of course, compel the Balkan
States to sit up and fix bayonets and take
notice of things from an entirely new point
of view. Meanwhile the Bear continues to
embrace the German in Poland and the
Austrian in Galicia. Every effort to shake
off the unfriendly hug is doggedly resisted,
and mighty armies still writhe in sanguin-
ary conflict half-way across the map of
Central Europe. Yon Hindenburg’s losses
in the last series of battles are stated to have
been enormous; and Germany can’t afford
such breaches in her lines. From the
western theatre of slaughter all that floats
to the outside is the distant sound of guns
muffled beneath the military communique
and the nebulosities of official recorders.
Neither side is winning much or losing much.
News that the German is not getting enough
to eat should be held over for later con-
sideration. The main question a few
weeks hence will be whether he is getting
enough bayonet pushed through him.' The
Kaiser’s scheme for blockading Britain by
blowing up the universe generally doesn’t
n npear to have seriously disturbed anyone
during the week, and as for his apoplectic
order that another effort be made to burst
through to Calais —well, it was a long way
to Calais before, but it is a long, long way
further now. In England recruiting is
said to be satisfactory, strikes are in full
blast, and the newspapers are warning the
nation that the time to cock-a-doodle-do has
not yet arrived. News of these strikes
nrobably srive our old friend Bill much more
comfort than President Woodrow Wilson’s
cablegram about many-happy-returns.
Still another fund is being started in Eng-
land. An appeal is being made for
garments for 2000 Belgian women and girls who
expect to become mothers. ... funds or clothes,
chiefly sleeping and bed jackets, chemises, petti-
coats, stockings, etc., and old shirts.
These 2000 terrible cases are in south-eastern
districts of England. Nothing is known of
the numbers in the entire United Kingdom,
m France and still in Belgium. One of
the most repugnant features of this form of
German culture is that thousands of its
heirs will be British-born.
A side-issue seriously affecting chemists
has been raised in connection with the treat-
ment of venereal diseases in N. S. Wales.
When the British Medical Association
talked to Health Minister Flowers recently,
one of the deputation, who obviously was
not speaking out of his turn, suggested that
it should be made a “penal offence for a
person other than a legalised practitioner to
deal with a venereal case.” This proposal,
which bore the innocent appearance of being
merely a desire on the part of the B.M.A.
to drive the quack to honest work, was
promptly and earnestly “quite agreed” to
by the Minister. But the quack was onlv
half the target on which the doctors hail
their guns trained. At present hundreds
of cases of simple venereal disease are
treated by reputable pharmacists. One
Sydney chemist of good standing admits to
(>0 patients weekly; and he is probably quite
right in claiming that if qualified pharma-
cists didn’t treat simple cases in their early
stages for a few shillings, many an indiscreet
youngster wouldn’t be treated at all.
N.S.W. Full Court sat one day last week
to hear what Gent. One had to say about the
disappearance of certain funds. Tie had
been solicitor to the estate of a deceased
person, and to him certain ladies (nuns)
had paid moneys amounting to £ll,OOO for
the purpose of discharging a mortgage. Of
this money £7OOO. instead of being handed
to the trustees, had apparently been ad-
vanced to a wildly speculative concern, and
about half thereof had disappeared, the final
result being that the ladies are still in law
indebted for £5OOO to the estate. Then
there was another hiatus connected with
“the Smith estate.” After hearing what
the Judge in Equity had discovered and
what Gent. One had to say, which wasn’t
very much, their Honors remarked that it
didn’t seem the way for an officer of such
an honorable court to play cricket. So the
name of Arthur Deery was struck off the
roll.

“The Silver King” departs from Sydney
Royal on Friday night. On Saturday the
sugary “Beaucaire” will be revived, and
Julius Ivnight, a-fire with love, will tremu-
lously invite his cold, proud Mary Carlyle
to melt and lean her head on his ardent
chest. And instead of sensibly giving him
the cold shoulder, she will melt as desired,
and all will be happiness and perspiration.
