With tears in their advts., the J.C.W.
people announce that the highly successful
“High Jinks” must cease at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney, on Friday, March 19, in order to
clear the stage for the hotly-named “Cinder-
ella,” which is due to panto, on the following
evening.
According to some deceased suburban hor-
ticulturist, call an onion a half-brick if you
like, and it will still bring tears to your
eyes in spite of you. Similarly, call ye old
bellowdrama by a fancy name, tranship it
from 1915 back to 1050, put it in a fancy
costume and make it say “Verily, odds
bodikins, ye know not what ye ask, gad-
zooks,” and it is bellowdrama all the same.
Such is the case with “Lady Godiva” at Syd-
ney Adelphi. The villain may be Algar,
Earl of Northumbria, but his brass headgear
and his lace shirt front can’t hide the tall
top hat, the frock coat and the immaculate
trousers of the 1914-15 villain ; and though
you label the heroine Lady Godiva she weeps
just as well, is maltreated to the same extent,
and comes out triumphant in precisely the
same manner as Mary Martin, of the “Time :
the Present” show, who sniffles through
three unfriendly acts and then kisses ’er
lovin’ ’usband in the fourth. But, although
it is bellowdrama, “Lady Godiva” is a cut
above most of them. The costumes and
setting alone would account for this; but
the company also rises to the occasion. To
be tied down to a “bad girl” series for months,
and then to get on to something worth while,
must be like taking off a pair of tight boots.
The result is that “Lady Godiva” threatens
to be a big success. Miss Oriel Hotson
(Lady G.), Gwen Dorise (Lady Aldwyth,
her sister), Rutland Beckett (Algar) and
C. R. Stanford (Unwolf) will be mainly
responsible if it is. And when Godiva’s
horse becomes more accustomed to the sur-
roundings, it, too, should be a valuable asset.

The cycling Westwoods, now at the Tivoli,

The authorities having declared the “Lucky
Number” Carnival out of order, a pinch of
skill has had to be blended with the mess
of luck to be served up at Sydney’s White
City to-night (Thursday). Under the new
arrangement a couple of dukes in ordinary
attire will parade the amusement park
armed each with a £lO cheque, which will
be handed over to the first he- and she-male
doinandiijg it. If this prospecting for a
tenner does not provide enough excitement
for one night, the visitors can turn to the
regular thrills, which will be in full gallop
—including “Fighting the Flames, and the
Leap of Death.” At the end of this week
the Leap will move on to make room for
another sensational turn.

Another consignment of Sydney pictures:
Lyric : “The Man from the Sea,” being one of the
few cases wherein the sea does give up its dead,
although the corpse isn’t as dead as expected.
Colonial: “The Scorpion Sting,” a fearsome thing,
evidently written by a German, wherein an un-
fortunate murderer has the option of getting (1)
married or (2) hanged.
Empress : “The Poor Folk’s Boy,” concerning a
monish lender and the family he busted. A de-
lightful little allegory, maybe, N.S.W. being the
busted family. Willie Holman in that case is
the pretty Pomeranian poodle that leads the blind
old man to the pawnshop. Unfortunately Holman
was busy explaining something when the picture was
taken, so he is not in it.

A lot of mud has flowed down the Yarra
since Anderson first staged “When London
Sleeps,” but its revival at Melb. King’s last
Saturday was almost as amazing as
ever. No intermediate melodramas have
produced their thrills with finer audacity
than the yarn of the Circus Queen who
bestowed her priceless heart and thumping
big legacy on the Star of the Air. When
that spirited lady (or somebody like her)
walks from a burning roof-top by way of a
George Mozart in a burlesque of toe "Post
and Peasant ” overture at Sydney vuo[?].
telephone wii-e, she seems to have tested
Providence about as far as it will go; but
her final rescue from incineration puts the
lid on her pot of luck. She is about to be
grilled sacrificially at the feet of a brazen
idol when her young man emerges from the
interior of the heathen deity and pulls her
out of the steam. In all the annals of stage
gallantry no other hero has been quite so
opportune. Guy Hastings puts in some
hard work as this dauntless David, and
Miss Wilton is the indestructible Queenie
who makes him happy. Temple Harrison
is the comic relief man who feigns to be a
lady of easy virtue at a den of infamy run
by the villainess, and Cannam is the in-
credible Bertie who mistakes Harrison afore-
said for a soiled dove. And there are others,
including the emphatic Vox in the character
of the' Crookedest Captain in Christendom.

At Melbourne Princess’s this week the
Willoughby management is revealing the
loveliness of George Cross in the tattered
Ouidaesque frenzy, “Under Two Flags.”
George pictures Bertie Cecil lioyellieu, and
nobly incurs the responsibility for his
brother’s crime with the same old kiss-curl
on his blameless brow. Also he brings the
high-heeled boots and other familiar effects
to Beauty’s aid, and for about three hours
revels in military upholstery and borrowed
height. Miss Vera Itemee, as Cigarette, in-
dulges in an evening’s intoxication on
George’s glaring charms, and finally sacri-
fices her life with a noisy grandeur that
leaves no gunpowder to be desired. Chat-
eauroy, the Black Hawk, who stifles the
hero’s _ high ambitions with petty persecu-
tion, is a rather unkempt-looking ruffian,
perhaps correctly presented by R. Bellairs
as a case of soap deferred and a haircut in
abeyance. A refreshing contrast is Lord
Rockingham (Ronald Riley), whose forged
signature pushes the lamentable Bertie into
exile. Every day is washing day with Rock-
ingham, and even out in the Algerian desert
he maintains the freshly starched and ironed
appearance. J. J. Ennis is the blithering
old Lord Royellieu, puffed out with the pride
of excessive caste, and Frank Neil officiates
as Rake, the. vociferous shadow of the lead-
ing man. Miss Bowman is a graceful misfit
in the part of Lady Guinevere, and Miss
Jean Robertson is a singularly dull bird as
the “Silver Pheasant” that George Cross
finds so much to his liking. Next Saturday
the beauteous actor will try some Quayle on
toast as John Tornado in “The Christian.”

At the Tivoli, Melbourne, a new turn
-—Thorne and Leopold—was added last
week to a bill which otherwise is mostly
Follies, plus Rickaby. The Follies continue
to suggest that a little scenic setting and
chorus go a long way towards infusing
variety into a. vaudeville show. Alfredo, for
instance, carries more weight as a violinist
in a garden scene than if he were merely
a with no background to speak of.
Monte Wolf, the rag-timer, was down with
indisposition last week, and his substitute
left much to be desired. Monte leaves a
good deal, but the locum tenons surpassed
him in that respect. An important recrdit
to the Tivoli forces is promised in the person
of James Corbett, formerly of the prize-ring,
but now of theatrical circles.

GILBERT AND SULLIVAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Though Gilbert’s wit may quit the s.age,
And Sullivan’s chaste music be
Belittled by a future gauge
Of operatic levity,
New cynics shall, unthinking, quote
Some lines the flippant poet wrote,
And plagiarists, with pompous ptan,
Repeat the art of Sullivan.
The theme of “Woman, Wine and Song”
By those twin spirits was tabooed ;
They never turned the list’ning throng
To thoughts lascivious or lewd.
The dead musician charms our soul
With plaintive strains, or sweetly droll
And daffodils ’midst nettles wave
On Gilbert’s literary grave.
They offer, in a form unique,
Good mental food for maid or child—
Refreshment to the men who seek
A well of humor undefiled.
In Gil. and Sul. we taste the truth
Which age distils from dreams of youth.
In tuneful song and pungent wheeze
Abide eternal verities.
VICTORIA.
O. K.

“The Square Triangle,” the principal
sermon at Hoyt’s National and Olympia
(Melbourne) this week, is based on a text
which sets out the folly of a leisure mis-
spent in the decollete society of the very
naughty. “Merely Mother,” an equally long
discourse, is an improving dissertation on
neglected filial duties; while “Through the
Firing Line” is a successful attempt to stage
war with a blood-soaked Belgian back-
ground. Among the topical literature “The
Clowns of Europe” earns loud sounds from
the populace for finishing in precipitate
allegory the chequered career of Kaiser
AVillie and his witless heir.
In a Melbourne timber-yard :
Hans Heysen, showing some more pictures at the
Melbourne Athenaeum, is still practising forestry
with diligence. Gums in all sizes and atmospheres
are limned with a magical deftness and feeling for
color that sets the critic combing his thoughts for
the necessary gracious superlative. Several old
friends, including the massive “Sunset Haze” and
“AVhite Gums,” have not lost their power to make,
you wide-eyed; but there are forests of new saplings
shimmering under bright skies, or showing daintily
through mist like the shapely appointments of a
Summer girl. Two or three excellent pictures are
lent by Melba, one conspicuously rich study, “After-
glow,” revealing the typical gum-strewn scenery
turning pink in the fag-end of a golden afternoon.
Some striking chalk drawings lend variety to the
display; and a taking set of pastels demonstrate a
hlue-shirted bloke in various stages of tired feeling,

“De Yid”: South Australia is divided
against itself in the matter of pray-
ing. One-half the population is prov-
ing hard for rain. The other, the
country half is imploring the Lord
to hold up the flood-gates till the last
ot the summer heat has gone, otherwise thev
know they will have a quick growth of
early grass promptly scorched to powder,
lhe Adehude half wants to see the reservoir
mied. Not only are householders forbidden
to water their gardens, but they have been
urged to refrain- from using the plunge-bath
and to content themselves with a basin and
sponge. The knowledge that they can pro-
bably wash themselves much cleaner that
way takes nothing from the miseries of the
cold tubber. It is three months now since
the Waterworks Department declined to
make any further connections to either the
sewerage or water-supply systems in the
Metropolitan district. This means that quite
two-tlnrds of the building trade has been
hung-up. There have probably never been
so many skilled artisans out of work in the
State since the drought of the early ’9o’s.

A war scene.
Louis .T. Lohr (the father of Marie)
returned to Melbourne the other week, and
is now receiving his old friends at a beautiful
big house adjacent to the Cyclorama. Un-
fortunately the business that brought
“Daddy” back to town from the sandy
reaches of Inverloch was his health, and
the roomy domicile aforesaid is the new
private hospital associated with St. Vin-
cent’s. There is an operating theatre on
the premises, but Lohr, having wearied of
the life, is not going to give a show
there. As a matter of fact, he is getting
better. It was no easy matter for such a
very good fellow to get much better, but
Lohr managed to do it.

“Mid-Channel”: “H. 0.” (B. 25/2/’ls) is
radiant over J. M. Barrie’s adoption or the
Shaw method of describing his scenes and
characters. When a play resembles a novel
then it isn’t a play. Shaw gives quite a
couple of pages in introducing his scenes.
Here’s a sample from “Capt. Brassbound’s
Conversion.” A man and a woman enter.
After 12 lines spent on the man, this goes
on for the woman: “The lady is between 30
and 40, tall, very good-looking, sympathetic,
intelligent, tender and humoring” (his dia-
logue ought to indicate all this), “dressed
with cunning simplicity”—(the following
is just tripe) “not as a business-like, tailor-
made, gaitered tourist, but as if she lived
at the next cottage and had dropped in for
tea in blouse and flowered straw hat. A
woman of great vitality,” etc., unto the 15th
line. With his scenes—even interiors—you
have to wade through a morass of this stuff.
Now take an instance from Pinero, always
graphically brief in the description of his
scenes: “Iris is a beautiful woman with a
soft, appealing voice, and movements- in-
stinct with simple grace and dignity.”
Another: “Lady Orreyed is a pretty, affected
doll of a woman, with a mincing voice and
flaxen hair.” Just one more: “Zoe is a
charming, animated, bright-eyed woman
wearing some costly furs.” In his scenic
descriptions Pinero avoids anything this way:
“This being the interior of a house which
might be expected to possess (2) tennis
courts, (3) motor garages, and (5) or (6)
acres of date-plum plantations.” That is
Shavian. “H. 0.” calls Pinero’s description
telegraphic and cataloguey. Which is the
better —a telegram giving a graphic outline
of an occurrence or a long-winded rigmarole
in letter form which leaves one wondering
what the devil it is all about?
“Harrison O.”: “Moana’s” excuse for
those Yankee dramatists who expand ideas
suitable for 15-minute sketches into three-
and four-act plays is too thin. He alleges:
“The one-act plav is just about the most
difficult literary product to plant anywhere.
Unless the one-acter is of the slapstick
variety suitable for impolite vaudeville, it
is as hard to get rid of as a turned-up nose.”
The reverse is really true. One-acters of
the right sort have not been in so great
demand for many years, owing to the in-
vasion of the .’alls by stars from the “legiti-
mate.” As these and a host of smaller fry
want one-act playlets, work of the kind has
lately been turned out by Shaw, Pinero,
Edward Knoblauch and J. M. Barrie; and
none of it that I have met is of the slapstick
variety. In any case the dramatist who, by
padding, makes what should be a one-act
play into a four-acter is no more entitled to
respect than the grocer who mixes sand with
his sugar.
Harry Plimmer, who has again got into
harness, and with Winter Hall takes a com-
pany to Maoriland, is the grandson of a tough
old party who landed at Wellington with
Captain Cook or Tasman or someone, and,
pitching his ti-tree whare on the beach, re-
fused to shift until the wind blew him up
among the hills. A newspaper office now
stands . where the old man’s garden was;
but Plimmer’s Steps remain a crooked in-
convenience for neighboring goats. Plim-
mer’s company will include Beatrice Day.
Cert Boswell, Nellie Calvin, Muriel Dale,
Cyril Mackay, P. Plimmer, Latham and
Harry Halley.
“P- . Gram”: The fatuousness of the
“criticism” of the daily press was painfully
shown in the latest Sydney notices of the
Julius Knight season. Irene Browne, who
“supports,” was recently credited with
“making the hit of her career as Berenice in
‘The Sign of the Cross,’ a performance her
Nellie Denver could not possibly come up
to,” or words to that effect. As a matter
of fact, it is as Nellie Denver and in similar
parts that Irene finds her level. As a melo-
drama heroine she might make good. As a
Patrician of liome—Heaven forbid ! The
only woman who looked and walked and
acted like a patrician in the “Cross” re-
vival was Dorothy Cumming, even though
she was cast for the thankless part of
Ancaria, who was bold and bad and danger-
ous to know.
“C. Ockney”: Sydney has many oppor-
tunities of viewing the pro. and pro-ess at
close quarters. M. and Madame Grease-
paint are constantly to be met at tea-fights*
out-door. processions and White City cele-
brations, and the public must have grown
familiar by now with the open-air make-up
of all its favorite mimes. But yet another
chance presents itself of rubbing shoulders
with the mummer—the so-called “profes-
sional matinSe.” Ostensibly the morning
show is given, “in response to many requests
from the artists at the various theatres.”
The announcement continues naively: “The
public will be admitted at the ordinary
prices.” When “professional matinees”
were introduced in England (I believe Wil-
son .Barrett was the culprit') the general
public was rigorously excluded, and it took
its matinee on the doorstep of the theatre
watching the stars go in. But nowadays it
would seem the pros, in front of the cur-
tain are as much nart of the show as those
behind, which, all things considered is
deuced cheap at “ordinary prices.”
“E. 0.5.”: Re picture shows in the Pacific
Islands (B. 25/2/’l5), an enterprising drink-
dispenser at Port Moresby < Papua) runs
one adjacent to his pub on three nights a
week. A seat (canvas , deck chair) costs
3s. Floor is of concrete; roof, the glittering
and elevated sky. The lighting is by elec-
tricity, which also generates cold storage.
Died _ recently, in Maoriland, David Scott,
who built his own monument in many light-
houses that blink around the bitten coast
of the shivery islands. Born in Tasmania
73 years ago, he was schooled in M.L. and
served an apprenticeship to shipbuilding in
Sydney. When the Gabriel’s Gully gold rush
set in. Scott headed back to M.L. and
entered the service of the old Panama Ship-
ping Co. From that he passed into the
Marine Department, and, later, went to the
Public Works Department as a railway-
bridge inspector. In 1880 he was back with
the Marine Department, and took charge of
lighthouse construction and maintenance.
jThe lights at Cane Campbell, Cuvier
Island, Pencarrow. Nugget Head, Akaroa
Head, Cape Maria van Diemen. Palliser
Bay, Mokohinau, East Cape and Hikurangi
Point were nart of the family born and
reared under his roof.
Another Crimea veteran heard the Last
Trumpet a few days ago in Southern Maori-
land. Captain Sydney H. Davies was a son
of Admiral George Davies, and followed his
father to the ocean in 1851. He served with
the Black Sea fleet, the naval batteries
before Sebastopol, and the land forces (16th
Foot) during the unpleasantness with Rus-
sia in ’54-’56. At the close of hostilities
Davies went to Canada, thence to the Dis-
united States to whirl into the Uncivil War.
He joined the Confederate army, served!
with “Stonewall” Jackson and became
assistant Inspector-General to Heath’s Divi-
sion. When Lee surrendered, Davies adven-
tured to M.L. and butted into the war with
the natives. He earned the M.L. medal in
that disturbance ; and when the trouble was
over, there being no other shindy handy, he
settled down to the quiet life.
THE FIDDLER ON THE COAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a wizened old fellow,
And he came to us there in the rain,
As we lounged on the Duchess, waiting
For the midnight Sydney train.
He said: “I haven’t a penny;
I’ve only my fiddle here.
Will you give me a ride to the city
For a tune, Mr. Engineer?”
It was dark and wet at the Junction,
And Joe swore, clear and true:
“We haven’t no time for music;
We’re only the engine-crew.
But climb up there on the tender,
And hang on tight when she rolls”
, And that’s where we put the fiddler,
Up on the dirty coals.
With the midnight mail behind us,
We heard the town-clocks chime,
And v the throb of her spinning drivers
Soon quickened to racing time.
Her hoarse, deep whistle echoed
And rang as we raced along,
AVith the galloping side-rods singing
Their brave old galloping song.
But into the well-known measure
We heard strange voices weave—
Sounds of jovial laughter,
Sorrowful sounds that grieve.
Then Joe, by' hiS: shining levers,
Swore-: “You may eat me whole,
If it isn’t that fiddler fellow,
Fiddlin’ up on the coal.”
Shovel, to flaring furnace,
I coaled the old girl well—-
The diier of all the ranges,
Fast, and sound as a bell.
And the red glare showed the fiddler
Like a little, earth-stained mole,
Swaying so, to her swinging,
AAld/fiddling up on the coal.
A } J
Sweet and cleaiAas our whistle
Echoing dowJKa glen,
His strings niglL burst to give us
“Thte Marchjdf the Cameron Men.”
Then he played us “Tipperary”—
It’s a long, long way, no doubt;
But,it wouldn’t be far to travel,
If we let thd Duchess out.
' “Daddy, good-night! Dear Daddy”—
’.Twas a wailing tune he played.
Thrbugh the yell of her wheels' Joe heard it,
As he rattled her down the grade.
Faster than regulations,
He let the long train coast;
And we galloped her up the incline
To the strains of “The Washington Post.”
Then Joe stepped back from his levers,
Crunching the coal he strode.
Said he: “Come down, old fellow,
I reckon you’ve pulled your load.”
And that’s how we came to the city,
Which is mostly the wanderers' goal,
With the little fiddler sleeping,
And only the rain on the coal.
N.S.'W.
WILL LAWSON.

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
John Bull is tightening the blockade
against food on its way to Germany. The
professors of economics at the University of
Berlin have issued an appeal to the popu-
lace to eat potato bread. Any Sunday,
now, in Germany, the popular hymn is “The
Wheat By-and-Bye.”
About the German scheme of furtively
dropping explosives on old-age pensioners
and retired butlers in England, W. T. Bull,
who lives close handy at Great Winchester-
Street, London Wall, E.C., writes: —
“G.D.” (B. ’29/10/’l4), commenting upon the
efforts of Count Zeppelin and the Butcher of Berlin
to put the fear of the Almighty (Bill’s junior partner)
into the inhabitants of this isle, mentions that
insurance against damage by hostile aircraft was
being arranged at eighteenpence per cent. At the
time his remarks were penned the figure was 0.K.,
but the first series of cold shivers having passed,
bomb-risk insurance has slumped heavily. Even
though the rate has come down with a run, it is
difficult to find premium-payers. You can now get
an insurance effected at one shilling per £lOO,
while if you show signs of breaking off the dis-
cussion and trusting to luck and Kitchener’s anti-
aircraft corps, you will be offered a sharp reduction
to close the business. The fact is, a Zeppelin is an
ungainly craft which cannot easily be handled un-
less weather conditions are ideal, while the excellent
target offered by the big sausage makes a gunner’s
mouth water. Further, its range of effective action
is hopelessly limited. When 250 miles from home
the elongated cigar must hop back or be prepared
for trouble. The main German base is at Cologne,
about 175 miles from the Smoke. It is rumored
that at places outside the prospective area of de-
struction insurance brokers will make a present of
a policy to all and sundry, just to pretend to them-
selves they are busy. But don’t believe that!
Lloyd’s men give nothing away, except their pals.

A really brilliant romance-writer has been
told by an Adelaide police court that she was
applying her talent in the wrong direction.
Mrs. “Mary Nellie Smith” had claimed a
maternity bonus, E. M. Franks registered
the birth, and Mrs. Murry was the lady who
attended professionally ; also Kathleen Gray
witnessed the claim, and a Mr. and Mrs.
Birmingham gave a kind of general support.
The whole crowd:—including the alleged
mother —had no existence except in the
super-heated imagination of the prisoner,
whose name bore no resemblance to any of
theirs. She put in her best work when, being
visited by a suspicious official, she told of a
half-sovereign lent to “Mrs. Smith,” which
she had little hope of ever seeing again. She
even hinted a dark suspicion that Mrs. Smith
was not properly married. After which the
careless, artistic temperament led her into
the obvious error of applying for the mater-
nity bonus on her own account, in a hand-
writing which was already familiar to the
Department. In court she admitted every-
thing, and a clever counsel, throwing the
blame on her husband and five hungry chil-
dren. got her released as a First Offender.

“Vagabond” from the trenches: —
The part played by the London mo-bus in the war
is a side-issue worth remembering when Australia
considers the development of street transport. Few of
us care to think now of what the British Expedition,
ary Force would have done in the early days without
those ’buses. Later all sorts of motor-lorries and
carts were commandeered and rushed to the Front;
but right at the awful start it was the
mo-bus that the chief transport Wallah had
to depend upon. Hundreds, of ’em petrol-
led off in a long string from London to Plymouth
and other West Country ports, whence they were
shipped over by steamer loads; and ever since they
have been carting food and forage, ammunition and
troops, all over France and Belgium. One meets them
here, there and everywhere, some of them still with
the old legends up, others coated in drab “service
dress,” a barrel of petrol hoisted on the doorstep,
and an extra rain-shield rigged over the driving seat.
Whatever arguments there may be In favor of the tram-
car for street traffic, it is obvious that it isn’t much
use for rushing troops or stores around the country.
Not only the ’buses themselves, but the men at the
wheel can be made use of at their own particular and
tremendously important job. On the other hand, I
wonder just how many Australian tram-drivers are fit
to tackle any job required by an army in thi field.
They would probably be like their own trolly-cars—
mighty useful on the lines; quite useless off them.

“Huffin Guffin”: As I write this, sheep on
the banks of the Wannon River, in Western
Victoria, are feeding from lopped branches
of sheoak. I have never seen small stock
fed thus before, though in the late ’7o’s I
saw bullockies sneaking oak for their cattle
from the squatters’ trees. Bullocks in those
direful days would travel miles to the sound
of the axe. So would the station hands —
to catch the bullockies and give them their
kind regards or something. But feeding
sheep on sheoak in Western Vic., within a
few miles of the Henty country—it’s a whole
essay on the drought.
* * * *
A new terror has been added to the life
of the Sydney wowser, and to that of the
mild ginger-beer-drinking citizen who is not
a wowesr. This is the arsenical
bottle. The sudden death of one man, and
the violent illness of several others, after
having drunk ginger-beer, resulted in an
examination* of the pots that had contained
the tipple, when it was discovered that one
of them still contained enough arsenic to
kill two men. The" evidence of an officer
of the Health Department was that the
bottle-Washing machine in the ginger-beer
factory was “not working properly,” and
that “owing to the pressure of water not
being sufficient” it was possible that a
“hardened rim of some sediment” (arsenic
or any other pleasant tonic) might remain in
the bottles before the ginger-beer was put
in. Yet, although the Coroner said “there
is no doubt, of course, that the arsenic must
have been in the bottle before it was washed,
and remained in during the time it was
washed through some apparent defect in the
machinery,” he declared that there was “no
criminal 'liability against anyone.” This does
not. of course, mean that there is no civil
liability and, therefore, the relatives of
the dead man may have something to
say, as well as the persons who just escaped
death, after being violently ill. But that is
not enough: society ought to take a hand
and create a criminal liability, if it doesn’t
at present exist.
* * * *
Sydney publicans, who a few short months
ago filled a large hall, and, having flung out
two or three recalcitrants, passed a resolu-
tion to push the price of irrigation upwards,
cementing this decision by the solemn roar-
ing of “God Save the King,” are now torn
asunder by recriminatory gibe and provoca-
tive reproach. Having passed the resolution
that the democratic pot should be fourpence
and whisky-and-soda sevenpence, Bung
rushed back to the seat of custom, altered
his cash register, issued emphatic instruc-
tions to his nymphs, and stood back to take
the consequences—in cash. But ere the
sun had set backslidings were reported ; and
next day there were definite rumors in select
circles that a pot might still be had for
the . serviceable thrum. Fierce warnings
against blacklegging were thrown at every
Boniface in the metropolis suspected of a
wavering loyalty; yet within a month
another scrap of paper had been thrown to
the . winds, and most hotels were working
again on the old schedule. Now all of them
have been ordered to fall back, wherefore
the suppression of thirst is undertaken with
promptness and despatch all round at
the former tariff. But though the parched
manhood of the city has viewed without
emotion this hasty re-adjustment, woe and
lamentation only can be heard from the
council room of the trade serangs; and mur-
murings about “regret mingled with aston-
ishment,” “disloyal blacklegs,” and “disaster
and ruination” are rumbling like the rever-
berations of eternity.
FIRST AID.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I sat alone in the bush to-day and a tree-top wind
went by,
And the fragrant peace and cheep of birds, unseen
in the branches high,
Took me back again to that dim stream where our
luck we used to try.
’Twas little we caught in those still depths but the
golden perch of Peace,
And the long, long thoughts, the restful thoughts,
chat give to the heart release,
While the willow leaves came whirling down, and
the Earth bade Sorrow cease.
Oh, not abroad in the crowded street, or home with
the babes and wife,
Shall a weary man find strong, clear thought to
nerve him for further strife,
And arm him against the word of hate that wounds
like a Gurkha's knife.
Come out! Gome out to the wordless trees that
straight to your heart will croon !
Come out! Come out from your dusty books!
Away to the bush, you loon!
Nature, your mother, awaits you there with peace
as her wondrous boon !
Do you think you’re done with the Earth for good,
you pile of breathing clay!
From dust you came —to the dust you go ! Away to
the trees ! Away !
And Red Cros3 Nature will bind your wounds as
she’s binding mine to-day!
N.S.W.

From “Member of the Town Planning
Association”: —
“O’Do,” who writes (B. 25/2/’l5) accusing the
various town-planning associations of inaction, has
evidently been frustrated in some nefarious plan
to subdivide pocket-handkerchief allotments. Either
that or it is to “O’Do’s” interests to decry a move-
ment which, in its onward sweep, is defeating the
rascally efforts of those who would stifle their fellow
men in tenements unfit for use as dog-kennels. '1 he
various town-planning associations are composed of
honorary officers and noble-hearted men and women,
who see in the movement the quickest and surest
means of ameliorating the conditions of life in the
sunless under world. They do not race about the
country soliciting town-planning jobs. But their ad-
vice is always freely given to tho- e who seek it. In
the case of Manly (N.S.W.) a complete scheme was
prepared for the local council, llecently the clauses
in the N.S.W. Local Government Amending Bill
relating to town-planning were reviewed by the
Council of the Association, and certain suggestions
made were appreciatively acknowledged by the Govern-
ment. A start had to be made somewhere and some-
low, and the N S.W. Association decided that the
wisest policy in the beginning was to educate the
masses to a higher appreciation of the benefit of
town planning. This line was pursued in the first

Sydney police arfe conducting a campaign
for the suppression of street orators of the
Socialist persuasion, and several offenders
have been fined under new traffic regulations
for “speaking in a public street, thereby
attracting together a number of persons in
such a way as to inconvenience other per-
sons in the street.” These men are enthusi-
astically “taking out” the alternative. The
Socialists, who would probably object to
having their meetings suppressed in any case,
absolutely refuse to move off while the Salv’
Army is allowed to give its testimony pn
the opposite corner. Last week a large body
of police, instead of issuing summonses
against the street orators who refused to
move on, arrested them on the spot and
incidentally gave a fine imitation of the
manner in which a heavyweight John Hop
can overpower a loquacious miscreant of
nine stone. The Socialists are now threaten-
ing a “fill the gaols” campaign, the idea be-
ing to burst the prison walls by the pressure
of martyrs within. Meantime the Salv’
Army continues to occupy the other corner
and exhort its dear brother to come to Glory
and throw in a sprat and make the collection
up to a bob.
* * * *
The Rhenish Zeitunc 4 admits that Eng-
lishmen possess many of the German’s best
virtues. In fact, everybody seems to possess
them- —except the Germans.
From a puddle in Flanders :
In The Bulletin of October 22 (it’s a long way to
Tipperary) a writer propounds the theory that the
attempts of British tars to save the enemy who is
struggling to keep his nose above a watery grave,
after having enthusiastically blown the said enemy’s
ship to Glory, “has vividly illustrated the British dread
of death by drowning.’’ I wonder what sort of patent
“dread” the writer would label the heroism that
brings the V.C. to British privates who leave the
shelter of the trenches to bring in wounded Germans!
There’s a much easier explanation of this save-your-
enemy spirit than any theoretical land or water
“dread.” It is simply the inherent sporting instinct
which prompts a boxer to hold out his hand and hoist
his opponent to his feet and say something nice and
soothing after having spent an hour or so fren-
ziedly trying to knock him into the middle of next
week and out the other side.

“Sr” : The Hobart railway station is to be
rebuilt. It is also proposed to replace the
one at Launceston as soon as a deep-water
pier is fixed. The northern town has at
present the clumsiest and most dilapidated
railway outfit in Australia. Viewed from
the front it looks like a col-
lection of bankrupt coffee-
stalls, and from the side it has
been mistaken for a stack of
second-hand palings. Hobart’s
station is only a degree better.
The engineer’s office is in a
sort of dog kennel. The man-
ager inhabits an independent
cottage on the boundary-line
between two smells —the creek
and the gasometer. There is
only one siding where a train
of decent size can put in ; if
that is occupied by any other
old steam ’bus the
train has to sleep
out among the trucks
on a side line and
there discharge pas*
sengers. But to im-
prove things means to
change things; and
Hobart is so afraid
of change that, there
is bound to be a row
—just as there would
be if it were pro-
posed to put lavatory
carriages on the
Island’s one “ex-
press” or provide a
railway - refreshment
room en route. True,
the train stops for
refreshments; but to
get them you have to
cross a paddock and
a road and breast
the bar of a way-
side pub, and maybe get your pocket picked
in the process.

“Pint Pot” imparts the odor of sanctity
to beef* —
About nationalisation of the pub business (B.
25/2/’l5). In an unofficial way the Church, or a
section of it, has given the movement its blessing.
In England ecclesiastical approval has only got as
far as “disinterested management” by public trust
companies, three of. which have been in existence
for some years. The Archbishop of Canterbury and
a number of lesser gaiters are among the trustees,
and Earl Grey, who was one of its initiators, enthusi-
astically boosts the movement. The memorandum of
association limits dividends to a maximum of five
per cent., which is Steadily paid, and no person may
hold more than 200 shares. Surplus profits are used
to acquire new licenses, improve premises, provide
gardens and sports grounds in connection with the
pub where possible, and generally make the house
attractive. Managers are allowed no commission on
sale of alcohol, but- lare given handsome bonuses on
tea, coffee and other non-intoxicants. The trust
companies, combining as they do a moderately good
investment with a moral aim as a salve to the
conscience of the pious investor, are going ahead
rapidly—or were before the war broke out. One of
their troubles is that they find it hard to get hold
of pubs except in country localities; and that they
are opposed tooth and nail by the official “temper-
ance” organisations. In Australia the Anglican
denominational paper has gone farther than private
trust control of pubs, and has barracked straight
out for municipal or State ownership. Much con-
troversy has raged among the elect, but the paper’s
policy is making headway. In Melbourne L. V.
Biggs, chief of staff of the Age, carried the matter
last December before the local Anglican Synod, and-
strove to get that body to ask the Government to
insert a question at the forth-coming local-option poll
which would enable voters to express their opinion on
the matter of municipal or State control as well
as on continuance, reduction, or no-license. His attempt
failed, owing to the opposition of the “temperanee”
fanatics and their followers, who rallied to support
Bung as cheerfully as they unfailingly comfort Ikey
in the shadow of the tote. But a strong minority
of the Synod was with Biggs, and the storm which
arose in a heated debate is still raging over the tea-
cups in clerical circles. The Church usually opposes
reforms until they are adopted, and then says that
in reality it approved them all along; and
probably when Bung has been nationalised, the
Church will conveniently forget all the hard things
it said about nationalisation, and will point to the
attitude of its denominational paper (much abused at
the time) as evidence that it led the way towards
the reform.
THE IRON CROSS.
In a case just heard before the Melbourne
Divorce Court it appeared that the petitioner,
when a sailor on H.M.S. Torch, married
the lady at half-past eight at night, being
then under strict orders to be back on deck
by 12. The main reason for the hasty pro-
ceeding seems to have been that the woman
wanted a wine license, and couldn’t get it
while single. The Torch .left for sea that
night with the bridegroom aboard, and on
returning the lover found his lass had flown.
That was 14 years ago, and she had re-
mained, flown ever since. The Court said
Yes, and the long stern chase was over.
There seems to be attached to this story a
moral about the folly of marrying a woman
because she wants a wine license. It looks
almost as reckless as to marry a poor girl
because she is “lonely.”
The employees of the N.S.W. Govt, work-
shops at Walsh Island (Newcastle) have been
showing how easy it is for men really earn-
est over the matter to find an excuse for a
strike. The first trouble arose through the
authorities insisting that the men, on disem-
barking at Newcastle from the ferry, should
walk over a gang-plank instead of jumping
ashore. The men said they didn’t see why
they should have to walk a gang-plank at
one end of a journey and not at the other;
so when the ferry reached Walsh Island
one morning they refused to go ashore until
a plank was provided. As there was no
plank to provide, and the men continued to
refuse, they were taken back to Newcastle
and lost a day’s pay. Meantime the authori-
ties chased round and secured a plank; and
the men returned to work next day with a
glorious feeling that they had vindicated
the imperishable principles of gang-planks
and Unionism. But not for long. After
another week or so the men on arriving at
the island one morning discovered that a
steamer was at the wharf they usually
landed -a't; wherefore they were taken to
another wharf. Although the other wharf
was just as good looiung as their own, and
the gang-plank was quite substantial and
respectable, they nailed their colors to the
mast and themselves to the ferry—and lost
another day’s pay. It seems to be a splen-
did thing to lose a day’s pay.
Anglican Archbishop Clarke, barracking
for his side in Melbourne the other day:—
Surely after 20 centuries of Christian experience
the world knows enough of its blessings to make it
worth our while to tight for tuun. So the wasie ot
life, of treasure, is justified, because it is in the
cause of Christianity itself.
But the naughty sceptic wants to know
how it happens that, after 20 centuries of
Christian experience, the most Christian
nations are now more active than ever at
the pagan pastime of cutting one another’s
throat. When archbishops, and other lead-
ing Christians justify war on the ground
that the cause of Christianity is to be
benefited, by the wholesale slaughter of
Christians, what comment is left for the
heathen ?
THE BUSH RANGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
See the narrow gash cut through the trees,
And the riflemen prone
On the flat ledge of stone
Taking aim at their indolent ease.
Note the targets that swing at a wrench
Of unseen hands that wave
Little flags in the grave
In the gully—the newly-cut trench.
Hear the echoing shots and the whir
Of the bullets that lust
To be tearing flesh ! Dust
Spirts behind the black “bull.’’ “Hit it, sir!”
In this gully of peace that the sun
Has left for the day
They are learning to slay.
Hark ! the forest is mocking the gun.
“With the steel and the fire that you get,
With your! toil and your tears
From my heart”—-so Earth sneers —
“You must fight for me, fight for me yet !
“Loud your vomiting guns and vain boasts;
Yet the fruit of your toil
But enriches my soil,
And I swallow in silence your hosts!”
Thus the Earth, while the bullets that lust
From the markers’ trench lash,
When the long rifles crash,
A whiff cf inconsequent dust.

THE “TOP NOTCH” SCOTCH.
“RING GEORGE IV.”
WHISKY.
A UNIFORM STANDARD of QUALITY.
ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
William Goldwyer, a resident of Cottesloe
W.A.), went for a stroll on January 20, and on
February 21 woke up somewhere in the Darling
Ranges to find an inquisitive dog barking at him.
Where he had been, or what he did in the interval
he doesn’t remember; all he can faintly recall is
that he had water now and then, and all the time felt
horribly sleepy. Four years ago, in South America,
William was stung by a tsetse fly, and he has been
liable to be a pilgrim in Dreamland ever since. Before
setting out on these expeditions he suffers first from
weakness, then from loss of will power, and next
from an irresistible desire to sleep. There are the
makings of an ideal politician in him.
One of the industries that booms through
the war is the manufacture of —what do you think ?
barbed wire! Millions of miles of it have been used
for defensive work in Belgium and France, and much
jnore of it will be used for entanglements before the
war’s ended. Factories in U.S.A. are working at
tremendous pressure to supply the Allies, and sevei’al
new mills have been rushed up. But that hasn’t
kept the price down; so the peaceful person who
wants to build an ordinary fence these days uses the
stuff without spikes in it. Whereat old Poley and
Strawberry give the Kaiser three cheers.

“Torch”: “De Oriel” and “ILS.” make
me weary in well doing with their talk about midget
post-offices. Herewith my entry. The post, money-
order and telephone office at Tangambalanga (Vic.)
is so small that when you want to use the ’phone the
postmistress has first to edge herself outside to give
you room to enter. This in a township that has a
daily-mail service, two pubs, (where you can pass the
time while waiting your turn at the ’phone), two
banks, two stores, two blacksmiths’ shops, bootery,
bakery, butchery and butter-factory.

“Lagani-Namo”: The missionary at
Mekeo (Papua) caught young Aipiana ere the wicked-
ness of his ancestors had manifested itself. In due
course he was baptised and renamed Josepha (Joseph).
Some time later, the padre, journeying through the
bush, happened on a hunting party gorging on a
wild pig. As it was a Friday and most, of the
hunters were his parishioners, the pilot felt disap-
pointed and angry. Whilst the father still roundly
denounced them, Josepha leapt to his feet and con-
fronted his pastor. “What for you speak altogether
hoy bad? I bin tell you this one (indicating the
repast) he no more pig. I no lie. That, time I
small boy along mission, name belonga me Aipiana ;
by-an’-bye you puttem water on top alonga head,
then me no more Aipiana. Proper name belonga me
Josepha. I bin do ’em this feller (pig) all-a-same.
First time, true, he pig; but, I savvy no good I
kai-kai pig alonga Friday. I bin puttem plenty feller
water on top this feller, and I tellepi altogether hoy
him no more pig —him plxi.”

“Tsactuo” : Keloids or scarification marks
are most important among the dark-skinned tribes
of Papua. They are usually formed_by cutting the
skin and inserting the chewed leaf Of a-certain plant
to prevent the edges from uniting.- A daub of wet
clay is placed over all. The juice of the Sesuvium
portulacnstrum is sometimes used to produce suppur-
atiou and ensure a more
or less raised scar. In tlie
Papuan Gulf powdered
clam-shell is used for the
same purpose. The majority
of the scarifications seen
above the breasts of the
Fly River women are
made with the idea that
they prevent the breasts
becoming pendulous, and
in many tribes a woman
is not marriageable until
she is treated. Keloids
are cut among the Fly
peoples as clan badges on
the men : . also, they are in-
flicted as mourning signs
and for decorative pur-
poses. When a boy kills his
first turtle or dugong his
sister often cuts a scar on.
her arm. In the estuary
of the Fly conventional re-
presentations of centipedes
are cut on the women's
legs as charms against these crawlers. On the north
coast keloids are usually mourning marks, though
occasionally they are tallies of illicit love affairs.
“See th’ Dane”: Micky Doyle was a
young bush black-and-white artist with a tremendous
admiration for Norman Lindsay and Father Ryan,
P.P. When he “did” the Day of Judgment in the
daring Lindsay manner, he made his friend Father
Ryan the central figure, and gave him a special body-
guard of angels to keep off the clutching d —d souls.
(There were a lot of d—d, and most of them
strongly resembled unpopular residents of the dis-
trict.) But the finest touch of all was the bicycle.
Father Ryan had had his new motor-bicycle stolen
by some sacrilegious thief at the last agricultural
show, and Micky, with a fine sense of the fitness of
things, had drawn an angel holding a bike at the
foot of the central cloud-throne. An August Finger
was pointed at the machine, and on the margin of
the drawing was written in a scrawling boyish
hand, “Come ye blessed Father Ryan and take the
new bike which I have prepared for thee!”
“The Pariah”: The Victorian Cressy,
phonetically misspelt after the battle, dates back to
the days before the gold rushes. For years the large
landowner sat on its chest, and it breathed with ex-
treme difficulty. As soon as the last of the estates
was cut up the place boomed, and land in the town-
ship recently brought £5 a foot. In the <>ld days
Cressy was the 'last camp of the bullockies and team-
sters who hauled wool to Geelong from parts ’way
back. The Wooadyaloak River here has some large,
deep pools, going to 20ft., and a few trees (unique
in this part) gave shade. Sustenance, in 10-gallon
kegs, was supplied by a Frenchman named D’Auverg-
nay. His memory is kept sweet in the name of the
only pub—Frenchman’s Inn. D’Auvergnay had no
bar. He handed out the liquor through a sliding
window, and the plains resounded with the howls of
wassailing bullockies.

“Biddy B.”: The New York Evening
Journal settles any argument about-sheep-shearing
records. This way :
Tlie world's sheep-shearing record is 2394 in
nine hours.
And it isn’t likely to be broken for some time either.
The man who can do more than 44 sheep per minute
for nine hours on end is a ringer. In Australia we
don't reckon it a fair thing to try to do more than
41. And then we knock off half-way through the nine
hours for a cigarette.

“The Reefer” : Some years ago a wizened-
up morsel of humanity from Tipperary had a crowd
of niggers sluicing for gold on a Papuan field. Dis-
covering a husky Orokiva stealing, Irish Tim
attempted to chastise him. He might just as well
have tussled with an elephant. Presently this
dawned on him, and he bethought him of his big
countryman Paddy Henncssy, located in a distant
gully. Snatching a pencil and paper he wrote: —
Dere Hinnissy, —Th’ bearer of this has been
thievin’ ’n’ given me a dale of tlirouble, plese giv
him a good batin.
The offending boy was then despatched with the
epistle. He got back late the following day, very
stiff and tender in ivuts, and very chastened in
temper. Thenceforward he was the best boy in
that party. Some months elapsed. One day the
boss discovered that he was out of matches. The
store was a seven-days’ journey for a native, so
Tim, with another great effort, and not without
many blots, wrote asking Hennessy to send some
matches by bearer. Thinking it would be a pleasant
change from the sluicing, Tim called the . Orokiva
boy to carry the note. But when the boy heard
the destination, he back-pedalled, and made no bones
about what he thought of friend Hennessy. “What
for you very wild along me this time, Taubada ? I
no stealin’. I bin work very good all-e time. What
for you sendem me along plurry Paddy?”- “I’m not
wild, me bhoy. This feller letter he tellim Paddy I no
got matches ; p’raps he sendem some.” The Orokiva
stood now on one log, and again on the other,
screwing his toes in an agony of apprehension.
“Taubada [master], 1 too much fright. This time
I savvy fashion belong white man. He sendim boy
along plurry letter. Plurry letter he tellem nother
feller friend, ‘More better him makum plenty plurry
fight to boy.’ No good. Plurry Paddy ’e wild
like plurry alligator. ’Spose I go, close up I finish
[die].” “No! No! Me bhoy, 1 tell you true, this
letter he no talkem fight—-he talkem matches.”
“Well, Taubada, more better you showem me first
time.” (Tim does ■so and translates it word for
word.) Suddenly, as he secs a big blot, the boy
plants his finger on it, and interjects: “Ha! Tau-
bada ! No good you gammon me! What name thin
idler?”

“Petifi”: The waters that came down at
Ladore weren’t a circumstance compared with the
Murrumbidgee when it romped past Wagga
(N.S.W.) the other Sunday. During the previous
couple of days five or six inches of wet descended
upon the watershed higher up the river, though
only a few points fell on Wagga itself; and just
before dawn things began to happen. With a rush
and a boom a bank of water, six feet high, white
and phosphorescent in the starlight, swept down,
carrying sheep, rabbits, fences, logs, p.n.’s, hay-
stacks, mortgages, pigs, signs of wet weather, and
sundries. Fish galore struggled in the murky tur-
moil, and the populace with one spontaneous accord
went a-eatching them. The hauls made that
Sabbath morn will be lied about at great length
by the Oldest Inhabitant three-quarters of a century
to come.

“Kiltie” : The black cormorant, commonly
known as the shag or diver, is prolific on the Macleay
River (N.S.W.) flats; and nobody, except tourists,
lias taken much notice of him until recently, when
he developed a new habit. In a stretch of dead
timber he and the missus built hundreds of nests,
Old timers say they have, never before known these
fish-birds to have their villas above ground-level,
Would you kindly pass the naturalist?
“Iveeser” is quite frank about it:—
What between the clamor for more Protection and
the barks for Freetrade and the blood-curdling
stories of atrocities in Flanders published by the
daily rags hereabouts; what with the bursting of
the Queensland boom and men walking about, looking
for work, tucker and beer, my brain got over-heated
with it all. So I went to the nearest pub and
drank a,long beer—it mightha’ been more—-and aero ;
planed home to bed and dreamed that Australia had
declared war on the Emperor of Freetrade and we all
(except the Freetraders) went to the front, armed
with prickly pears and ticks, and smashed the Syd-
ney at Cocos Islands. We roamed the seas for munce
and munce and munce in Snowy Baker’s Stadium
trying to intercept the ship oil which Griffiths
Bought’em and Co. were coming out from Noo Yark,
but just as we were hoisting the N.S.W. Labor
party’s official diver overboard to look for the policy
Holman said he hadn’t lost, 1 opened my eyes. A
red-bearded face was at the window. “Wot yer
doin’ there?” I asked; “sneakin’ me tea?” “Not
on yer life; but have yer seen wot these Ger’ogs ’ave
been doin’?” and he came in and cut off both my
hands and one foot and started to read the full,
true and particular account of the war as printed in
the Bundaloo Bloodstain. That was three weeks
ago. He’s been reading it ever since, and says he
won’t give me a dropper booze till the war’s over
and Andy Fisher’s opened a pub in the Territory.

“Eddyson”: This is the story of Garran
who went on the land. Garran was a city worker
with an ambition to throw off the serfdom of
slaving for wages, and be a man on his own land.
He saved, and screwed, and married, and still
screwed and saved, while he studied theoretical
farming. Then he went with the pioneers a little
further out than The Limit, and built a house on 300
acres of Gippsland gum country, just where the
Hungry Ranges begin to swell up from the flats.
Garran declared war, and charged the massed enemy
with his axe, and one by one the gums fell before
him for a year without seeming to make any differ-
ence. They had been in occupation two years be-
fore his wife went for a walk alone. Mrs. Garran
was 28 and pretty. She was bred in Adelaide,
and had small love of the bush and its glories.
Two yards from a beaten track she was as helpless
and flurried as a sick chicken; and the hush at
Moryet was towering high and hopelessly tangled
with supplejack, and spider fern, and gross under-
growth of sapling, and bracken, and stinkweed
scrub. Great gorges ran into the range, too,
deepening as they ran, their sides set thick with
heather and slim white gums, their depths a net-
work of ferns and sickly shrubs. These gorges
had tributary ravines, dark and choked with growth.
The whole formed a maze that taxed the skill and
endurance of the hardest and craftiest of the old
bushmen. After the second day Mrs. Garran’s
chances seemed hopeless; but Garran hung on, and
Hutchinson, the surveyor, stood by him for two
days more. When the blacktrackers confessed them-
selves beaten, and the widely scattered neighbors
gave up even thinking of getting the bones of
Annie Garran, Steve Garran continued the search.
He had mapped out the locality into sections, and
he gave one day to each; and all day long he
fought his way through scrub, and fierce opposing
creepers, like some insane animal on a scent. Steve
also had abandoned hope of finding his wife alive;
but he swore to find her body or die. Six months
later hd was still hunting, and all about Moryet
and The Limit they said he was cracked, and called
him “Mad Garran.” He looked the part; gaunt
as Death and brown as a Lascar, ragged, hairy and
half-starved. One day, returning from the search,
he found a letter lying on his kitchen table, where
some passer from The Limit had left it. It bore
the Sydney postmark, and was poorly written and
very short. It ran: “You needn’t hunt for yore
wife. I saw her in a theatre here larst night
with a bloke what was your friend; Hutchinson
his name.” The letter was anonymous. Garran
gave up his search. He sold his land, and re-
turned to wage-slavery in Melbourne. Within a
year he married again, and nine weeks after that
they found the body of Annie Garran tucked well
under an over-hanging flat rock in one of the seven
ravines, where she had crept for shelter, and where
she had died, probably from snakebite, the Coroner
said. And Steve Garran, comfortable in his new
surroundings, with no ambition left to be a man
standing on his own land, wonders and wonders
who his friend and anonymous correspondent of
12 months ago was.
* * * *
“Wandi” : Among the busy nomads of the
bush the tailor does as well as any, but he has to be
as careful about his cuts and flits as his shop brother
in town. Nothing talks like a tad-fitting suit. It
condemns its maker wherever it goes. Usually the
needle and scissors expert travels in a tilted cart, in
which he carries a few rolls of tweeds and serges,
lhere is not much variety, but then there is very
little variation in the-dress affected by the workers
where he moves. Stockmen and drovers mostly adopt
one style, and, to a large extent, other groups do the
same thing'. Therefore, the tailor, when he has been
round once or twice, knows exactly what is required.
He stops wherever he gets an order. Often he finds
a week or two s work from the manager of the run
or the manager’s wife. At such times he is saved the
trouble of cooking for himself, for he has his meals
with the station-hands. At shearing sheds and mus-
tering camps, also, he enjoys this privilege. He in-
cludes dyeing and pressing in his operations, especi-
ally if he is a married man, and has his wife travel-
ling with him. In this case they take in mending,
and that is a big lift. Prompt cash is not always
necessary, for when the wandering firm works round
periodically payment is collected from reliable cus-
tomers on the next or succeeding trip. As the round
may occupy at least six months, accounts are often
outstanding for a year or more. For convenience,
when the firm has plenty of work to do, a temporary
workshop is rigged up with a tent fly. Under this
the sewing-machine, standing on the grass, is worked
early and late. The Factories Act does not intrude.
The man generally works in the vehicle, though in
calm, warm weather he sometimes sits on the ground
in the shade of a tree. Providing they haven’t chil-
dren to carry with them and care for, it is not an
unpleasant life for either man or missus—except in
wet weather, when cooking and travelling have often
to be done under the most disagreeable circumstances.
Not all the members of this trade are equipped with
vehicles and machines. A number tramp the roads
with their worldly possessions slung on their backs.
I heir main work is strapping and repairing, of which
they get a good deal to do at the sheds and on small
mining fields. Now and again they make a suit of
clothes, when the material is provided. They also
make oilskins and fur caps, and when no other work
is obtainable they turn their hands to the stitching
of waterbags.
“Bill Bowyang” : Coming back to the big
trees, I have seen a log of cedar 25ft. in girth.
Ten years ago a blue-gum was felled at Little
•lilliby, Wyrong, near Newcastle, which yielded
265 railway sleepers, each measuring 7ft. 6in. x
Din. The ironbark of Queensland and N.S.W. also
grows to a great size, and the black-butt of the
same States has furnished specimens 300 ft. high and
45ft. in circumference. The red-gum of South Aus.
furnished a specimen, grown at Mount Lofty, over
200 ft. high by 25ft. in girth. A tree at Mount
Bavv Baw (Vic.) was 326 ft. high and 25ft. Tin. in
girth, Another in the Otway Ranges, the top
broken off, was 290 ft. high and 32ft. in girth.
Another of the same species was 219 ft. 9in. in height
and of- the Reidian circumference of 48ft. 6in. All
the girths were taken 6ft. from the ground. The
crowning specimen was found stretched on the
ground in the Berwick Ranges: it was 300 ft. from
tip to toe, and 81ft. round the waistcoat.
WHERE OLD BILL TUCKER SLEEPS}.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There isn’t any sculptured dome,
Nor any ornate railing round
Old Billy Tucker’s final home,
But he will slumber just as sound ;
He never had a taste for swank ;
He loved the primal heights and deeps—
And there grow sun-dews on the bank
Where old Bill Tucker sleeps.
His name is on a wooden post;
He wouldn’t wish it otherwise.
No marble slab his deeds shall boast,
Nor granite glow with gilded lies.
He held himself as of the crop
A mystic Nature sows and reaps f ~
And Rest has settled down, to stop
Where old Bill Tucker sleeps.
His view of life was plain enough—
To always take the open track ;
To lepd a hand when times were rough ;
To crack a joke when skies were black.
His scorn was for the renegade,
The paltry soul that schemes and creeps.
No wonder Peace should haunt the shade
Where old Bill Tucker sleeps.
But we have got his epitaph
Engraved, within, upon our hearts,
His cheery words, his kindly laugh,
His wit to turn the word that smarts.
And may our slumber be as sound,
When Death has got us all for keeps,
As that beneath the grass-grown mound
Where old Bill Tucker sleeps!
VICTORIA.
C. G. A. COLLES.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
A C. Thompson and Frank Thompson,
brothers of the sitting M.L. A. for Ityde
(N.KW.) and sons of .1. B. Thompson, who
sat for Queanbeyan in 1877-81, have re-
ceived commissions in the Bth Bo.val Dublin
Fusiliers. The Thompsons have been for
three-quarters of a century connected with
Bathurst. The original Thompson was first
postmaster at that town, and gave the post
over to his son. A\ . G. Thompson. Between
them the two held the office for something
like 70 years.
Ilarvey Finlayson. killed in a motor-
smash in Melbourne last week, was the only
son of J. 11. Finlayson, formerly editor of
the Adelaide Register, and now lingering
on in S. Aus. in broken-down health. The
junior had a varied career, going through
the Boer war when very young, then acting
as vet. on the Aeneas Gunns’ Arafura station
ia the N.T., and ending as a motor agent in
Adelaide. He was on a brief visit to Mel-
bourne. and was driving his own car when
it skidded and upset.
A Melbourne paper, an effusive admirer
of the High Commissioner, quotes a London
post-prandial orator to the effect that George
Reid is "‘a king among men.” King of
Grease, presumably.
James Westley, Melbourne solicitor, is
dead, aged GG. The theatrical profession
found in him a stout friend, and often he
espoused successfully the side of the per-
former against the management. Mr.
Westley acted as agent for French, the
printer of plays, and brought many a per-
spiring amateur up with a legal jolt in the
eye for author’s fees. His daughter, Mrs.
Davey, was Mayoress of Melbourne before
the arrival of the Ilennessys.
From “The Oldest Inhabitant”: —
The first white child horn in Port Phillip was William
James Hobart Thorne, who arrived November 25,
1803, at the Collins Camp, just inside Port Phillip
Heads. On Christmas Day, underneath the gum-
trees overlooking what is now Sorrento, after
Chaplain Robert Knopwood’s sermon, Lieutenant-
Governor Collins handed the little son of the Sergeant
of Marines to the parson and stood god-father. In six
weeks time the whole settlement —excepting William
Buckley and another, who had decamped—removed to
the Derwent, where Collins built a city. The Thorne
child was named after Lord Hobart, Secretary of
State.
The last- survivor of the Birkenhead,
wrecked on February 2G, 3852, is dead. He
was a corporal, name o’ Smith, and during
tile historic catastrophe saved the life of
Captain Lucas. Now, G 8 years after, Smith
goes to prospect the place lie formerly
dodged. I.ike many of his gallant old tribe,
he died in a workhouse. If he’d only stayed
away from the Birkenhead and written a
Poem about what took place, Smith would
have made a better finish.
Dead at SS, Frank Bullen. who wrote
“The Cruise of the Cachelot” and other
stories. Early life was spent as seaman
before the mast in whaling craft and other
vessels in the Southern Seas. In later years
he had the post of a minor clerk at the
meteorological office in London, kept a small
shop for a time with his wife, and was
generally wholly miserable and hard up. A
lecturing tour of Australia eight or nine
years ago failed, and Bullen returned to
England in deeper gloom than ever. Then
cirrhosis of the liver gripped him, and the
last watches of the big-hearted seaman were
those of a hopeless invalid.
BILL’S NEW ALLY.
Dr. .1. P. Kennedy, formerly of Levin
(M.L.), who died in London recently, had
unique experiences of the old boom-and-burst
days in that distressful country. Lie started
to earn a crust in the M.L. Treasury in
Vogel’s time, and became private secretary
to that exnensive juggler. Presently the
wild-cat Midland .Railway between Christ-
church and Westland was born, and Vogel
gave Ivennedv the job of secretary to the
big co. behind the project. But building
the railway through the Alps proved too big
a contract for it and it died. That was
80 years ago, and the Government has been
struggling to finish the line ever since.
Kennedy eventually went to Edinburgh and
studied medicine, returning to practice in
Maoriland. A while ago something fatal
gripped him and he hurried Fogwards. to
consult a specialist. But Death refused to
let go.
Cecil L. Ilartt, whose black and white
work is familiar to Bulletin readers, has
decided to draw the German’s blood instead
of his picture. The man he hopes to serve
under is a Light Horse lieutenant who has
written many an interesting par. for the
“Aboriginalities” pages.

The death of the German Crown Prince is
again reported.
Who for his Fatherland has died
Is as a hero glorified.
How shall they ring his name in rhymes,
Who’s died for home a dozen times!
The McKinnon Pass on the famous Mil-
ford Track in Maoriland was crossed the
other week by a six-year-old tourist named
Horn. A kid of that age who takes on a
60-mile stroll over a rough track with a
mountain pass thrown in is a fairly tough
Maoriland sample.
The first white child born in Westralia
was a daughter of Lieutenant J. S. Roe.
Surveyor-General, who immigrated on
Christmas Day, J Slil). The baby afterwards
became Mrs. S, P. Phillips.- Her parents
arrived on .Tune 8. 1821), in the ship Par-
melia, which brought the first Governor,
James Stirling, and his family, with officials
and intending settlers to the number of 69.
The Rev. J. B. Wittenoom was the first
chaplain to the settlement. Roes and Wit-
tenooms are still persons of consequence there.
Mrs. Adrian Porter,-daughter of the late
Ilenniker Heaton, announces in the London
papers that she is editing a life, of her late
papa, and appeals to his friends for letters
and personal reminiscences. It may be a
bit difficult to reconstruct the .pushful Hen-
uiker’s early Australian career in accord-
ance with latter-day requirements. There
are, for instance, people still living in many
townships of N. 8. Wales who would think
it no harm to say that Heaton was travel-
ling canvasser for the Town and Country
Journal; but that wouldn’t: suit the sub-
sequently-aristocratic Henniker’s earnest de-
sire not to have his baroneted name associ-
ated with such a plebeian pursuit. After
marrying into the boss’s family, his idea
was to get into the House of Commons as
M.P. for an Irish Nationalist constituency.
His wife and Mrs. Dwyer Gray, wife of a
leading Irish M.P., who was proprietor of the
Dublin Freeman’s Journal, had been old
friends in Sydney, and he thought he could
work it that way. He wrote a beautifully
illustrated book about Rome, and presented
copies of it to all the Irish R.C. bishops.
He also subscribed liberally to the National-
ist funds in Dublin. All in vain! There
was too much of the Sassenach and too
little of the brogue to suit any Irish con-
stituency. So he transferred his subscrip-
tions to the campaign fund of the Carlton
Club, and got a nomination for Canterbury.
The sleepy old cathedral city had recently
come in for some handsome legacies from
the wealthy Dr. Beaney, of Melbourne, a
native of the place; so Canterbury was
favorably disposed towards an Australian
candidate, and Ilenniker got in. He was a
wretched speaker; but he had a good deal
of ohl Sam Bennett’s money to play with,
and he showed considerable shrewdness in
specialising on postal reforms —a fertile but
previously neglected field, which he worked
with excellent results for the public and
much kudos for himself. He made some
enemies, among them old Saul Samuel, long-
time Agent-General for N. S. Wales in Lon-
don, who detested him. According to the
gospel of Saul, Henniker’s first job was in a
Melbourne livery stable. He was plain
John in those days; afterwards—well, any-
one who knows of the picturesque vocabu-
lary of Saul of Sydney can guess what his
enemy was afterwards.
The Town Clerk of Melbourne. John Clay-
ton, prepares to retire. Though incorporated
nearly three-quarters of a century ago. the
city has had only four clerks. The first,
John Charles King, an Irishman from Ulster,
held office until 1851, when he was sent to
London as an anti-transportation delegate,
and did good work. On his return he
entered the Assembly, subsequently joined
the Nicholson Ministry as Commissioner for
Works, and died within a month. The next
clerk was William Kerr, founder of the
Argus. He was a man of great general
ability, but in finance a dreadful muddler,
llis capacity in this respect forced him to
sell the paper, and at a subsequent date
(1856) to resign the municipal billet. 11 is
successor, Edmund (Jerald Fitzgibbon. found
a wilderness in the way of books and accounts
to set in order. Kerr was always in deadly
opposition to John O’Sharassy; yet when
hard times came Big John made him railway-
station master at Sunbury. Fitzgibbon
ruled for 35 years, and retired only to take
the chairmanship of the Metropolitan Board
of Works. “Fitz” had studied for the Bar.
and was admitted ; but he never held a brief,
excepting for the corporation, lie married
a daughter of tin' wealthy Micky Dawson,
of Phoenix Park. Brunswick. John Clayton
followed him in 1891. but has been all
through overshadowed by tin* memory of the
masterful man In' succeeded.
“Jules”: None of the papers seem to
appreciate the feat of Captain Oswald Watt
in winning the Cross of the Legion of Honor
on the battlefield. After the Medaille
Militaire. which the President of France
conferred on Joffrc the other day. it is about
the highest military decoration the Republic
bestows. The achievement of the Civil
Cross is, of course, an easier matter. Cap-
tain Watt, by the way. has behaved remark-
ably in other directions. lit' is one of the
few Australian peace-time soldiers who have
gone to the Front, and the only one who has
been there from the beginning. Also lie
is the only ex-AJI.C. who has done any-
thing notable so far, except get killed or
wounded. Other Australians who have dis-
tinguished themselves are Sydney Pickles,
the famous airman, who was half-killed last
year; and Captain Bex Osborne, of the big
N.S.W. station-owning family.

THE INVALID.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the song service held in the Y.M.C.A. marquee
on Sunday evening, Mr. E. E. Wright, field officer
at the Kxpeditionaries’ training camp at Broad-
meadows, launched a “clean lip” campaign.... with
the following brief pledge: “I promise always to
endeavor to use only clean language, and to influence
my comrades to do the same!”
The pale young man he comes to me,
An’ chats me good an’ fait';
“The langwidge that you use,” ses he,
“Pollutes the good, clean air.
Why don't yeh chuck sich silly rot.
An’ line-up with the Clean Lip lot ?”
But, square ’n’ all, I got no use
For them poor, shrinkin’ guys,
Who, at the sound of coves’ abuse,
Turn pale, an’ rolls their eyes.
To use the fancy swears I hear
Conies natural as sinking beer.
Beef an’ blood-gr:. ;y’s light in' food,
Not milk; but, all the same,
I (ome to see there ain’t no good
In this erook-langwidge game.
An’ so a little vow I made.
An’ joined their swell Clean Lip Brigade.
’Twas ’ard! But sternly I pursoo'd
Me course; an’ wore a frown
Thro’ swallerin’ me speech unchewed,
An’ chokin’ curse-words down.
Oh, dear! It was a dreadful stunt,!
Then, gracious me, I hit the Front!
A feller in the firin’ line,
Tied up with sich a gag,
Who has to curse by look an' sign,
He fair gets out the rag.
An’ so, 1 ses, each time I shoots,
“I'll teach you, you—you —-you —you brootsl”
I don't care wot the goodies say,
It’s cruel, fightin’ dumb!
To curse a bit, once in a way,
Believes ver feelin's some.
I kills four men in fair, clean fight,
An’ seven extra out er spite.
All’ then there come the bay’nit charge.
The blokes to left an’ right
They all was cursin’ fine and large,
But I keep mum, an' fight.
I plunks a square-’ead in the wind—-
“Annoying fellow; there!” 1 grinned.
With that a great, big ’ulkin' chap,
Comes at me with a sword
(The thing / needed in that scrap
Was just one little word).
“Haw! You—you person,” I begun;
But, while I talks, he gets in one.
Fair in the chest I gets that swipe,
An’ crumbles in a heap,
An’ starts to think the time is ripe
To ’avc, a long, deep sleep.
“You are intensely rude,” I said—•
An’ so they leaves me there for dead.
They invaierds me ’ome, although
The wound gives me no cares.
The cause of my complaint, I know,
Was bottlin’ up me swears—-
Congestion of me “Damns” denied :
It made me feel all swelled inside.
The pale young man lie comes to me.
“Ah, friend,” he says, “how now?
Your lips are clean, I’m pleased to see,
An’ you ’ave ken’ ver vow!”
“Me lips is bonzer,” I replied,
“But, ’strutli, me throat is scarified!”

The co.’s interest on investments amounted
to f 10,O7(i: so all the underwriting profit and
a bit besides was put to reserve, where of
course it will be available for payment of
claims. A co. which is as moderate as that
in its own demands deserves the good opinion
of people with fire and marine policies to
place.
On the other hand, The Bulletin is quite
prepared to admit that a co. which has to bite
something out of the underwriting profits in
order to pay a 13 p.e. div., might be quite as
worthy of three cheers. The mean assets of
the co. for last year totalled £234,000. De-
duct £35,000 for non-interest earning assets
and you get £199,000, which brought in
£10,070, = a shade over 5.0. c. all round. Of
the total £115,965 is tied down to mortgages.
£58.719 is in unspecified debentures, and
£17,318 is in offices and other property. Of
the investments that leaves only about £9OOO
in deposits. Admittedly an insurance office
can more safely lend its money on fixed
mortgages than a bank can lend deposit money
on the same security. In this case, for in-
stance, besides the £9OOO of deposits there is
about £35,000 of agents’, .balances and till
money. During the first half of the year
about £40,000 net of premiums will also come
in. Thus by that time it will have £75,000
available; and if the co. is struck
by June 30 with £75,000 of claims
it will have dreadful luck. But, all
the same, an insurance co. is liable
to have dreadful luck ; wherefore the tying-up
of so much money in mortgages and deben-
tures strikes this paper as a bit too risky.
It is quite true that it is Maoriland’s habitual
practice; and it is quite true also that Maori-
land sheep and farm country is better security,
as a rule, than Australian, because the Maori-
land security can be relied upon to at least
earn the interest, whereas the Australian
security may be struck by drought which will
not only knock out all hope of profit that
year, but by wiping out the stock will affect
the profit for years to come. Still that
doesn’t make the Maoriland mortgage
liquid, and it is the non-liquid nature of
security that is liable to cause trouble to
cos. which must pay out large sums quickly.
Nevertheless, as suggested just now, this
applies less to the Standard than to many
other M.L. institutions.
Further Standard comparisons:—

The loss rate was stiff; and that may be
accepted as the result, direct and indirect,
of the war, helped by the Australian drought,
which has added, as usual, to the inflamma-
bility of many risks. Considering the aver-
age loss rate, the expense rate is by no
means low. It is, however, rather astonish-
ing to find that it actually fell last year in
the face of a substantially-increased premium
income. Maybe the co., which deals largely
in marine lines, took a considerable amount
of war risk. If it didn’t, and if the increase
was ordinary business, then the co. did sur-
prisingly well to get it without paying a lot
for it. As for the future, the interest earn-
ings are likely to be larger rather than
smaller; though losses may be a bit heavier
than usual this year. Altogether, assuming
that nothing happens to require swift reali-
sation of securities, the war, so far as can
at present be foreseen, will not do the co.
enough damage to call for a crutch or even
a piece of sticking-plaster.

Jan.’ ’15..35,692 8 .. 16,000 19.692 152,630
"The machinery and plant have been main-
tained in a high state of efficiency, and all
expenditure in connection therewith has
Deep defrayed out of current revenue as
heretofore.” The “maintenance” has been
something more than is generally meant by
the word: improvements and additions have
been frequently included. Probably the
plant is thus worth more than it was six
months ago; nevertheless, ostensibly to pro-
vide for depreciation, £5OOO has been writ-
ten off profits. Even with that deduction
and a similar one in the preceding term,
enough profit was shown in the past 12
months to pay a dividend of just on 16 p.c.
Vo doubt this result was helped by the in-
creased prices recently charged to the
publicans, and. by them passed on to the
public. But that help couldn’t have been
of much consequence, since the charge for
calling up spirits wasn’t increased until
three weeks before these accounts closed.
Running back over the past few fat years
these figures are seen :
•Jan., July, Jan.,
1910. 1911. 1915.

Breweries, hotels and plant 939.588 413,175 416,953
Stocks 54,611 78,321 72,310

charges . . . . . . 33,724 53,696 66,121
Total assets less liabilities five years ago
were £467,589. To-day they are £596,131.
Vo capital has been got in during the period.
Thus Tooheys, Ltd., has paid its dividend,
paid for “maintenance” as above mentioned,
and in five years has added £128,542 to its
net assets. That is something over £25,000
a year, or enough to- pay an additional 6 p.c.
1 tow much of that was surplus net profit
depends, of course, upon the depreciation of
properties not covered by renewals, etc.; but
the chances are that, as a lot of value was
added which never went on to the balance-
sheet, there isn’t much to deduct from that
6 p.c.
That covers five very profitable years.
The next, five are not so certain; the five
beyond that are more uncertain still.’ True,
though always under fire, the trade is as
prosperous as ever. A few beer-pumps here
and ihere are silenced; and immediately, as
a rule, their business strolls along to another.
Thus the brewery and wholesale man haven’t
suffered: on the contrary, they seem to
flourish on what was intended to poison them.
But whether the future developments will be
so innocuous remains to be seen. In Britain,
since the war. hours of business for pubs
have been cut into hard: and S. Aus. is just
trving to follow suit. That will be bad for
Bung and the brewer, too. Meantime, how-
ever, it is fairly clear that the general public
isn’t disposed to commit burglary on Bung’s
premises. If, as a necessary preliminary to
State ownership, it decides to gradually clear
Bung out, it is pretty certain that Bung
will be paid to go, as in Victoria —even
though, indirectly, he has to provide the
purchase price himself.

The co. used to be in £lO shares, which
originally were divided up among the three
firms that were amalgamated. During the
past term the £lO shares were split up into
ten, and 20,000 6 p.c. cumulative pref. shares
were issued at par. The gross profits in-
clude sums added to two depreciation
reserves. The past half-year’s history in that
respect has been as follows: —
June, ’l4. Dec., ’l4. Inc,
