Share premiums . . 6,800 6,800
Insurance and General . . 34,934 36,256 1322
Unappropriated .. ... 9,590 10,214 624
Total.. £153,676 £162,622 £8946
So £7OOO of the profit has been transferred
to the Depreciation Fund. Now, the
steamers, tugs, city freehold, engineering
works and even the furniture are taken in at
cost; they represent £391,860 of the total
assets of £462,269. Out of revenue the co.
pays for repairs and renewals; it may pos-
sibly pay also out of revenue for some im-
provements made in the coitrse of renewals.
But this Depreciation Fund apparently has
to provide for the replacement of ships as
they become out of date. How much ought to
be thus put aside to provide for wear and
tear which isn’t covered by patches it is
impossible to,„say. But- if £7OOO was a
fail’ thing to write off, then the true profit
last half-year was £1946 more than the
dividends called for: and it has to be noted
that, though div. was paid on the pref.
capital for a few weeks, not only that money
hut a good deal besides was represented by
a new vessel which couldn’t have earned
anything to speak of.
A five years’ survey may yield some-
thing more definite: —
Dec.. 'O9. June, ’l4: Dec.. 14.
Steamships and other
non-liquid tisets .. £231,382 £347,352 £401,416
Liquid assets . . 37,003 62,379 60,853
Liabilities .. .. 19,255 44,416 63,830
In five years lint co. has spent £170,034 on
steamships, etc., and lias changed a liquid
surplus of £18.348 into a deficit of £2977 :
and meantime it has got in £36.853 of capi-
tal and £6BOO of premiums. The total of
those four items is £84.978. In other words,
the co. has got £84,978 from outside, and
has had £170,034 to spend: so, in addition
to a small amount written off, perhaps for
depreciation of stores, the co. has in five
years earned (1) its dividends; (2) what-
ever it has spent on repairs and renewals;
and (3) £85,050 which has been available
for replacement of worn-out ships, etc.
That £85,050 is at least 50 p.c. of the book
value of the ships as at Dec., 1909; and as
that is more than enough to cover deprecia-
tion, it can be said very confidently that
the dividends have been more than earned.

After you have absorbed the table The
Bulletin is bound to say that it isn’t sure
the meal will do you much good. This may
suggest why;—
Dec.,'o4. Dec.,’l3. June,’l4. Dec.,’l4.
Liquid assets . . £42,267 £119,07.3 £92,883 £78,970
Liabilities . . 31,188 25,495 65,980 60,325
Surplus ..£11,079 £93,578 £20,903 £18,045
In 12 months the co. has sopped up about
£40,000 of its liquid assets and added nearly
£35,000 to its liabilities; which really means
that it lias put over £75,000 into the busi-
ness. Much if not most of it has gone
into improved facilities for cold storage, in-
cluding the purchase of the freehold of the
co.’s principal iceberg factory. It does not
follow, because a co. could make a good
showing with £116,284 in “freeholds, lease-
holds, plant, property, etc.,” as at June, 1013,
that it should do equally well now when
the corresponding entry stands at £204,426.
Still the co. hasn’t thrown up the business
which has been so profitable in the past.
The little comparison printed just now
shows that in the nine years between 1904
and 1916 the co. added £82,499 to its surplus
of liquid assets. It not only paid its usually
handsome dividends and. added considerably
to its plant, but it also put by in cash and
liquid assets an average of over £9OOO a
year—a result far better than you would
expect on looking at the first table. The
new purchases are not likely to reduce the
profit, unless (as is not impossible) the
management makes the fatal mistake of now
neglecting the small things from which the
old profits were extracted.

The last is one of the best half-years the co.
has had; which looks as though some body
might chip in with a good word for the
Kaiser. Somehow patriotism and beer react on
one another. You shout for the Empire and
then you w r ant to shout for yourself; and
after you have shouted twice for yourself
(and a friend, for the sake of appearances)
you will shout for the world. Also there
has been the indirect result of William’s
outbreak: something has been added to
Customs duties, and the man who drinks
German lager is a person bad enough to
kick his saintly grandmother to death and
dance on her grave. So “the local trade
has been exceedingly good, and that with
the mainland show's a satisfactory increase.
The newly-imposed Customs duties should
tend to promote a still larger trade with
the Australian continent.” Hooray !
The co. can do with the extra profit; for,
on paper, its position hasn’t been satis-
factory :

Capital (w’hich was written down some
years ago) is £lOB,OOO. Liabilities total
£110,441. Thus the co. has more of other
people’s capital than it has of its own risked
in the business, excluding the reserves. And
no less than £184,195 is freehold brewery
and hotel and other property—more than
five times as much as there is in stock and
plant. The trouble is the co. has a big area
of land which is bringing in little or nothing:
and though some day it may be sold, in small
blocks at a profit, the attempts to get it off
haven’t so far been very encouraging. The
drain of interest on borrowed capital repre-
sented by this dead property makes the
brewery part of the business look less profit-
able than it really is. In addition to the
estates the co. lias a pretty big holding of
hotels: mid it is doubtful whether a proper
amount is written off to cover the deprecia-
tion of these premises. This is another way
of saying that it isn’t safe to assume that
the profits are really a farthing more than
the published accounts allege.

But that, last lino, though it follows the
co.’s own figures, hardly does the co. .jus-

Sydney plant to the new works up the Lane
Cove River was charged against a revenue

allowances are made for the actual cost of
removal and the temporary higher costs,
etc., due to the change, there was a bad
shrinkage in profits. The directors admit
that the war treated them badly; but the
State Governments “are energetically
pushing ahead with very extensive water-
supply schemes, necessitating the use of
immense quantities of piping, and the direc-
tors anticipate that wood pipes will be
utilised to a very large extent.” Certainly
it is a fact that, by the grace of the British
Government and with the assistance of the
Commonwealth Government and the banks,
there will still be loan money to splash up
for 12 months or so; after that —what? It
can't go on. The construction boom is
simply being flogged to keep it going a little
while longer. The future is therefore likely
to hold some lean seasons for this co. For-
tunately its capital is small —£70,000 —and
a modest turnover would yield it bread and
butter. Liabilities on Dec. 31 were £23,423:
but on the other side stocks and book debts
alone amounted to £66,553. so the financial
position isn’t uncomfortable.

Happy co. this! Six months ago it neglected
to credit itself with a casual £31,468 “value
of products produced prior to June 30,
1014”; also it credited itself twice over with
£10,807 refunded by the N.S.W. Commis-
sioners of Taxation. In the accounts now
published the past half-year’s profit is
swollen by the consequent £20,661; but in
the foregoing table the amount is credited
to the previous term. Probably some of it
should belong to a still more previous term ;
but at any rate none of it rightly belongs to
the period just past. The errors and ad-
justments illustrate what The Bulletin
has said many times—that in concerns of
this sort the figures are at best approxima-
tions. For instance, on any given date this
co. under normal conditions has probably
20,000 tons of ore more or less on the way to
the commercial-product stage. Some of it
is only partly broken down, some is broken
underground, some is in reserve at the sur-
face, some is sauntering through the mill.
What amount the co. credits itself with in
respect of this stuff is largely a matter of
policy or liver. And then there is the un-
realised commercial product. This co., for
instance, has a quantity of slimes. A while
ago slimes, though they are rich in metals,
were more of a nuisance than an asset. Then
someone discovered something, and Owen.
Mcßride and others discovered other things
and slimes are probably worth £1 per ton
to-day. In the present case, the markets
having been closed, it was necessary
to include as an asset, this half-year,
the concentrates, etc., which have been piled
up; and that couldn't very well be done
without debiting this half-year with the
quantity brought forward from the previous
half. Hence, n<> doubt, the discovery of the
error, which probably isn’t quite so much
of an error as it is made out to be.
Because there are more estimates even
than usual the present accounts are worth
even less than usual. Contracts for the sale
uf the lead concentrates were suspended
automatically by tin l declaration of war.
From that date the North has worked only
half-time, its output going to the B. 11.
Prop.’s furnaces at Port Pirie instead of t<*
German buyers. But products have banked
up: it is only recentlv that much of a
market was found for lead. Consequently,
whereas in the accounts published six months
ago no product is shown as being on hand,
there are now concentrates, refined metal
and bullion valued at £121.810 after pro-
viding £31.312 for smelting costs and reali-

sation charges, together with another £6OOO
for slimes under contract of sale. Obviously
till the concentrates, refined metal and bul-
lion are sold and settled for nobody can say
exactly what they will fetch ; and the varia-
tions are liable to be very great in such
markets as those for lead and spelter. Iso
hint is given as to the prices which the
metals are assumed to be worth. It is pos-
sible, however, that a fairly liberal value has
been assumed, seeing that £lO,OOO has been
formally passed to a Contingency Fund,
making it £50.000 altogether. The main ob-
ject of this fund is no doubt to protect the
estimates, so that the co. may be able to
write off something in case it has taken
credit for too much. Incidentally, the
£lO,OOO put to the Contingency Fund and
another £lO,OOO credited to Depreciation
and General Purposes Reserve are both in-
cluded in the table as profits. Nothing else
appears to have been written off to cover
wear and tear. The half-year’s profit of
£56,216 is therefore an over-statement, even
provided that only fair values have been
taken for silver, lead and zinc. Also the re-
serves are not true reserves. They very
rarely are in the case of a mining co.
Making the adiustments necessary owing
to fhe errors previously mentioned, the posi-
tion as to surplus of liquid and semi-liquid
assets has of late been: —
' Dee., 1913 £208,460
June, 1914.. 231,886
Dec., 1914 274,803
That shows a gain of £42,917 for the past
six months. The difference between that
and the nominal profit shown in the first
table is made up almost wholly of money
spent on the mine and charged to capital
account (£1738), ditto spent on plant
(£5350), and slimes under contract of sale
(£6000). Assuming that the valuation of
ihe products on hand turns out just right,
the £42,917 is much nearer the true
profit for the half-year than £56,216
is. Considering the fact that costs have
necessarily gone up on account of the inter-
mittent work, that isn’t so bad either. As
102,735 tons of ore were milled, it means
nearly 9s. per ton; and as manager Weir
estimates that he has 3,000,000 tons in
reserve above the 1400 ft. level, that would
give £1,350,000 =£2 ss. per share, plus the
9s per share of surplus liquid assets. In
the past seven years tlie B. 11. South has
paid divs. equal to 10s. per ton; and tlie
B. H. North can do as well. As the mine
certainly doesn’t peter out at 1400 ft., where
the lode is as much as 134 ft. wide, the pre-
sent market of £2 4s. per share isn’t extra-
vagant.
Finally: “The negotiations for the pur-
chase of 'the Port Pirie smelting works have
been advanced sufficiently to enable the re-
presentatives of the B. H. Prop., B. IT.
South and North B. 11. to announce that
a mutually acceptable basis has been found
for the completion of the transaction, sub-
ject to agreement of details which are now
being considered. The directors anticipate
that this important matter will be finalised
at an early date.” This of course refers to
the proposal to form a co. to take over the
B. H. Prop, outfit at Pirie.

A figure which wasn’t as plain as it should
have been led to a miscalculation in con-
nection with the South Broken Hill (B.
4/3/T5). The ore in reserve, 3,350,000 tons,
was taken as a million tons less, which
meant a difference of just on half a million
in the .probable profits, equal to nearly
£2 10s. per share—taking the past seven
.(•ears’ experience as a guide. On the
2.350,000-ton basis the probable profit
worked out at something over £B, including
the cash and other assets in hand. The extra
tonnage brings the figure up to £lO 10s. —
always on the assumption that the past
seven years’ experience as to values, recov-
eries and prices will be averaged in the
future.
* * * *
The Victorian Government has perhaps
better facilities than any other State for
sounding the money market': Bail lieu, for
one thing, is a Minister. It is significant,
therefore, that, instead of waiting till the
beginning of next year to arrange the re-
newal of the li-niiilion loan falling due in
April, 1916, the Government invites holders
to act now, and pocket an extra ? p.c. this
year. There should be no difficulty about it.
Apart from the fact that Victoria has been,
comparatively speaking, only a moderate fre-
quenter of the pawnshop, investors begin to
realise the benefits of putting their own or
trust moneys into security which is free
from State income tax. Just how rapacious
the State income-tax collector can be, and
promises to be for the next few years, may be
judged from the fact that in N. S. Wales
the Commissioners are making demands for
two years’ tax on insurance reversionary
bonuses. They are demanding, in other
words, a cash payment on account of a debt
that isn’t collectable for perhaps 30 years.

From George Blakemore: The Bulletin
(4/3/T5) asks what I would put in the
place of the labor conditions attached to
mining leases. In crude outline, this: —
1. A modification of the United States mining- law
applying to tenure of mining- properties, the prin-
cipal modification being that the title shall be lease-
hold, forfeitable on non-payment of rent.
2. Each leaseholder to spend in improvements not
less than 2s. 6d. per acre each six mouths for a
period of four years. Non-fulfilment of the improve-
ment clause to be met by cancellation.
3. At the end of four years the rent not to exceed
lg, per acre for the first 80 acres and not more than
6d. per acre for all additional contiguous acres held
by the same person or company.
4. The rent to be assessed by the Warden at the
time of the issuance of the lease, the assessment to
be based on reports of the officers of the Mines
Department. The Warden to have the power to
defer rent charges for any part of any 12 months.
5. To make up for the loss of revenue from
clauses 3 and 4, a tax of i p.c. to be imposed on
the net profits made by every person or company
extracting any class of commercial metal or mineral
from the earth, this to include all stone quarries.
6. An Advances to Mines Board to be formed and
endowed with £250,000 at least per annum (which
sum must be spent) to take over the present Pros-
pecting Vote and to make advances to mines in the
early stages of their development, such advances to
be made on reports and recommendations from the
Mining or Geological Departments. Advances to
be a first charge up to 50 p.c. of the net profits if so
ordered by the Board.
7. Simplify the present methods of pegging and
applying for leases on Crown or private property.
8. Give tlie miner the same rights in law as the
ordinary citizen has.
Summed up these proposals mean security
of tenure except by the miner’s own act,
protection from attacks of the secret in-
former and hlackmaUer, no rent if the show
is poor and struggling, a tax on the net pro-
fits of mines which can afford to pay, and a
wise monetary assistance to promising
mines in the early days of . their
career. If these ideas were adopted in
a modern Mining Act, it is safe to pro-
phesy a restoration of the mining industry,
and in the revival, labor must benefit. The
present; laws are a garotte to the industry.

The Southern Cross battery at Ardlethan
has put through 150 tons for Coot’s Bygoo,
bulking 2 p.c., and 100 tons for the Outcast
tributors. bulking 3 p.c. There is no cham-
pagne in that. The White Crystal, which
hasn’t power to run the whole mill, has been
putting through the seconds—pretty good
proof that there are no firsts. The Car-
pathid lias had very fair developments, but
costs are, as hitherto, very high. Of the
others Ardlethan Options and the Champion
(for which a l-192nd share was sold in
Temora in the boom time for £600) have a
couple of men apiece. And that’s about all
there is left of Ardlethau’s glory.
* t * *
Y Water, the Melbourne-owned show with
the best modern dividend record in the New
England group, looks like having a new
life. Lately, on all the information avail-
able, it was a question whether it was worth
while going on. It would just pay with the
tin market normal, but the co. would have
to depend on abnormal prices for profits.
So far something like 3,000,000 yards have
been taken out, averaging about l jlb.; and
the plant called for complete reorganisation
to do good work in the future. Unfortunately
on the old figures the unworked side was
reckoned to be worth three-quarters of a lb.
at most, and it was doubtful wliethei that
was worth the risk. Recently, as the side of
the property hitherto attended to will be
worked out by the end of the year, il was
judged a fair thing to close-bore the other
part. The result, checked and re-checked,
is a good lib., with a much bigger yardage
than was anticipated. As for some unex-
plained reason the co. had a big tonnage of
tin on hand when the war started, the pro-
ceeds at present fine prices, with the cash
in hand, will pay the heavy expenses of
reconstruction. The plant has ahead of it
as good ground as any it has bitten through.
This, however, will last only till about the
end of the year. Then the other side will be
tackled, and there (say) 4,000,000 cub.
yards are waiting-—about 25 p.c. more than
the co. has thus far handled altogether. But
as indicated up above, the average value
will be probably Jib. per yard less; and all
that |lb. comes off the profits.

Great Cobar has received this message
from the receiver in London: ‘’Proposals
for re-opening the mines agreed to by com-

When money is available only a few men

co. has applied for three months further sus-

Mine leases.
* * *
A Victorian crowd is interested in the old
Swallow’s Nest mine, near Mount David
(N.S.W.). This show has had a profitable
past in a district where many proposi-
tions have been tried and most of them
found wanting. At one time it produced
a lot of gold and paid handsomely; and as
a low-grade show of the easily worked and
fairly reliable order it is the best in the
district on present appearances. Mount
Gossan, Breakfast Creek and Swallow’s
Nest are all instances of Victorian mining
men going further afield. In that their
principal aim is legitimate prospecting,
their influx into N. S. Wales is admirable.
Breakfast Creek, near O’Connell
(N.S.W.), is to resume crushing, after a
prolonged hang-up owing to scarcity of
water. This show, which is Victorian-
owned, is shaping fairly, with enough gold
to pay its way; but the water supply is very
bad. Sooner or later, if prospects continue
to be encouraging, the question of a much
larger claim, with better catchment area,
will have to be faced. •
Until Edna May came along Black Range
was the ray of sunlight in Victorian adven-
turing in W.A., the yield from 238,283 tons
being 221,7270 z. gold. As £243,718 has
been paid in dividends, the mine has given
a profit of over £1 per oz. won. The co.
still has £12,000 cash in hand for the direc-
tors to play with. Their present intention
is to stop shaft-sinking, withdraw plant from
the bottom levels where the quartz is no
good, and gut the rest of the mine. Black
Range therefore will soon become a has-been
with a good record to look back on. Accord-
ing to present advices, the Elisha to this
mining Elijah is to be the Pinnacles, near
the Day Dawn, over which a working option
was taken a long while back. Ilans Irvine
has left the Board, and so has had a free
hand to pick and choose in the Edna pad-
dock.
* * * *
With seven or eight others, Ilans Irvine
has taken an option over the Myrtle, Edna
West and Edna Main claims, next the Edna
Central (W.A.). Coupar, of the Black
Range, was reported to have given the leases
his blessing. Whether that was tittle-tattle
or no, the mere whisper that Hans Irvine
had collected another good thing meant the
flotation in 10 minutes of a 65,000 share co.
of 10s. each. Vendors took 17,500 fully pa ids
and £6OOO cash, and the public loaded up
47,500 contribs. at ss. each. Thus ended
the second lesson. The first was taught to
the crowd who, walking for months over
the blocks, never' grabbed one option. The
third lesson will form the subject of a later
par.
* * * *
The Queensland Mines Department lias
recommended a £16,000 subsidy, spread over
two or three years, to sink a deep shaft on
the Iguana reef at Croydon. The shallow
levels of the field are pretty well exhausted,
so the new venture is a matter of life or
death. The Government is to pay half the
cost.
* * *
Vulcan (Q.), which had £4032 cash when
last half-year opened, had worked the bal-
ances down to £1152 by the time it closed,
as well as drawing on general stocks. And
there isn’t much to show for it—only ore
broken valued at £l7OO net. Practically
throughout the half-year the mine was all-
but closed, and no sales were made. War, of
course. What prospecting was done con-
tinued to hold out little hope of regular
bodies in the bottom levels: at 1200 ft. rich
ore was struck, but it was only a patch. The
best prospects are still at 750 ft., where there
is a good body in virgin ground waiting for
the pick.
* * * *
Lode Hill (Q.) is suffering from an
altack of boulders, iron sand and expect-
ancy. Solid unloading in Melbourne for a
week foreshadowed a low yield. So. the stern
eye has again been east on certain rotund
gentlemen who are thought to know a good
deal more about what’s what at Lode Hill
than the directors seem to do.
* * * *
Chiltern Valley, under old John Cock, is
doing its second dash on a brand-new ((in-
stitution. It starts with 14,710 sq. fathoms
to operate on. Having proved that the
wash opened up in cross drives averages
nearly an ounce, a good beginning has beeu
made. The gutter is 700 ft. wide, theoreti-
cally what it ought to be, for a narrow lead
at Rutherglen has generally spelt scoured
ground and little gold. John Cock has dug
along with one shift for six months to get
the lower level and the escape drive well
ahead. Now the water is coming out he is
casting about for men and cash to hustle on
to the dividend stage. To date over £1.000,000
of gold has come out of Chiltern Valley
puddlers under John Cock’s rule, so he
ought to know his mine.
* * * *
Suffolk United, Bendigo, always puts a
crowd or two of tributors on to help up calls.
Minister Brown, of the Mines Department,
now wants some more men put on. and
the co. isn’t demurring, though some of the,
tribute conditions exacted by the Government
are stiff. The co. itself has been poking
about between 1000 ft. and 1670 ft., but has
grubbed out very little that is payable. The
temptation exists to come up to 800 ft. and
go after the shoot of stone on which New
Moon is working to the co.’s boundary. No
work has been done south above this point,
so there is plenty of room for a decent
development in the old mine.

Before the end of the year Mount Lyell
expects to increase its output, and has
already made a start to increase its furnace
capacity. Its average for the last 12 weeks
(1712 tons blister) pans out 2.16 p.c. cop-
per, with ss. worth of gold and silver. Those
people who are always talking of Australia’s
onerous labor conditions are invited to
examine Lyell’s return, which shows an
average extraction of approximately 325. per
ton (copper at £6O) and a handsome profit.
Last half-year Mount Bischoff raised
51,533 tons, which is less than half the usual
quantity; 48,354 tons were sent to the mill,
and 3179 tons were rejected because of ex-
treme poverty. The return was 194 tons
concentrates, . worth slightly over UU p.c.
metal. The smelter also treated 913?. tons
of ore on public account for 040 tons of tin.
The average from purchased ore (mostly
stream tin) was thus u shade over 70 p.t-.
Bischoff required to treat more than 400
tons ore to produce one ton pure tin, so last
half-year it figured as a \ p.c. proposition.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, March S, 1915.
My Dear Arini, —
Actually a fund has been started in
Sydney that lias nothing to do with the
war. “To recognise Mr. W. A. Notting’s
public services in connection with the Har-
bor foreshores” a meeting was held at the
R.P.A. Yacht Club’s rooms the other evening,
and the club led off with £2O. Years and
years ago the crew of the yacht lolanthe
tried to land on a scrap of Harbor fore-
shore, and was driven off". 'The scrap which
the yachtsmen, who were in trouble, had
dared to thus violate was sacred Private
Property. The circumstances are misty now ;
but I know the crew or some of it was
drowned. That gave Mr. Netting his mis-
sion in life. Not another foot of Harbor
foreshore should be sold by the State if he
could prevent it; and the State should buy
back every handy lot that came into the
market.
Mr. Notting at the time —he was a printer,
I think—had no following at all. He was
a pen writing letters in the wilderness —a
voice crying out upon the waters. But he
never missed a chance to push his crusade
a few yards nearer his Holy Sepulchre; and
by degrees he got yachtsmen interested, and
then hundreds of good land-lubber citizens
who saw that the people had precious little
access left to their Beautiful Harbor. He
thus created a public opinion on his pet
subject so strong that not only were the
foreshores that remained public property
jealously preserved, but a good start was
made at Vaucluse to recover some of what
had been lost. A man like W. A. Notting
isn’t apt to become a millionaire; but he is
more apt than most people to wear himself
out. Something of the kind has happened;
hence the grateful R.F.A. Yacht Club has
got to work in order to say “Thanks” in the
most unmistakable manner.
A wedding party has done something new.
The other day. when Dorothy Taylor was
married to Leslie Waterhouse, of the Vickery
clan, at Lindfield, her sister was in hospital
as the result of a lost appendix. So, the
ceremony over, the bridal party in full force
and regalia visited Miss Polly, and exchanged
condolences and congratulations. The con-
dolences—to make things quite clear—were
left with the lady at the hospital.
The Artists’ war fund show will have
been opened by Governor Strickland before
you get this weekly reminder that you’re
seven days older. In the same Cause a
conversazione occurs in the melting atmo-
sphere of the Royal Art Society’s tabernacle
on Wednesday evening.
The art students of both societies are
putting their wits together to arrange a
series of tableaux for the benefit of the
Patriotic cash-box at Easter, or thereabouts.
Some of the Gallery pictures are to be
reproduced. There is, of course, no escape
for that hardy annual, “The Sea hath its
Pearls”; and the Awakening of Australia,
when navigator Cook dropped anchor in
Botany Bay, will follow two living pictures
in which the Dutch and French make no
serious attempts to stir the sleeping con-
tinent.
The Arts and Crafts disciples have given
their best wares to the artists’ war-fund
exhibition. Rhoda Wager’s filigi’ee silver
work is shown at its best in a glass case;
and Mildred Creed, with beaten brass com-
forts for the suburban villa, Florence Leist,
Olive Pink, Mrs. Soderberg and Violet
Teague are other notable contributors. Roll
up! Evei’ybody gets something handsome
for his one to five guineas, paid on the nail.
As the show is valued in cold figures at
£2OOO, the Red Cross fund should swell
visibly in a day or two.
Miss Nicholls, secretary of the Hobart
Art Society, has written to the organisers
of the N.S.W. Artists’ motor-lorry fund,
asking for details. Tasmania and Queens-
land are both throwing out feelers, and are
likely to come along presently with similar
attacks on the public pocket.
Barrister Bert Norris has persuaded
Bessie Lane Mullins to say “yes,” and con-
gratulations are pouring in to both parties,
as they say in bush classics. The girl is
the eldest daughter of John Lane Mullins,
whose popularity with artists and writers
in this town keeps him busy. If he isn’t
heating up a subscriotion list for some
impecunious literary genius on the wing to
other shores, you can easily locate him as
the person who keeps calm on an artists’
committee. Miss Bessie’s young man is a
son of the late R. A. Norris, a one-time
manager of the A.J.S. Bank. Years ago
John L. Mullins and Norris perc used to
hunt in couples when they started off to run
to earth some art trophy. Now the folds
of the old friendship are to be caught up
within a wedding ring.
The Admiralty lady whose lot fell in such
strenuous times is booked to leave for
Britain in a week or two. There is a
hankering to get nearer the lighting line on
the part of various naval officers’ dames,
and the flitting is likely to go on. Still, we
yet have with us the redoubtable Miss Hen-
derson, who can run the entertainments for
seamen’s wives in the most firm and gentle-
manly manner, so Mrs. Able Seamen has no
cause for tears.
Dr. Bobart, who came to Australia years
ago as surgeon on H.M.S. Dart, and return-
ing to England did more important medical
work for the navy, died at Neutral Bay
last week. He must have been almost the
first Australian medical graduate to put on
a naval surgeon’s uniform. Lately, retir-
ing from the sea, he settled on the South
Coast. He was a good Australian ; and though
Bulletin sub-editors wished he had had a
better writing master, they always wel-
comed his pretty numerous contributions.
Died last week, John Creed, LL.B., son of
Dr. Creed, M.L.C., and a member of the
N.S.W. junior Bar. After giving up
the pursuit of briefs, he did some
tutoring in the Outback. then was
municipal librarian at Broken Hill, and
finally was in the service of one of the
big • mining cos. Incidentally he was the
husband of Louise Mack, the literary lady
who remained calm enough to powder her
nose when shells screeched over her hotel
in Antwerp.
News comes this way of Australian artist
Itupert Bunny and his French wife, who
are likely to leave their beloved Paris in
July—for a while, anyhow. Art is having
a deep sleep in the usually gay city. The
Bunnies meanwhile devote their time to
the patriotic cause. Madame is helping to
nurse the wounded; Rupert is working at
the American Club, which is doing ambu-
lance work and being generally a good
Samaritan.
Mrs. Trefle, widow of the late Minister
for Lands, left Sydney for Melbourne by
Sunday night’s express. Thence she goes
to Launceston to spend a month or so with
brother Mick Shelley, who is reckoned one
of the best-looking Irishmen in the Jam
Island.
A committee has been formed to provide
some memorial of the popular Trefle. The
preliminaries are in the hands of the Aus-
tralasian Provincial Press Association, of
which the deceased Minister was a conspicu-
ous member—he part-owned a newspaper at
Temora, you remember.
A preliminary meeting in connection
with the State’s special Belgian fund, with
Premier Holman in charge, was held in the
Executive Council Chamber. The notice
convening this gathering was so vague that
about 1400 women stamped off to the Town
Hall to look after their end of the business.
The gentle Cullen dame meanwhile cooed
softly over the 30-odd who were at the cor-
rect address, and who wanted to know if
Woman was going to be s-s-slighted on the
new committee. Ladies with countenances
as soft as corrugated iron loudly demanded
their Rights. When their ruffled plumage
had been stroked the right way, three mem-
bers of our sex were chosen to sit with Man
on the Committee. Lady Cullen, Mrs. W.
A. Holman and Mrs. A. B. Piddington are
the triplet. Chief Secretary ,T. 11. Cann is
president, and Percy Hunter and W. Beavis
are hon. secretaries. The committee, which
is strengthened by financial advisers such as
Russell French, T. A. Dibbs and Richard
Teece, will leave no available shilling un-
turned.
Chief Secretary Cann has just had his
own personal troubles; news came to him
last week of his father’s death in Northum-
berland. The old miner had lived to see
one son Speaker, Treasurer, Chief Secretary
and a few other things in this State, and
another son'a member of Australia’s Parlia-
ment. Yet they had no advantages to start
with. The present Chief Secretary was at
work underground as soon as the' law would
let him go. What education he ever had he
picked up on the way. But he inherited
from somewhere the knack of saying the soft
answer that tnrneth away wrath. Nobody
else in the Ministry can purr quite so
softly.
A Sydneysider just back from the South
says Melba is A 1 again, after a hurried
call for the doctor while she was staying at
the sub-regal Stanleys’ crib. Visions of
appendicitis haunted the singer, till author-
ity decided that it was only a severe chill.
A draught, that cut across the singer’s
shoulders like an iced knife while she
warbled at Geelong for the Red Cross is
blamed for the misadventure. Melba is now
booked to leave Australia on April 14. But
there is nothing definite dragging at her on
the other side, and she may linger here till
May. In October, her TJ.S. tour begins.
Daughter-in-law Eileen is a great success,
and the George Armstrong menage runs on
velvet since her advent.
The first Jap journalist to come this way
for news, T. A. Shafto. left Sydney for
Maoriland on March 3. He represents the
Asahi Shimbun, otherwise the Morning
Sun, which goes to press in Tokio and
Osaka, and is the biggest independent news-
organ in Japan. Mr. Shafto says he is on
the trail of progressive legislation, and is
interested in those M.L. laws that seek to
put butter on the poor man’s bread. Also,
lie means to inquire how Woman handles
her vote in the Dominion.
The youngsters who go to Hebrew schools
in this city are working overtime for a

the lion. secs.. Louis S. Lowenthal, Alpha,
Ocean-street, Bondi; and Fanny L. Hart,
7 Council-street, Waverley. The sale occurs
in the Oddfellows’ Hall on March 24 and
25.
The struggle for a crust still leads us on.
Whangarei, a . pleasant little township
perched on the top, of Maoriland, has just
handed out a taxi-driver’s license to a local
maiden.
Our Aides are proving themselves true to
type. They are only carpet knights till the
opportunity shows the way. Of Governor
Stanley’s staff, little Captain Gale has lost
an arm. Captain C. B. Wilson merely com-
plains that the shrapnel interferes some-
times with shaving. Mr. Wilberforce’s per-
formance was short and sharp. From Lon-
don to the trenches —an hour in the mud-
then back to the base with a fractured leg.
Ilarry Talbot, lately of Queensland, is doing
good work. Captain “Tommy” Curwen is
reported to have become engaged to a widow
and done equally brave things in the trenches.
Captain Kirkpatrick, who accompanied
Lady Fuller on her last visit to Australia, is
reported killed.
11. N. Southwell, formerly Paling’s con-
cert manager, writes from London that the
theatres are keeping full, though managers
take pleasure in gloomily prophesying that
“it cannot last.” For that poor reason, per-
haps, they are going to very little expense.
Yet “the queues are as long as they would
be if the shows were good,” says “Southie”
—though that’s not to say that they will
last as long. For his own part he went, on
Christmas Eve to see Tree’s “David Copper-
field.” Christmas in the grey old city was
exciting by reason of the hurried visit of
soldiers on leave from France for 48 hours
or so. “Such reunions in England were
unique, and naturally the war was the chief
topic. In London, where of late years snow
is rarely seen, it came down softly for a day
and made even the smuts turn pale.... The
systematic cleaning of the streets here is as
mysterious as it is effective. I never see
anyone doing it, yet it is done all right.”
The Willie Cooper dame has been vigor-
ously attacking the Cardigan vest question,
and piles of these knitted comforts are reach-
ing the right people through her efforts.
Eva Benson, the Westralian girl who won
the Hackett prize given by the Society jof
Arts in Perth, is doing well in England. She
lately won a bronze medal, the highest awgi'd
for modelling, in the National Competition
of the Art Schools of the Empire. Eva is
new turning seriously to sculpture, and
recently chiselled a satisfactory bust of
Winthrop Hackett, the Perth newspaper
proprietor who was over in Sydney lately,
looking a very much older man, by the way,
than before he went through the long arbi-
tration case touching the interest of late
partner Harper in the West Australian.
Surgeon-Colonel Ahearne, of the Army
Medical Corps, till lately chief medical officer
in North Queensland, was booked to lecture
on “The Australian Expedition” when the
last mail left London. The proceeds were
for one of the gaping war funds. The
Queensland man, who joined the Northern
defence force in 1882, took a precocious ex-
perience to his Australian billet. In his
whiskerless youth he saw service in the
Franco-Prussian war as a medical student
attached to the Anglo-American Ambulance
corps.
Travelling military kitchens have caught
the fancy of most housewives in this State.
State Commandant Wallack had to say
“Thank you very much” for three of them
the other day. Mrs. John Waugh (on
behalf of the Women’s Liberal League,
which drinks tea in the Queen Victoria Club)
and Mrs. H. W. Fell (as voice of the North
Sydney branch) were sponsors for two.
Mrs. Burnell was godma for the third, which
comes from the Parramatta end of the
League.
Katoomba bowling rink is not so rushed
by womenkind as are the Waverley greens,
where the hosts gallantly provide afternoon
tea for thirsty onlookers of the petticoat
persuasion. Still, Katoomba rink is having
a few Wednesday afternoon visitors of our
sex, who tie themselves up in gossamers to
see Papa do his worst with the fat round
balls. Wednesday is the day when the
suburbs go to the mountains to wrestle for
the Peacock Cup. Burwood came out on top
at last week’s outing. Mr. Reggie Dean, a
well-known Waverley bowler, who has been
staying on the adjacent hilltop, has been
putting in his spare time at the Katoomba
rink where the breeze slips round the
shoulder of the mountain. Dean, senior, now
gone over to the quiet majority, was one of
Sydney’s early auctioneers, in the days when
his firm, afterwards Bradley, Newton and
Lamb, implored you to say whether you were
all done near the site of the S. M. Herald
office.
The Allen Taylors’ roomy bungalow at
Leura was bought a while ago by Mr. Lou
Davies. Mrs. Lou was one of the moneyed
Spark family; but hubby lias plenty of am-
munition of his own to fire at the wolf at
the gate. Some time ago, you may remember,
he bought, the Arthur Allens’ waterside nest
at Port Hacking —the place where so many
honeymoon couples used to bill and coo,
though none of them ever billed like the
honeymoon boarding-houses.
Chief Justice Cullen, of the N. S. Wales
Bench, is promoted to “Lord Chief Justice
of Australia” by the Windsor Magazine.
He and Admiral Patey were making swift
strides across the local sod when the camera
caught them. But unless the war-dog is un-
expectedly agile, the Judge will win. His
coat-tails always fly ahead of the crowd
homeward bound from the Mosman ferry.
Squatter Pegler, of South Australia,
a hearty-looking old gentleman with a
Daddy Christmas beard, motors where other
folks take train or Shanks’s ponies. He fell
in love with our Blue Mountains some time
ago, and had a run up the other day to see
if they were in the same place. He petrolled
over from S. Australia, as usual, and showed
no signs of wear and tear at the end of his
long journey.
Engineer Sydney Williams, of Rockhamp-
ton, and a couple of daughters, have been
holidaying at Leura. Mrs. Richard Binnie,
the Point Piper lady whose knitting needles
are working overtime, is just descending to
town from the same chalet. Solicitor Fitz-
gerald, a Brisbane lawyer, who is off to
England in the Nestor, has been breathing
mountain air also.
Now that Josephine Cohan, who is Mrs.
Fred Niblo off the boards, is going to leave
us we can look back on her succession of
elegant frocks with a minor pang of envy.
Except sometimes. For instance, it has for
months fallen to the lot of Josephine to fre-
quently star in plays that had a snowstorm
falling from the flies. That meant furs, a
heavy coat and a large muff in a tempera-
ture as grilling as that of any place you can
think of. “Seven Keys to Baldpate” kept
her dressed for blizzards for a very big slice
of our present summer. Still, she bore up
nobly, and wore fur hats at the saucy angle
that went with the stage freeze.
The tartan breaks out in several new
places in the Autumn fashions. The after-
math of the French admiration for the Hie-
lander’s kilts is a minor frenzy of plaid
with floral designs added to the homely
Scotch background. French names com-
plete this entente cordiale in fancy silks.
Another old friend of the lady with the long
burse is the heavy brocade, with velvet blobs
stamped promiscuously over its flowered
folds. The latest veils, I notice, just come
to the tip of the nose, and slice the counten-
ance in two pieces.
The Red Cross Produce Depot, 215
George-street, wants tobacco for Tommy
Kangaroo and his friend Atkins, who are at
the Front. Walking sticks, stout enough to
help a wounded man limp on the road to
recovery, are also asked for.
A Goulburn correspondent sends a fear-
some list of Manchester goods that a local
linen tea dragged out of a Town Hall gather-
ing for the hosnital there, The youthful
Mayoress, Miss Lily Betts, supported her
father’s efforts to cast a mild glow of fes-
tivity over the display of sheeting and ban-
dages. Music was contributed by a good-
looking Goulburn girl, Nora Conolly, who
uplifted her voice presumably in a song of
thanksgiving. At the piano was Archdeacon
Bartlett, the cleric who is accused of being
both useful and ornamental.
Artist Ethel Phillips Fox has just had
a reminder that her 1914 subscription to
the Paris Autumn Salon is due. She is a
member of that august body, which is mak-
ing over to the French war funds all the
cash it can scrape together from last year’s
subscriptions.
For an out-door fete in aid of the Belgian
Fund how would a croquet tournament go?
If players dressed in the crinoline period
the effect would be helped. Even Mountain
golf links so far unbend as to give the
wooden mallets house-room. So players must
be fairly plentiful, if crinolines are scarce.
Miss Maud Tighe, a sister of Harry
Tighe, who now pursues the inky way in
London, is returning to Sydney after a nip
to war-scarred Europe.
Governor Strickland recently tore aside
the drapery on a memorial fountain to the
late .T. K. Chisholm at Camden. The Chis-
holm family, like the Macarthur Onslows,
got in early on good pastoral land there-
abouts, and so avoided the rush and the
modern land-ballot papers.
lours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

SPECIALIST EXPLAINS CAUSE OF
STOMACH TROUBLE.
valuable advice to sufferers.
“There are many different forms of
stomach trouble,” said a well-known special-
ist recently, “but practicallv all are traceable
to excessive acidity and food fermentation.
That is why the results obtained from the
use of drugs are usually so disappointing.
Admitting fermentation . and consequent
acidity of the food contents to be the under-
lying cause of most forms of indigestion, it
naturally follows that the use of a reliable
antacid, such as the pure bisurated magnesia
which is so frequently prescribed by physi-
cians, will produce better results than any
known drug or combination of drugs.
Accordingly I almost invariably advise
those who complain of digestive trouble to
get some bisuratcd magnesia (note the
name carefully, as other forms are unsuit-
able for this purpose) from their chemist,
and take half a teaspoonful of the powder,
or two 5-grain compressed tablets, with a
little water after meals. This, by immedi-
ately neutralising the acid and stopping the
fermentation, removes the cause of all the
trouble and insures normal and healthy
digestion.”*

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, March 8, 1915.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Thursday was a day of days for racing;
also it was a day of days for bookmaking,
the unexpected happening with the usual per-
sistence. Women put on the loveliest clothes
they could beg, buy or owe for. The most
expensive shops say they had a record early-
morning- sale of hat models and fancy shoes
and stockings. The numerous vice-regal
party took turns in the box. Lady Stanley
tied a big pink straw hat under her
chin —you know what I mean —and
talked to Frank Suttor, whose white
moustache turns up at an almost Kaiser-
esque angle. He is here, from Sydney, on a
visit to the M.-F.’s. Lady Helen took two
pretty Bail lieu girls into the box for a yarn
at an ‘‘off” quarter of an hour. The Bail-
lieux mostly sat in a good-looking family
row. They all danced a sort of family taran-
tella, and shook hands with each other when
Wallacliia won the last race. They had gee-
gees travelling for all the big things, and
this was the first catch. The red and drab
of the Syme stable had two extraordinary
wins, and the Symes were wreathed in smiles.
Mrs. Geoffrey wore black chiffon velvet in a
flare, and a gold-silk-lace collar like a cava-
lier’s cape The newest collars are almost
a garment in themselves. Mrs. McAllister, a
daughter of David Syme, had her peggy-bag
weighed down with winnings. Perhaps the
ghost*of King David would condone the once-
I'orbidden gambling—the bawbees that fell to
the family were so many.
Mrs. Kenna, from Sydney, wore a cool
black dress covered with small silk roses and
a cute veiling of black net. It was a master-
piece of expensive simplicity. The Doctor-
had on. the loveliest silk socks, with old-gold
stripes, and his trousers amounted to a poem.
Mrs. Sternberg brought her sister, Mrs. Har-
ris, who is visiting from Maoriland. The
Harrises- “steward” Riccarton racecourse at
Christchurch, and have a palace with a
racing stable “up the Papanui-road,” where
all the Christchurch mansions are.
Mrs. “Glenlotli” Pete, down from her near-
the-Murfay sheep-run—she says she'll never
have faith in a blade of grass again—wore a
duck of a white turban (Indian model), and
fixed up a theatre party with her friends to
end the gay race-week. It is an awful thing
«o be a drought-afflicted station-owner.
The Melbourne girl who recently married
Rich Thompson, of Carleon (N.S.W.), wore
white cloth of a marvellous cut, and her hus-
band had on a proud look. The two have
just finished a Murray-Darling motor-e/nß-
steamboat trip, and sandwiched in the
Autumn races before surfing at Lome for
Master. These rich people have hard work
to get away with Time.
The Walter Bruntons are over from Syd-
ney. He is wearing spats. She also exhibits
a number of most expensive garments. The
aged William Cooper (Bart.) brought his
rheumatism along, or else found it here wait-
ing for him on his arrival, and could only get
round the betting-paddock with a stick.
Melba, in transparent black and an ex-
pensively simple hat, opened Hans Heysen’s
show in the Athenaeum Gallery last Thurs-
day. Local artists and noble folk for-
gathered like equals, and murmured their
admiration like persons of similar clay,
while the Star mentioned, in about seven
sprightly words, that Hans came from Den-
mark, that he was a genius, that he had
never been on speaking terms with Bill the
Awful, and that all his painting was guar-
anteed pure Australian. She wound up
with a half-strangled higli-kick that swished
the black expensiveness into its proper
place, and then skipped off the dais. Melba’s
v< ait hf ulness positively makes you gasp.
She looks young and fresh enough for white
muslin and blue bows. Among the best of
the Heysen pictures are a group lent by
Melba showing Coombe Cottage, some of
Melba’s turkeys and her pet gardener in
pastel. The marble verandah came from
Kurope with the moss ready grown on it,
but the turkeys are home made.
Australian Cup Day sagged socially to a
small crowd of tailored women who were
not afraid of spoiling in the wet. A tre-
mendous dust- and rain-storm made a chilly
tunnel of the grandstand and blew into the
teeth of the horses coming up the straight.
Governor Stanley and visiting Governor
Galway were the only vice-regals who ven-
tured out, and so they had their choice of
corners of the box. Mrs. Tye’s Anglo-Saxon
fairness was set off by beautiful furs. Mrs.
Monash tried to forget her military sponsor’s
absence in the joy of a peacock-blue kilted
coat; and there were times when she ap-
peared to be succeeding. Mrs. Alderman
Jeffries, in navy blue, and the daughter
who always wears a kiss-curl were present,
and went thankfully to their appointed
places at the Croker tea-party. The racing
men were all there, my dear —quite a change
from the usual allowance of one he to 20
shes at the tea-scramble. There probably
was quite a lot of betting and noise in the
paddock, as usual —but the women let it
severely alone and troubled never a bookie
with a surreptitious half-crown. They
huddled on the stand and kept within diving
distance of the blessed spot, where blue
noses and tossed switches can be rectified
at a moment’s notice. The Fergusons
stayed at home —possibly to help to get the
dinner ready. Their visitors are legion, and
an added crowd was invited for dinner on
Australian Cup night. Colonel and Mrs.
Legge were guests. Mrs. Legge has just
returned after a two years’ absence in Fog-
land. Miss Eadith Walker, from Sydney-
side, has been rewarded for the loan of her
home to the Mofergs by a shakedown and
numerous meals at Government House. The
Powers and the Caseys and the Everard
Brownes turned up as a matter of course.
Melba was asked, but did not go, being well-
nigh exhausted with her Patriotic efforts.
Champion Stakes Day was a riot of
men. The women were so few as to be
hardly visible to the unglassed eye. Nothing-
but plain, drab-suited men. Not an ostrich
plume in their bowler hats; not a spangle
on their costumes. Still there were a few
gracious exceptions. Lady Helen was
there—the only female vice-regal in evidence.
A number of Falkiners arrived in a gleeful
manner, but they became palpably peeved
when Land of Song met its second defeat.
Mrs. Everard Browne gave a lunch party, at
which several choice merinoes and high-class
crossbreds from up Riverina way were
present. The Jack Winter-Irvines also trans-
pired. ‘Dewar” Thiel brought his hand-
some wife and daughter. A tearing north wind
blew angrily out of a dull copper sky, but
Mrs. Guy Jones insisted on wearing her new
black chiffon muff. Hiskens, of Moonee Val-
ley racecourse, turned up alone. His wife is
in Banana land.
Mrs. Hugh Denison wore another-fetching
frock. It was of black corded silk, and the
out —well, there, my dear, words fail me.
With this costume she affected a smart black
hat. The Fred Smiths, also of Sydney,
worthily represented the vast Smith family.
Bachelor Sadlier —he who haunts racecourses
and the steps of the Australian Club—went
about with his arm in a black sling; and
when friends asked him if he had
returned from the war he moaned that lie
had broken a sinew. Theo Marks’s pretty
wife wore the trickiest dress, like a bolster
of blue cloth, and above it a pill-box hat.
There were vine leaves and libations at
the Savoy on Saturday night. “Sports”
fresh from Flemington clustered in bunches.
Barry Lupino seized a chance, and became an
auctioneer. A walking-stick and other equally
useless articles were auctioned on behalf of
the badgered little Belgians. From owner
to owner the stick, etc., strayed, bringing-
each time a shower of cash. Lincoln
Plumer aided and abetted in the philan-
thropic plundering.
A Mr. Brown, of Suva, has been “doing”
the races very thoroughly. He came to Mel-
bourne to meet his wife and an adopted
daughter, both just returned from poor old
Europe. The trio did all possible things
well, and some things even better. For a few
brief days the name of Brown blazed in our
minds; but it is only glowing now. It is no
use trying to burn a name like Brown on
Ihe public memory.
Hugh Denison and his wife are over from
Sydney. But they say they came to get
cool —not to look at the races. Mrs. Deni-
son proved this by spending one of the “big”
days walking up Fern Tree Gully. Her hus-
band put in the time golfing. But Fleming-
ton has seen some of liis wife’s frocks. On
Australian Cup Day she hid from the rain
under the trees in a smart khaki tailored
dress. It had the newest kilted bustle-
basque. and the small turban that went with
it was the smartest thing out that day.
Dolly Castles was at most of the recent
races in tan fixings and fair hair (fixture).
Sam Hordern chuckled like a good business
man over the fact that his horse Onager
nearly paid for itself on its first day out.
lie gave the oft-quoted Mr. Connelly £lOOO
for the animal just before the lucky meeting.
Enid Whiting Waters, from Sydney, has
been haunting the races in good clothes. Her
husband is going to the war. The motto of
the average wife nowadays is: Let us dress
and be merry, for to-morrow we shall have
the hump and not care much how we look.
Melba intends getting out an Australian
gift book in aid of the Belgians, and is busy
extracting promises of contributions- from
local writers and artists.
Napoleon Boffard proposes to sing half a.
dozen ardent French love songs at the Cafe
Francatelli on Friday afternoon, March 12.
He will then take round his belltopper on be-
half of the Belgian Fund, and if, at the end
of his pilgrimage, the garment is not full, he
will publicly bound upon it, and leave the
premises, showing signs of annoyance.
In aid of the Queen Victoria Hospital for
Women and the Red Cross Funds—a Demon-
stration ! This is the resonant trumpet-call
of Prout Webb’s niece and Miss Lorimer,
of the Open Air Gymnasium. Lady Helen
Munro Ferguson is taking a personal' interest
in the show’, and so is the Mayoress: and
both of them will be there on March 19. It
is to be at the Auditorium. The girls will
fence, and pose as Grecian statues, and wear
snappy, short, white, kilted skirts instead of
the usual physical culture bathing-gown rig.
Men will be allowed to buy seats as a great
privilege.
The Brighton Ladies’ Swimming Club
splashed this year on behalf of the Bed
Cross Fund. There were no men present,
but the shrieks of schoolgirl barrackers tore
the air in a fairly satisfactory manner. A
yellow-haired flapper named Ethel Crau-
ford swam off with the championship, out-
classing Lily Beaurepaire by a nose. There
is a damp future ahead of this girl.
One of Sydney's recent popular naval
brides, Georgia Vaughan Lewis, daughter of
the late Parliamentary chatterbox, J. C.
Neild, is booked through for London. She
goes in hopes of snapping a honeymoon with
her husband during a vacation from his
North Sea watch. They had only a few
hours (or was it minutes?) together after
their Avedding. .
( Nesta, the youngest Clarkelet of the Mount
Sehank family, came to a pretty definite
arrangement Avith a young man during the
voyage out. lie is a son of Mars, return-
ing from the Front to his Sydney people,
slightly damaged.
At the. M.C.C. Tennis Tournament at St.
Kilda on Saturday l’at O’Hara Wood, aa'lio
has taken the local equivalent of the “King’s
Shilling-,’’ displayed a pair of stout khaki-
encased legs. In this costume lie Avatched
brother Arthur, partnered by Meryl Wax-
man, easily demolish their opponents.
Esther Fair bairn, in sombre black, which set
off her tawny thatch, Avas in to see her
friend Mini Pearson, of Kilmany Park, play.
The Pearson chick is just back from London,
where she presented her heart to a man in a
Hussar regiment.
There Avas, a party last Friday at the
liepertory Theatre Club, and all the brain-
sturm attended. Mrs. Crowther hostessed and
supplied the necessary tea for the invited
guests, Avho brought their Patriotic sewing.
When the function Avas fairly started the
crash of needles Avas audible even in the
blizzard of small talk. Miss Alma Palmer,
a flapper star of the Albert-street Conser-
vatoriuni, played Mozart till she nearly
burst with pride. The clever youngster has
composed some songs, Avhich Avcrc gently
dealt with by Mary Campbell. One lone
soldier took the floor in khaki. lie was a
Bepertory actor once, with skyscraping am-
bitions ; but now lie is going to the Front,
Avhere only the rattle of the Maxims will
remind him of the applause he used to Avin.
Mrs. Dr. Vance has gone to Tasmania for
the “birthday” of her father, Philip Fysli.
The patriarch has reached the 80tli mile-peg.
hen she. left, the idea Avas that the entire
Fysli family should motor to Brown River,
and drink the inevitable bumpers on its
mossy bank.
There was good fellowship and popping of
corks the other afternoon AA'hen the office
pals of Dal.gety’s avoo! expert, Frank Guth-
rie, bade him an re voir. He is leaA r ing to
take up the managership of the firm’s Gee-
long office, vice Mr. David Aitken, who died
some Aveeks ago. Frank Guthrie knows as
much about avool as any other expert in Aus-
tralia.. and his knowledge is backed by
vivacity. Mrs. Guthrie, a fair, pleasant
little lady, is much liked, notwithstanding
that she Avields a deadly club at bridge. She
is a Maorilander and a relative of theatrical
entrepreneur Beynolds Denniston.
Mrs. Kathleen Watson Deardon. avlio has
supplemented her “Litanies of Life” with
"Later Litanies,” tells the story against her
genial self. At Yarraman (Q.), where she
abide! h nowadays when she isn’t a visitor
to Melbourne, she offered prizes to the two
“best sorts”—boy and girl—in the local
State school. The winners were to be chosen
by ballot of pupils—one youngster, one vote.
A pert little Gladys, being congratulated
on Avinning the girl election, assumed in-
difference. “I’m not a bit proud,” said the
young dissembler. “I suppose the prize will
be one of Mrs. Deardon’s ‘Liberties of Life’
—and Avhat's the good of that?” However,
the rcAvards of popularity Avere big books,
Avith pictures in them. Later Gladys,
arrived at the age of flapperdom, Avill- pro-
bably grab all the Liberties of Life that
mother doesn’t absolutely prohibit.
Eben Gould, a favorite bachelor-doctor
AA'ho makes the pulse of Society beat faster,
is giving up his resident job at the Homoeo-
pathic Hospital and lighting a red lamp in
the vice-regal suburb of Malvern.
Claude Kingston, organist of the Collins-
street Baptist Tabernacle, is engaged to
Mabel Thompson, Avhose father is an English
alderman, but will probably recover from it
some day. The fiancee is a sister of Mrs.
F. C. Spurr, so there is a stained-glass
radiance over the joyful incident.
The Girl in the Taxi, whose cab-fares are
noAV paid by husband Tom Beynolds, the
proud owner of SAvedish Lad, is settling
down to the flatted life. The pair are
snugly fixed at Cliveden Mansions.
Alice, one of the three bonny daughters
of Mrs. Watt, of hereabout, is now Mrs.
Goode. She has not forsaken the stage, but
is still “turning” in vaudeville as though
untouched by the cares of matrimony.
Mrs. George Cliirnside is coming back to
Melbourne by the Orontes, bringing her
daughter Audrey with her. Audrey is the
girl who was reported to be driving her own
motor-car at the Front. The story was
afterwards said to be greatly exaggerated.
Many are glad that Mrs. George is coming
back. For one thing, they keep abdut the
best cook in Melbourne at Airlie.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The Wattle Day League demonstration on Satur-
day was in aid of the Motor-Ambulance Fund.
Scout-craft, physical drill, first aid, and children's
musical marches occurred at the Jubilee Oval in
the afternoon. In the morning a sale of patriotic
badges' earned over £lOO. The Public Service
passed the same day collecting boots. They raked
in nearly 4000 pairs. The boots are to be distri-
buted by the Patriotic Fund committee. There is
not much wrong with the Empire when you find
people going round boot-oh-ing ,i'or it in large
numbers.
Ten per cent, of the cash proceeds of -our. Tennis
Tournament next week is to go to the Belgian
Fund. The Governor will not only preside at tin-
luncheon on the opening day and distribute the prizes,
but he intends also to play in some of the matches.
The “Cheer-up” Society arranged a welcome on
Friday for the S.A. marines who have returned
from Papua, and at the same time opened a
marquee which has been reared at the back of the
City Baths as a kind of clubroom for use by mem-
bers of the contingents that are in camp.
The Sportsmen’s Battalion looks like coming to
pass. It may raise the awkward question: What is
a sportsman? How about bookies and barbers and
tobacconists? They all simply revel in sport. I
suppose the solution will be the fact that none of
them are likely to arouse discussion by attempting
to go to the Front.
Gwen Williams (daughter of Dr. W. I).,
now at the Front) lias been playing "lead”
in the Fog in a new scandaldrama dealing
with the backstairs history of Napoleon and
Josephine. Gwen, one of Charlotte Hem-
ming’s pupils, was given a small part by
Matheson Lang when the “Third Floor
Hack” was passing here, and went with the
co. to S’Africa.
Elocutionist Lilian Prichard's latest en-
gagement is of a pro-matrimonial nature, the
other party being young Alan Mitchell., son
of the R. J. Mitchells, of Warracknabeal.
The fiance is with the Expeditionary Force.
Dublin papers report the wedding of J. T.
Donovan, LL.B., one of the Irish envoys
who visited Australia a year or so back, to
Miss A. Ralph, of Auckland (M.L.).
Father OMlara, of Xewrv. extracted their
“I wills” in the pro-Cathedral, Marlborough-
street.
G. Bray Gordon, here with “Bunty Pulls
the Strings,” has left Graham Moffat’s com-
pany and is off to the trenches.
The baritone of the elegant Rebottaro is
but rarely heard nowadays; the explanation
being that the Signor lias gone in for poultry
farming on a large scale at Cheltenham,
where he spends a good deal of his time.
Once in a while lie leaves his nest and does
the Block. 'But he always has his pockets
full of fowls, and any musical girl who
yearns to have a word with him must talk
of roosters’ top-notes if she would hold him
for as long as it takes to produce an egg.
George Fairbairn and his male relatives
are determined to be well prepared for the
worst. The ex-politician drills his people
enthusiastically, and makes the-same sort of
sounds as the professional drill instructors,
who bark at'influential suburbanites by star-
light wherever there is room to line up.
From “Rosna,” in London:—
The ex-Gippsland writing lady, Mary Grant Bruce,
and her Major have had more than their share of
worry these baleful times. After rushing back to
Britain to rejoin the colors, Major Bruce was
ordered off to the Royal Dublin Fusiliers; but a
germ attacked him, and lie was forced to stay be-
hind for an encounter with London’ surgeons. lie is
now at Teddington, convalescing with relatives who
are mourning a. son—another Major Bruce.
Judge Cliomley’s daughters, having let the
Toorak house, are thinking of going to Eng-
land to join sister Mary, who mixes the pap
for the Victoria League.
The Repertory Theatre Club will move to
the Snowden Theatre, just over Prince’s
Bridge, next month. The women of the
Club, by the way, have formed a Red Cross
centre, and meet every Friday to sew for
soldiers.
An ex-Victorian, now in London, who
struck it rich in the West, and was on the
eye of wedding with a moneyed Australian
girl, is now wondering dazedly why lie intro-
duced his donah to his pal. His pal had
very little money, but he possessed a litle
and the lady fell in love with him or it.
Presently the man who only had money got
his ring back with a polite note explaining
that the sender had decided to keep the ear)
from busting.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

FADS!
The public who have been using an Inter-
nal Lubricant in the shape of some form of
Mineral Oil or Petroleum Jelly to relievo
Constipation are finding out that this whole-
sale Lubrication of their internal organs is
destroying one of Nature’s most vital fluids
—the Bile. Nature finds its work being-
done by a foreign agent, grows less and Jess
active, and at last ceases to put forth any
effort. This is the effect produced by the
use of an Internal Lubricant. It destroys
the natural movement of the bowels, because
it supersedes the flow of the Bile, and, there-
fore, the taking of Liquid Paraffin in any
of its forms is a source of future danger
to anyone so foolish as to be carried away by
this most unfortunate fad.
To put it as clearly as possible, the Liver
can be compared to a big sponge full of
holes. It becomes covered over with a slimy
substance, and these holes or ducts from
which the Bile flows become stopped up.
consequently the Bile is forced back into
the system, producing two direct results:
(a) a derangement of the entire system, and
(b) Constipation, because the Bile cannot
act on the waste matter in the Intestinal
Canal, and it remains in the system. This
is what is commonly known as a Torpid
Liver. Something is wanted to get at the
cause—something that will act on the Liver
and open up these ducts. Not something
that will simply carry off the waste in the
system, leaving it in just the same state to
repeat over again just what: has taken place.
No poisonous mineral product will take the
place of the Bile given each human being by
an All-wise Creator to carry the waste mat-
ter out; of the system, and aid in the diges-
tion and assimilation of our food. People
resorting to this mineral oil. either in igno-
rance or not, and practically saying, “We
have no further use for the Bile, we have
something to relieve Constipation, that is all
we care’’—will pay for their ignorance or
impudence in thinking they can improve on
the Creator’s work.
Sometimes Nature only requires a little
assistance _ to get over an obstruction, and
if something is used to merely give that
assistance and not usurp all Nature’s func-
tions and take the entire operation out of
Natures hands, then valuable help is being-
rendered. This is where the invaluable pro-
perties of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are
apparent. They do not usurp Nature’s
functions, destroying all their natural
activity, but when Nature needs help to
carry its often too heavy burden, caused by
overeating and drinking, then Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills assist Nature. They act
on the stomach, and. above all. on the Liver,
causing the stopped-up ducts to release tin-
flow of Bile that has been withheld. It is
this flow of Bile that causes the bowels to
act, and any remedy that ignores this fact
is causing damage to the great human
mechanism. It is the constant and regular
flow of Bile that aids in the digestion and
assimilation of food, and when this flow
ceases from any cause the system at once is
affected throughout, and Constipation, of
course, the inevitable result.
For nearly a century Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills have been in use as a remedy for
liver ailments. They get at the cause. They
open the ducts and allow the Bile to flow,
aiding and assisting Nature, not in any
degree superseding or weakening the natural
functions. A pill occasionally produces
regularity and good health, and with reason-
able care regarding overtaxing the digestive
organs with either food or drink, a healthy
condition can be maintained. This is the
secret of good health in man. woman and
child, and there is no getting away from it.
Any fad that is based on the principle that
it can take the place of the Bile is a fraud
and a danger, and should be speedily put
where it belongs—back into the oil tank for
lubricating machinery, not into the human
system.*

BIRDS OR A HEATHER.
Fearful crudities by the Turks are reported.
Fifty Armenians were stripped and compelled to leap
into an abyss at the Devil’s Gap. This was continued
until one Araienian carried a Turk with him. Then
the remainder were shot. Two hundred and fifty
Armenians were massacred at Tamvot, and the
women carried into captivity. — Cable.

Though members of the Holman Ministry
have been keeping laboriously silent about
the totalisator, George Black, who joined the
team only a few days ago, seems to reckon
that the subject would be all the better for
an airiug. Therefore in his first speech as
a Minister he gave the totalisator a blessing
and said that the bookmaker was “a parasite
who could be easily dispensed with.” These
observations suggest that Black has summed
up Holman’s recent remarks about “the
people Avlio were supporting the totalisator
for bad reasons” and finds them mere muddy
drivel.
The whooping noises made over the result
of the Federal election for the Grampians
seat were without point, seeing that Salmon’s
victory makes no difference —the Govern-
ment’s majority in the Reps, is still nine.
The Grampians is one of the seats always
tottering, and a hundred or two votes will
push it either Avay. Stewart in his two
attempts nearly shoved Mans Irvine out;
but the extra few votes to, do the trick were
lacking. Then, last election, Jolley, bring-
ing his talents 'as ail"’, advocate to bear,
gathered in the few extras, and the job was
done. This time Salmon got the balance
on bis side, but nobody knows better than lie
that he will have to fight every hour to hold
it. His majority was gained in two centres
—lnglewood and Wedderburn —which are
both infested by cow and wheat, cockies
whose only Joss is.cheap labor ; and it is a
hard job to keep that lot sweet.

Victoria, which has /been the sanest of
all the Australian States during the recent
borrowing. orgy, deserves to have no diffi-
culty in couvei/tmg : per cent. Troasijry
bonds which Will mature,on All Fpojs’/Day
next year. The Governipe.nt’s .•proposal to
holders is that they should 'confe ytlpiig 'at
once and accept 4 per cent, stock or deben-
tures dated April, 1915, and maturing in
1927. That would give them not only a
half per cent, extra for the new term, but
a half per cent, more for the unexpired
period of the old term. Practically all the
million and a quarter or thereabouts con-
cerned is held in Australia. In the way of
giving itself its own IOU —which is better
than giving it to anybody else —Victoria has
done well during the last 10 years or so.
Between June, 1901. and June, 1914, it
increased its .State debt by £16,058,451 (N.
S. Wales plunged to the extent of £47,935,000
in the same period), and the Southern "State
actually reduced its foreign public debt by
£5,372,235.
That strong man with the rigid backbone,
Victoria’s Mines Minister J. Dry Brown,
has taken a grip of the devastating dredge
business, and all is now well. A year ago
•the companies were notified that they would
have to fulfil the conditions of their leases —-
mostly as to re-soiling—or quit. They ig-
nored ,the .information. The MimSfiCr then
decided to stand Am,moi;e nonsense, ; antl gave
them..seven days’’notice to carry out their
contracts,; .in default, forfeiture. The
days passed casually, like . the previous 370
or thereabouts. Then Dry Brown, realising
that lie had given them every reasonable op-
' portunity, resolved —to send an officer to
the companies to explain exactly what the
Department would be pleased if they would
do.
The farmer gets to the right place at
times. Thus the Ilopetoun wheat growers
lately decided to assure the Peacock Govern-
ment of their firm conviction that the Price
of Foods Board had “played into the hands
of speculators, to the ruination of the very
people whom it was appointed to protect,” by
first fixing the price of wheat, which in-
duced farmers to sell, and then abolishing
the limit, so that farmers needing seed had
to pay exorbitant prices. In other words,
the farmers who had wheat to sell were
inveigled into selling it at a comparatively
low price for the benefit of the dealers, and
then the farmers who didn’t have wheat were
obliged to pay a high price for seed, also for
the benefit of the dealers.
From official returns published by Maori-
land’s Labor Department:—
In Maoriland it costs a man over £3O to go on
strike, and not in one case in five does he get the
thing he struck for.
The Department’s calculations cover a
period of 20 years. Out of 144 strikes in
that time the employees were fully successful
in 25. the employers got the best of 84, and
compromises were arranged in 32. The big
recent waterside and miners’ strike cost the
victims £236,602 in wages! And that is
all they got out of it except a sore head and
a bad taste in the mouth, and perhaps a
suspicion that arbitration may have points
in its favor, after all.
The fight for a portfolio in X. S. Wales
has been renewed through the resignation of
Carmichael. One of the most persistent
battlers is W. C. Grahame, member for
Wickham, who polled well last time. lie
might have better chances if he did not pull
strings so obviously. The new favorite is
John Storey, the member for Balmain. He
has been in Parliament since July, 1001,
and is rather a modest man, as politicians
go. Stuart-Robertson, who ran Black
within two or three votes at the last selec-
tion, is still eager; but .he talked too plainly
after his last defeat. Dunn, who used to
be a school-teacher, is also in the shop-
window, labelled with a placard to remind
the caucus that ,the vacancy to be filled is
the Education office.

A candidate should be viewed with respect, if for
no other reason than that lie is. offering to serve his
country.... Why apologists .of one party should
regard the candidates, of another party as personally
detestable passes comprehension.
Which only shows that the Annus is much
in the habit of outrunning its own compre-
hension, inasmuch as its general politi-
cal attitude is that there is no creature <>n
earth more obnoxious than a Laborite offer-
ing his services to his country, unless it be
a Laborite .whose offer of service has been
accepted by his country.

years’ lease sold, say, six months ago upon
.the then profits for say £lO,OOO might
.. . .fall considerably in selling value.”
'This accounts for some of the milk in the
cocoa nut; but it is surely an outrageous

by the .State to. the holders of licenses to
sell grog should by reason of its cheapness
create special 'values which may be specu-

“II” writes as one aggrieved :■ —-
I don't want to abuse “Bill Sheep” (B. 25/2/‘l5).
I only want to point out a few of his inaccuracies about
the work of the N.S.W. Wheat Act. David Hall did
make prompt arrangements to keep the price of flour
down. Hence it costs only £ll/17/0 in N.S.W. as
against £lB/10/- per ton in Victoria, and a four-lb.
loaf is Bd. here against lOd. in Victoria, where
there is no restrictive legislation. Further William
can sell his wheat not only to “three enormously
rich firms,” but to 40 other firms which are not
enormously rich, but buy whenever the wheat is
presented, and pay spot cash.
Holman states that the meeting of State
Premiers in April “will really be a con-
ference on finance.” Six Premiers on their
loalisome, as it were.
In his latest observation about the sug-
gested “strategic” railway into Queensland,
Prime Minister Fisher uses 5(1 words to
illustrate how greatly such a line would
“facilitate commerce,” etc., to every one
word employed in reference to the military
usefulness of the project. Now, what is the
strategy —to fish with two hooks or with one
hook and two baits?
