Government Notices.
PROSPECTUS.
Victorian Government
Loan Conversion.
Under the Authority of Act No. 2026.
Interest at Four per centum free of
State Income Tax.
Principal and Interest payable in
Melbourne.
Redemption Fund of Ten Shillings per
centum will be provided annually.
Act Xb. 1982 Treasury Bonds 3-?* per
cent., maturing Ist April, 1916 £1,248,500,
of which .... £1,060,000
is offered for conversion.
Applications will be received at the Revenue and
Pay Office, Treasury, Melbourne, until the Ist day of
April, 1915, from Holders of Hi per cent. Treasury
Bonds due Ist April, 1916, for the surrender and
conversion of such Bonds into—
(a) Four per cent. Victorian Government De-
bentures under Act Xo. 2026. bearing date
Ist April, 1915, and due Ist April, 1927, or
(b) Four per cent. Victorian Government In-
scribed Stock under Act Xo. 2026, bearing
date Ist April, 1915, and maturing Ist
April, 1927.
Conversion will be at the rate of .£lOO of De-
bentures or Inscribed Stock for each £lOO of
Bonds surrendered, and will take effect from Ist
April, 1915.
Interest at Four per cent., free of State Income
Tax, will be payable on Ist April and Ist October
in each year, the first half-year’s interest to be
payable on Ist October, 1915.
Applicants must specify which Security is re-
quired, and in the case of Debentures the Denomina-
tion desired.
Stock may be inscribed in the name of—
(1) Sole proprietor;
(2) Joint proprietors not exceeding four (4) ;
(3) Corporations.
In case of joint proprietors the principal can be
dealt with only on the signatures of all the joint
holders.
Interest may be paid—
fa) to the proprietor on application at the Treas-
ury or at any Treasury Office in Victoria;
(h) by cheque posted to the proprietor’s registered
address;
(c) by payment into the proprietor’s account in a
bank at Melbourne.
Applications addressed to the Honorable the Trea-
surer, accompanied by the Bonds with the interest
coupons for the unexpired term attached, must be
lodged at the Treasury, Melbourne, on or before the
date named.
Receipts will be given for Bonds lodged, for con-
version, and pending the delivery of Debentures or
inscription of Stock the rights held under the
receipts may be transferred either wholly or in part
on application at the Treasury.
Members of recognised Stock Exchanges of Aus-
tralasia will be allowed Brokerage of One-half per
cent, upon all conversions applied for through them.
Brokerage will be payable on lodgment of the Bond-.
Forms of Application may be obtained at the
Revenue and Pay Office, State Treasury, Melbourne,
where a copy of the authorising Act may be in-
A. J. PEACOCK.
The Treasury, Treasurer of Victoria.
Melbourne, 2nd March, 1915.
SPORTING
NOTIONS
It is hard to explain why the Australian
Gup in the past 16 years has only twice been cap-
tured by a horse of the best class. Up to 1900,
when La Carabine got liome, the winner was a top-
noteher as often as not. Five successive winners
were Malua, Trident, Carlyon, Loehiel and Dread-
nought. Bobadil (1899) was the last of the brilliant
three-year-olds to win the race; and Tartan, who
was a five-year-old when he carried 9st. 51b. to the
fore, was one of the “quality.” But since Tartan
the 10 winners have, with the exception of The
Parisian, been handicap horses that never got a
reputation as first-class performers. This year there
was nothing of proved or probable excellence in the
field save De Gama, and lie, giving a lot of weight
to the other 15 starters, just failed to get a place.
Lempriere, the six-year-old gelding from America,
with only 7st. 41b. to stop him, took the lead as soon
as they got going and kept it to the end of the long,
long journey. His time was good enough, considering
the wind that blew across the course, but he never
put in any great work. He ran to the front and was
the stayer who stayed there, whilst AVishing Cap,
starting very slowly as usual, was the stayer who
worked his way from the tail end of the field into
second place at the finish. AVhen there were still
two miles to go Wishing Cap was any number of
lengths behind the leader, and lie couldn’t make up
the ground in the 31 minutes left for racing.
So Lempriere won by a length, without being
pressed for a final effort. Which was just as well;
for probably there was no superfluous steam left in
him. The Yankee had won. his race in the first
furlong, whereas AVishing Cap could only win the
second prize ( £500) in the last 100 yards. No
matter how long the journey the gee that can come
along from the start has a big pull over the slow be-
ginner.
The Bourke Handicap (seven furlongs), run on
Australian Cup day, is a chance for the sprinters
that have had a bit, or two bits, of bad luck in the
Newmarket. Traquette was one of the unfortunates
on the previous Saturday, and Golden AVire also got
a “rather bad run.” They finished first and second
in the Bourke Handicap, beating, among others, Gigan-
dra, Philio, Amator, Dhul’dul, and Miss Meadows, also
Achernar, who ran no better than before. lownit
was the only one of the Newmarket stars that came
out for the longer sprint, and the extra furlong and
51b. extra weight seemed to stop that honest battler
when the pinch came.
The results of certain races on the first
three days of the V.R.C. meeting added fuel to the
enthusiasm with which the rank and file of V.R.C.
members voted for the totalisator at last Friday’s
gathering of the Club. The “boil over” for the
Loch Plate, for instance, had made a lot of soreness,
and there was no ointment in the press announce-
ment that Land of Song’s jockey when questioned
by the stipendiary stewards “admitted that be rode
a bad race.” AVhat sort of race the jockey of
Carlita thought he hue}, ridden was not stated, though
its “badness” was ecjpally apparent to. the crowd.
The betting on the Lobh Plate may, of course, have
been influenced by the news,. that Mr. Norman Fal-
kiner, the owner of the favorite, wanted to scratch
his gee after the scratching time had expired, and
was refused permission by the V.R.C."stewards. Any-
how, Land of Song’s price went from 4 to 1 on to
5 to 2 on before the start, notwithstanding his satis-
factory showing in the preliminary canter. Evi-
dently the owner’s misgivings about the animal’s
fitness to run were not justified. The race was an
extraordinary opportunity for the pacemaker.
Ilayston was really the sorest horse in the field, and
he couldn’t gallop for nuts until he had gone a mile
or more. One has to presume that the experienced
riders of the “waiting” horses didn’t know how
slow they were going. Haystdn gradually shook
off his soreness and improved his pace, and then
Carlita, apparently, was unable to recover more than
five of the lengths which had been conceded to the
slow leader. Land of Song overtook Carlita and
made a belated effort to reach Ilayston, but the
despised outsider had a neck to the good. as they
passed the post. One bookmaker, not the biwest
of the fraternity, won £3900 over the Loch Plate
(four starters) and £5OOO in all on his day’s
betting. Some of his clients, no doubt, were among
the crowd of A r .R.C. members who carried a Tote
resolution next day. ■
The conclusion to be drawn from the two w.f.a.
races he might have won with different handling is
that Land of Song is at present the best horse in
Australia at any distance beyond a mile—presuming
that his disposition lias not been, soured. A willing
horse will not remain amiable if be is repeatedly
“messed about.” Indiscreet riding has tliusfar cost
liis owner a lot of stake money, not to mention the
backers and their many thousand quids
However, the poor books did very nicely out of
the w.f.a. racing at the meeting. On the first
day the St. Leger and Essendon Stakes winners
were favorites at two to one’ on, and 10 to nine
on respectively, but . the beaten second favorites
were freely backed at only ■ three to one and 10 to
nine against. Lesbos, the odds-on favorite for the
Sires Produce Stakes, was beaten. On the second
day there was only one w.f.a. race, and the odds-on
favorite was beaten. In the C. M. Lloyd Stakes
(third day) the books laid five to four Mountain
Knight, seven to two Blague, and nine to two Jolly
Beggar, and they were all beaten. In the Loch
Plate the betting'was five to two on Land of Song,
seven to two against Carlita and 12 to one Pistoi
King, after four to one had been laid on the
favorite; and they were all beaten. There was one
w.f.a. race on the fourth day, the market being
seven to four De Gama, two to one Land of Song,
and seven to one Bon Ton at the start, though the
punters had mostly invested their money at five to
four and six to four on one or other of the hot
favorites. And those three horses were beaten. An
immense proportion of the money wagered at the
meeting must have been profit to the poor books
who are now, with the warm approval of trainers,
lookers and sharps, raising a big Anti-Tote AVar
Fund.
When Warstep won the Dunedin (M.L.)
Gup the other day, carrying 9st. 1.31 b., she lumbered
the biggest weight that any winner has ever carried.
The record has hitherto been held by Lurline, who won
away back in 1874 with 9st. 91b. Only on five previous
occasions has a racer burdened with 9st. or over
been successful in the event; yet, in spite of her big
load, she went out a hot favorite. Her mother. Step-
dancer, won the same race twice in succession—in
1906 and 1907. The daughter has put up a fine
record this season. She ran a dead heat with Indigo
in the M.L. Cup and won the Auckland and Dunedin
Cups.
* * * *
Tlie racers in Westralia this season must
be a moderate lot if Dollar Dictator, the latest Perth
Cup winner, is one of the best. The imported
may be very good for his size, but he is only a pony, and
not a thick-set pony at that. In the Australian Cup
race it seemed absurd for fine big lumps of horseflesh
like AVishing Cap and Lempriere to be receiving
weight from Dollar Dictator. The little ’un
struggled along gamely under his Bst. —which wa3
not more than his AVestralian performances had
earned —but probably he couldn’t have won if he
had met the big ’uns on 141 b. better terms. Pre-
vious _ AVestralian champions in Jolly Beggar and
Artesian impressed one with their size when they
name along as strangers; but D.D. suggested that a
couple of hard bumps would settle his chance under
Any conditions.

A record for the Wanganui (M.L.) Cup
was established;the other day by Master Lupin (6st.
81b.). He made the trip, one mile and three-
quarters, in Smin. 0 3-ssee.—about a second better
than the previous best.

Another record for the Quaking Islands
was shattered last week by C. G. Dalgety’s profitable
AVarstep, one of the best mares seen on a M.L. course
for a lopg time. Recent victories in the South
brought her winnings up to £4650 —the biggest
amount in a season by any horse in M.L. To date
AVarstep has won £7570.
A. E . IT ARCY, Captain of the Australian
Golf Club.
Harold Hardwick, who had already won
some fame and several silver ornaments as an
amateur boxer, became a professional at Sydney
Stadium on Saturday night. The first thing he did
was to shove his left violently under the nose of Les
O’Donnell, bang him hard on the jaw with the right,
and welt him three times more on the same place (or
thereabouts) before the round ended. O’Donnell
went to his corner with a badly damaged face and.
the crowd began to consider the quickest wax- of
catching a tram. But, contrary to these early ex-
pectations, the disturbance raged over the whole
20 rounds, partly because O’Donnell is a tough pro-
position, but mainly because Hardwick boxed at
times wildly and didn’t know enough to finish liis •
man when be had him at his mercy. Many of his
blows were badly timed and liis attention too much
directed to O’Donnell’s head. The ninth round
should really have ended matters. O’Donnell, who
had been given a fair dose of punishment up to that
stage was severely assaulted in this round and only
survived through Hardwick’s slackness at the critical
moment. So the pair boxed on, O’Donnell with the
greater resource, Hardwick with the more skill.
The ex-amateur got the verdict and deserved it.
Both men were very tired at the end, Hardwick
particularly so.
Taking all things into consideration—the fact
that it was Hardwick’s first ensrae-ement with a pro-
fessional, that he was not as hard as he might be,
that the whole environment was novel, and that 20
rounds of stubborn fighting is mighty hard work —
Hardwick did very well and may confidently be ex-
pected to show considerable improvement later on.
The best that can be said about him now is
that be is a promising recruit, who on the first test
showed exceptional coolness. On the other hand, lie
doesn’t look the fighting animal; opposed to an
opponent having a more dexterous left than
O’Donnell he would certainly meet with many rude
remonstrances.
Hardwick, as an amateur athlete, spread
himself over many branches of sport, and his tracks
are plainly visible. In the swimming bath he won
the 100yds., 220yds., 880yds. 1320yd5., and mile
N.S.W. championships, and the 100yds., 220yds.,
440yds., 880yds. Australian championships. For
many years lib shared with Cecil Healy the 100yds.
record of 57sec. With the gloves Hardwick over-
shadowed the amateurs of his day, and, besides
being master of Australian heavyweights, he thumped
the world’s honors out of Hazell, champion of Great
Britain, a few years ago in London, in 2min. 33sec.;
and Julius Thompson, Canadian champion, on the
same occasion in 2min. 35sec. The lusty Australian
weighs 12 stone in his bones and is but 25 years of
age.
* * * 7.
There was a casual and abrupt encounter
on Saturday night at Brisbane, between Australian
Jerry Jerome and the African boxer Storbeck. Hav-
ing got the preliminary first round over, our aborig-
inal brother, studiously intent on being discourteous,
slammed the guest of the evening on the jaw with
his bony left. The said guest went down wallop.
Simultaneous with the thud, the referee commenced
to call the count. Storbeck rose at two, but appar-
ently it was merely to bow his acknowledgments, as
he promptly resumed liis lowly seat and, of course,
was disqualified. The crowd seemed to be annoyed,
judging by the uproar that ensued,
* * * *
N. S. Wales’ speediest cyclists tore away
with the two championship events during the first
night’s racing at the Sports Ground, Sydney, under
the management of the N.S.AV. Cyclists’ Union. S.
Burtinshaw won the mile and A. F. Taylor the five
miles. The mile was run off in four heats and final,
and starters included representatives from Victoria,
Queensland and Tasmania. Of the heats, N.S.W.
riders were first and second in each, excepting the
third, which was won by A. McDonald, of Victoria.
Burtinshaw vanquished competition in the final in
2min. 24 2-ssec. C. Hooper (N.S.W.) was second.
McDonald had the bad luck to be brought down when
he was going well 150yds. from home. For the five-
miles go there were 12 starters, and they rode bunched
the whole way, pacing prizes going, for the most
part, to the Queenslanders. Taylor whipped past the
judge first by half a wheel. McDonald (Vic.), E. T.
Byron (N.S.AV.) and Burtinshaw (N.S.W.) followed
in that order, with merely inches separating them.
Time, 13min. 9sec. There was another compressed
finish in the'half-mile inter-State handicap. The
sprint of W. J. Riordan (Q.) was like the prompting
of conscience or the wings of lightning. He flew
past a winner by a few inches from F. Kaltenbaek
(N.S.AV.) and M. AV. McMahon (N.S.AV.). The
dash, lasted 58 4-ssec.
* * * *
In winning the mile championship Burtin-
shaw brought his total of Australian cycling cham-
pionships to eight, thereby equalling the record
hitherto held by Percy Berry, of Queensland. Berry
crossed the “retirement” mark years ago, but, old
Mother Rumor notwithstanding, the N.S.AV. pedaller
looks like keeping busy long enough to land another
track “best.”
* * * *
The ancient smell that lingered round the
term “amateur” in connection with pedal-cycling is
arising in Victoria now from the motor-cycling- body.
Riders whose living is earned from the trade are
being allowed to appear upon the tracks against
competitors who are there solely for the sport.
Naturally, jthe so-called amateurs from the trade shops
Have ready means of gaining advantages over ordinary
riders, and this unfairness can only tend to disin-
cline o.r’s to take part in competitions. Control-
ling clubs are invited to. figure ouj for themselves
what that will mean eventually.

The first section of the N. S. Wales
Amateur Athletic Association’s championship compe-
titions at Ashfield (Sydney) on Saturday was satis-
factory evidence that the spring has not gone out
of Ma’s youth nor the muscle become slack in her
younger men. Some of the battles - were strenuous
and stirring. Even a glutton for excitement had a
satisfying serve in the quarter-mile championship
and the mile-walk championship. The former was
the glass which reflected the speed brilliancy of A.
F. Thorpe (South Sydney Harriers). In liis heat he
led the way home in 53 4-ssec. Forty minutes later
he fled over the distance, in the final, in the rattling
time of 50 3-ssec. He had hardly got his bellows
in working order again (a 20 minutes’ spell) when
he lined up for the mile championship and won with
ease in 45 l-ssec. The mile-walls provided a great
tussle between A. AV. Pickard (of " the police) and
H. T. Dent (South Sydney Harriers). The former
was the holder of the- "title and just managed to
keep it. Six min. 57sec. Other championships
contested were 100yds. sprint, snapped by G. E.
Hansel (East Sydney A.A.C.) with half a yard to
spare in 10 2-ssec. ; putting the shot, AV. J. O’Reilly
(Police A.A.C.), 38ft. 7}in.; running high jump,
R. Sidman (E.S.A.A.C.), sft. Sin. ; 120yds. hurdles,
J. AV. Fraser (S.S.H.), 16 2-ssec.; throwing the
discus, F, J. Morgan (E.S.A.A.C.), 101 ft. 5 5-Bin.;
and running hop, step and jump, H. D. Hinchey
(S.S.A.A.C.), 41ft. 6in. The Decathlon events were
a disappointment. • •T.'"'Maher (E.5.A.A.0.) is lead-
ing from the only other two athletes who signed
on for the contract of disclosing the all-round
champion. He won the running broad jump, 100yds.
and pole vault. AV. Biden (Y.M.C.A.), a novice,
was first in hurling the javelin. It was picked up
131 ft. 6in. away. Maher threw into second place
—l2sft. llfin. "

Victorians are cheering up over the find
of professional athlete R. McKenzie, who at Sheppar-
ton, recently, arrived first in an 880yds. handicap
(from 38yds.) in lmin.. 49sec., and second in a mile
(from 25yds.) in 4min. 15 3-ssec. —which is excellent
travelling. Money is behind a venture to include
McKenzie in the triangular match over 880yds.
between AV. F. Trembath and A. Dormer at Auck-
land (M.L.) ; and unless the watches were sleeping
at Shepparton, the Victorian Ought to be adjacent
when the cotton is brbken.
**: * *
Queensland’s long agitation to be allowed
to take the field in the [Sheffield Shield contests wasn’t
furthered by its team’s showing in inter-State cricket
this season. N.S.W., with a half-powered outfit,
defeated, the Northerners at Brisbane and Sydney,
and Victoria passed them out on the home ground.
One excuse that the Bhnanalanders can advance for
their weak play is that when they develop a strong
arm —such, for instance, as that of Ironmonger or
McLaren—-the Southern States seduce it. It’s a
pity, because evenly-balanced State elevens would
tend to revive interest in a game which for want of
it is fainting.
* * \ ' *• '• .* V ~
In Adelaide district cricket, two districts
are exactly equal after playing eight matches, the
reputedly unbeatable Eajst Torrens, who beat the
Adelaides, having lost to Port Adelaide at each
time of meeting. Ports are now a good third to the
other two named, who tie for top place. This is
all for the good of the game, the on-topness of "East
Torrens during the-past few seasons having tended to
become as monotonous as in the old days of the same
club, when it was called Norwood, and had Lyons,
Blinman and both Giffens in its team.
Some bright items from the ruck of Syd-
ney first-grade cricket on Saturday. Batting —Collins,
111 for Sydney against Balmain, 64 from boundary
wallops; McLean, 111 (coincidence that the two top
tallies of the day should be 111) for Gordon against
Glebe; Chamberlain, 93 not out, for North Sydney
against University—a dashing and stylish exhibition;
K. V. Minnett, his club mate, who contributed /7
before being run out; trifling scores of 40, 50 and
60 were made all over the- fields. Bowling—One hat
trick, by Randell, for Middle Harbor against Central
Cumberland; Coffey, four for 15, on behalf of Sydney
and against Balmain; Grimmett, who assisted t o
make things miserable for Balmain to the tune of
four for 55; Dupain, for Western Suburbs, four
Waverley residents at a cost of 35.
* * * *
,T. N. Crawford, now earning a crust as a
cricket coach at Dunedin (M.L.), has brought along
in first-grade play a team called The Colts, made
up of promising young players drawn from all the
clubs in the whusky and haggis metropolis. So far,
this lot, which Crawford captains, lias had a vivid
record, for out of 14 matches played it has won 13
right out, and drawn one. No need to be vehement
in saying that it is leading for the championship
—points, 27; nearest rival, 21. A lot of this
success, of course, is due to the howling of Crawford,
who generally ends up the afternoon’s work with
six or seven wickets for between 20 and 30 runs,
in fact, the slump in timber since he went into the
business is only equalled by the slump in Maoriland
pawnshop prospects.
*• * *
The rival sculling camps on the Parra-
imitta River —Kemp’s fit Abbotsford, and Towns’s at
Gladesville —are exchanging vigorous challenges. A
match between Alf Felton and Charlie Towns, and
another between Syd Keinp and Ford are at the
signing-up stage; while the challenge of lvemp and
Miss Kemp to Pearce and Miss Pearce (holders) for
the mixed double-sculls championship, plus a purse
of £lOO, will be decided on _ April 10. Other marine
disturbances in the offing include Billy Ripley v.
Ben Tlioroughgood, for the State heavy-boat cham-
pionship; and George Paddon ri. George Jack. The
sculling business is merrier than it has been for years.
* * * *
“Alf’' O’Brien, secretary of Sydney
Musicians’ Union, has a camp on the shores of Botany
Bay, called Bikarena, in memory of the days
when its owner was a mighty pedaller—lie carried
off a Sydney Thousand once. A big event amongst
Botany Bay sailing men came off recently, and
O’Brien decided to be in it. He Secured a boat
which had been rotting on the rocks for some
months, sewed it together with string, put some
glue into it, and entered the contraption for the
race. The Botany fishermen laughed at the ancient
Bikarena, and gave it last chance in the procession
and the limit start. But the leaky affair, with the
bailer-out, the mo§t important member of the crew,
and Sydney’s premier harpist handling the strings
of the jib, sailed a winner.
• * * *
Maorilanders are claiming a bowls record
at Inverkegville. One A. Fyfe (a first-year player)
and A. S. Froggatt, in handicap doubles, scored 45
to 0 in 14 beads. Their opponents reckoned that
the time was ripe to have something cool with it,
and the game ended without further argument.
* * * •»
Comes another bowls achievement from
Maoriland —where the game is more popular than
anywhere else in Australasia. In the wide-spread
Wellington district the mother club is named the
“Wellington,” and from it have sprung a large
family. For years past one Of the great days is
that upon which the. parent club provides a rink to
meet a representative l'ink from every one of the
younger, associations. This year 16 teams from
both sides met on a recent Saturday and the old ’un
won by a margin of 133. This is the eleventh year
in succession that ma lias beaten the children, and
she says if it isn’t something in the way of a
world’s record, things are not what a body has a
right to expect.

Something refreshingly novel in the way
of statuary has been erected by the A.W.U.
in the cemetery at Stuart Mill (Vic.) over
the grave of Donald Macdonell, ex-Cliief
Secretary in the N.S.W. Government, and
general secretary of the big union. The
monument consists of a huge 10-ton mass of
Harcourt granite in the rough. The inscrip-
tion on a bronze medallion tells Maedonell’s
story briefly, and concludes with Roderic
Quinn’s lines: —
He has mounted his horse and smiled farewell,
And rides where the shadows fall.
Honor him, men of the Western sheds,
Honor him, honor him all.
“Y.Z.”: Since the days of poor, plucky
little _ Judkins what may be called gas
morality has been somewhat neglected in
Melbourne; but recently another exponent
of the gospel has cropped up. He is carry.
ing on his crusade in a small paper of pious
tendencies and on public platforms wherever
suburban opportunity offers. His cry is
‘‘more light!” the idea being that the Devil
is to be outed by gas jets, and that the
way to Heaven is illuminated by vapor
which passes through a meter, All our
sands, Paddocks, and waste places are there-
fore to be flooded with light, and iniquity,
which goetli in darkness, will leave for the
hills. All that is necessary to convert even the
camp at Broadmeadows into an Eden, minus
apples and reptiles of all sorts, is a more
liberal application of gas. In short, the
main road to Paradise is the gasmain, and
the Bright and Shining Light so frequently
referred to in Scriptures is obtainable at
about 4s. per 1000 ft.

Dunlop Rubber Company, Melbourne
Dear Sir, —You will be interested to know that a little more than two
years ago, I had my 2 % h.p. motor cycle shod with a pair of your 26 x 2% in.
covers and tubes. They have given me the greatest satisfaction. One cover
and tube has run over 11,000 miles without a puncture. They give promise
of many a journey to come. During the period mentioned, the tyres have
travelled over some of the roughest roads in Victoria and New South Wales,
including a trip via Warburton over Mt. Matlock through Wood’s Point
along the valley of the Goulburn to Mansfield, and back through Kingdom
Gap and Healesville to Melbourne. I have had several runs to Geelong,
Ballarat, and other towns, including Kyabram ; and last week I travelled the
great overland route from Sydney to Melbourne
without a single tyre or tube trouble. As my
machine has to carry nearly 15 stone weight,
I think this fact, coupled with the details
given herein, is the strongest evidence as to the
durability of your Company’s workmanship.
Yours sincerely,
Rev. HENRY WORRALL.

“0.K.”: After the Australian Cup race
nmeh the same nasty things were said as when
Posinatus won the Melbourne Cup, the violent
eleventh-hour backing of Lempriere being adduced
as evidence in support of the nasty things. In short,
Rumor, who sometimes speaks in a raucous voice
and carries a black bag, declared that, whilst the
nags that would have beaten Lempriere couldn’t do
it, some that might have done so wouldn’t try.
Much the same influences that operated in the Posin-
atus case, it was said, were responsible for the end-
to-end victory of Lempriere. Rumor, the offspring
of Suspicion—Disappointment, is often an awful
liar, but it imparts so vile a stink to horseracing,
and the name of the alleged head stinker is so
notorious, that the - V.R.C. stewards can no longer
affect ignorance of the odor. They really stand
charged with permitting the lare-e majority of the
patrons of their show to be hoodwinked and plun-
dered by a little clique of wire-pullers whose high
priest is undoubtedly very astute. The Tote would
do much—if not everything—to ensure better condi-
tions for the public, quite apart from the fact that it
pavs a dividend on the second horse. No schemer,
however astute, can get the “cream” of the Tote
market, seeing that the market yields no cream. The
Tote registers no bogus bets for the bamboozling of
punters, nor does it bet, bogusly or otherwise, on
the horses whilst they are running. Bookmakers,
who sometimes do a bit of roguery on their own
account, are oftener the feeders of roguery, outside
punters and sanguine liorseowners being the producers
of the food which the books distribute. The Tote
would rid the racecourse of bookmakers and give
the public two chances to get a draw after each
race.
* * * *
In the grandstand reserve at Flemington,
just before the Australian Cun was run, two one-
legged men were noticed walking tog'ether and a
superstitious punter (not that any are non-
superstitious) wanted to know the prophetic mean-
ing of it. What tip did the two timber-pronped
sports suggest? Somebody’s inspiration was Winni-
peg, and the man who was looking for signs and por-
tents went and dropped his sugar on that animal.
The much-fancied Naxberry, which missed
the ’bus at the V.R.C. Autumn meeting, got his
fancy name this way. His owner and trainer, Burn,
rode Grafnax and Briberrv in their palmy days; so as
a moniker for the Positano colt, he joined the tail
end of the names of the famous jumpers.
* * * *
Edward Cutts —“old Ted” in the affec-
tionate memory of all Maoriland racing men—
weighed out for the Final Stakes the other day at
the home near Christchurch from which he had led
winners and losers for well nigh half a century. A
Maitland (N.S.W.) boy, Cutts took service with
Henry Redwood, away back in the dim ages of the
past, and crossing to M.L. was for many years
associated with that fine old man in training and
racing. Later he set up as a public trainer at
Riccarton and never lacked patronage, for Cutts
was a horsemaster of rare skill and a man of sterling
honesty. For a generation or so he trained for
George Clifford, and the relationship only ended
with death. The best horses Cutts ever trained
were probably Mata and Sir Modred. The best he
ever owned personally was Dudu. He brought Nelson
to Australia and saw that good, honest galloper
just snuffed out by Trident in the Australian Cup.
Another Cutts—but that was “Johnny”—rode for
John Tait in the N.S.W. Leger in 1847, and had
the mount on Archer in the first two Melbourne
Cups.
* * * *
In the midst of clamoring for athletes to
.quit, the playing fields and get into the Great Line,
S. M. Herald recently published a cablegram which
bracketed the deaths of General Gough, V.C., in
Flanders, and Kenneth Powell, at Ypres. In expla-
natory notes it was set out in five inches of close
type that Powell was one of England’s best tennis
players and a hurdler of some renown. Gough was
mentioned in five lines as having fought for the
Empire in three continents and won the highest
decoration for valor. The Herald’s interpretation
of V.C. is, apparently, Very Commonplace.
*• * *■ *
Much of the recent success of Britain’s
Flying Corps is due to the new “tabloid” bi-plarie
now in use. It is a very light, small affair, looking
like a dragon-fly, and not a great deal bigger ; but it
carries a well-protected 80h.p. engine and can do
up to 114 miles per hour. This gives it pace and
to spare over Germany’s Taube ’planes, and makes
it very difficult for anti-aircraft guns to hit.
* * * *
Despite the fact that the laudable craze
for drilling threatens to jostle such game-players as
have not listened to the call from Europe, N.S.W.
Rugby League is preparing its winter programme
with vigor. Additional grounds are being sought
and the grade competitions are to be even more
encouraged by subsidy this season than last. Also,
teams from Maoriland “land Queensland will assist
the industry. Last year £26,700 passed through
the hands of the League, which is £7OOO better
than the record of 1913. The chief incomes were
£12,426 “gate” from the Northern Union tour in
Australia, and £10,798 from the competition turn-
stiles. A profit of £1757 was made on the English-
men’s tour. The officials reckon it would be a pity
to waste money that lies around loose like that.
Incidentally about 130 League toeballers have thrown
away the jersey for the soldier’s tunic.
* » * *
During 18 years of district football (Aus-
tralian rules) in South Aus. the Port Adelaide team
has been six times premiers, eight times runners-up
and three in the third position. Last season its
senior combination booted through all matches (14
in the State competitions, one against the Victorian
champions, and one against the combined repre-
sentatives of six other S.A. clubs) without defeat.
That performance stands on its own ; but to glorify
it “Ports” had an average of over eight goals per
match to spare. Finally, in the glorious 1914 sea-
son, the team’s goal tally reached double figures in
13 out of 14 matches. Hats off!
THE IRREDEEMABLE TOTE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
And when all is said the totalisator must be ad-
mitted to be quite as mischievous as the bookmaker.
What harm can the latter do the individual or the
community that the machine is not capable of' doing ?
—Presbyterian parson, opposing the tote in a sec-
tarian paper in Melbourne.
The tote is a malignant thing,
- Its morals most infernal.
You'll see this engine vile, a-wing
At evil to outdo the Ring,
Depicted in a journal
Devqted as a rule to drones
fn pulpits, and to musty bones,
And tea, and toast, and currant scones.
The tote will dog our children’s feet,
And head them off from Zion ;
The elders it will slyly cheat—
Far better for a saint to meet
The usual ravening lion.
’Twill bribe the jockey on the job,
Delude the average man, and rob
The smartest Alee and the yob.
’Twill lure the owners on to lose
By many cunning courses;
’Twill make its way by subtle ruse,
And with the drugs that sharpers use
Will hocus helpless horses.
The vilest book, when all is done,
Has fear of the Hereafter, son—
Alas! the vicious tote has none!
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.

Rheumatism, Lumbago, Stone, Gravel,
and other Kidney & Bladder Troubles.
It seems as though a wave of desolation and destruction is sweep-
ing over the whole of Australia. Letters have been received during
the last .week from places as far apart as Port Macquarie. N.S.W.,
I Premantle, W.A., as well as hundreds of other smaller towns
; and villages in Australia.
Almost every one of these letters asks for
information. Every one of these people writes
asking what is the meaning of these aches
and pains in various parts of the body, which
everybody seems to be suffering from. Other
people are complaining of stiffness of joints
and acute backache, with a nasty taste in the
mouth first thing in the morning, constipa-
tion, puffiness under the eyes, and a general
feeling of weakness and irritability. In some
cases these pains have become so bad that
they have changed into specific complaints,
such as Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago.
Sciatica, Cystitis (Inflammation of the Blad-
der), Stone and Gravel. Every one of those
people is the victim of a disease which is
rapidly becoming the curse of the country—
kidney disease.
This fell complaint is carrying on its awful
work in every part of Australia to-day,
and hundreds of people are dying every week
who should be. alive and well, if thev had onlv
taken ordinary precautions. Surely' the above
is an index as to how serious kidney trouble
is if it is neglected. Finally, it becomes too
late, for kidney disease is often the forerunner
of deadly Bright's, Disease, which, as every-
body knows, is fatal in, almost every case.

“Hornet” : I lent my only surviving watch
to my wife to-day, and, needless to remark,
she dropped it. It was only the fourth she
had smashed within 12 months; which was
no doubt why she told me.l was a perfect
brute when I suggested that we’d better tell
the time iu future by a sun-dial. Going into
town, I met my neighbor the jeweller, and,
seeking for comfort, asked him if all women
were as clumsy. “Clumsy !” lie shouted, and
made the typist in the next seat jump.
“Clumsy! Good Heavens, yes!. If a woman
comes into my shop and buys a watch, its
odds-on she’s back with it inside a week.
You know what she’s done—she’s shoved it
off the mantelpiece. But you can’t tell ’em
it’s their fault —that would ruin business.
No, you’ve just got to listen to their rot and
then give ’em a new watch. 1 reckon there s
only one thing that's clumsier than a woman,
and that’s a cow !”
* * •» . *
“Caught Again”: Some of these hawkers
seem to know all the points in the game. I’ve
just been shown one. A few minutes after
the wife went out the other morning, while
I mended tin* fowdhouse in peace, a hard-
faced person appeared and announced that
“the lady” had directed him to leave some
vegetables. Being told to produce tin* goods
the li.f.p. disappeared, to stagger back a few
minutes later with a sack of potatoes. Fear-
ing the missus had gone daft I expostulated,
but was assured that there was "no ipistake.
Next the dealer rolled in three pumpkins,
and, after wiping the sweat off his face,
wheeled up a miscellaneous collection of peas,
onions and cabbages. Finally he opened his
countenance and remarked “Seventeen bob.
Cursing such unprecedented extravagance,
for we’ll never get through the pile of pro-
duce now on hand even by eating hard three
times a day. I paid the ruffian off, only to
learn soon after, from the expostulations cf
an angry girl, how neat the take-down had
been. Wliat she had done was to order three
shillings’ worth, but the smart Alec with
the ornate cart, encountering a simpleton m
old pants repairing a chicken run, sized up
the possibilities with great accuracy. Im
not grumbling, but if anyone happens to have
a bulldog with a taste for vegetable hawkers
I’m a cash buyer.
“Floradora packing” was introduced to
the Victorian Fruit Commission by a witness
who knew the business. Having received a
consignment of fruit, the agent hires experts
to repack it so that he has six cases for
every five lie has received. He pays the
grower, of course, for five cases, and the
grower is no wiser. That is Floradora Pack-
ing, and it makes everybody happy, especially
the agent.
•* * *
“Milo”: The manufacture of casein is
another Australian possibility that was flat-
tened out by the war. A factory was estab-
lished at Byron Bay (N.S.W.), where the
article was manufactured from butter-milk -
the only place in the world where that waste
product was successfully utilised for the pur-
pose, it was said. The future of the mdustiy
looked shiny, because butter-milk could be
ha'd almost for the taking away. But Ger-
many was the only buyer; and since Arma-
geddon started there has been no market at
all. Casein is used to deceive the public by
making it believe that the keys of its im-
ported German-made piano are of pure ivory,
and that the handles of its cutlery are of the
same material. Some day this country will
build all its own pianos and make all its
own cutlery, and then it will be independent
of Germany for a market for its compressed
butter-milk. Just now, however, the infant
is lying unconscious, and the milk that it
should be feeding op is being pumped out into
Byron Bay.
. * * *
“By Gum”: On: the subject of the box-
making “ring” in . Victoria there is some
misconception. Fruit eases are always made-
from off-cuts; if there was any money in
cutting the cases direct from log timber
plenty of the hardwood mills would have
taken it on long ago. If the orehardistswant-
to get even with the ring let them do as
the cow cookies did in N.S.W. —put up a
factory of their own. The ring will fight, of
course, but it only remains for the consumer
to keep loyal to his own co. and Pirate
Enterprise will come round with a cordial
invitation to call off the dog. The Co-op.
Box- Co. of N.S.W., Ltd., is turning out a
first-class butter case at a reasonable price;
and the local ring has sawn hunks off its
price list.

“.T.F.H.”: An advt. in London Times
offering “a good bonus for a Foreign Order’
has been attracting attention and evoking-
unfavorable comment. It has transpired
that there is—or was, before the war an
agency in Germany for the supply of these
Orders. It issued price-lists in the usual
commercial manner. The Order
was a Persian one —the “Sun of Nasr-ed-
din” —'Which could be bought for £25, and
entitled its purchaser to the prefix
“Chevalier.” The Turkish “Order of the
Osmanieh” could be got for £3OO. There
is no actual quotation list, but money enters
largely, if indirectly, into the acquisition of
British “Honors” also. In England. titles
are bought by large contributions to party
campaign funds. In Australia they have
been gqt by lending money to chronically
impecunious Premiers. An old-time Gover-
nor of Victoria was asked to recommend
squatter Samuel Wilson for a knighthood
on the ground of his services in the acclima-
tisation of salmon. “That’s not good
enough,” said his Ex.; “let him do some-
thing striking.” So the W ilson Hall of the
University of Melbourne came into being,
and the squatter got his K.C.M.G.
“Billbo”: I recently read in an English
newspaper that owing to the briskness m
the aeroplane-building industry there is a
great demand for Australian timbers witb a
high twisting efficiency. If that is the trut
let me introduce the hitherto-despised Blac
Sally” (Eucalyptus stettulata). This tine
ber's liability to warp, while a disadvantage
for many uses, may specially quality it roi
use in aeroplane construction, and also toi
building that other war-like implement, the
tennis racquet. Bushmen, not being banco
to aeroplane or tennis-racquet factories, as
a rule, use the wood as mere fuel.

“Moana” : As Mhoriland millers are com-
plaining bitterly about the “dirty” character
of the imported wheat, it looks as it the un-
happv country accepted a consignment ot
tribulation with the grain. One lot was
found to contain as mtich as < lb. of rubbish
per sack. Even in the most “dirty” wheat pro-
duced locally. 311 b. of rubbish is considered the
limit Several cheerful optimists have been
explaining that dirt doesn’t matter much in
wheat which has to be milled, but as a lot of
the stuff is being sold to farmers for use as
seed, there is likely to be an expansive crop
of new thistles, strange weeds _ and other
imported catastrophes blooming in the Ivicß-
etty Isles bv next summer. As the t ali-
"fornian thistle which over-runs big lumps ot
country,in the South Island was imported m
Massey should see to it that
all imported wheat; used for sowing is win-
nowed or graded first.

“Carl” : lie ambled into tbe studio wear-
ing ordinary clothes and a suit-case. He asked
for the dressing-room, and presently returned
arrayed in a bishop’s full-dress uniform-
silk'bands, tassels; everything except the
spurs. He was struggling with the collar.
“Can I help you?” I* asked. 2So thank
you,” he replied. “The d—d things too
small.” When I had got back on the rails
‘is it were, I murmured that it did me gocd
to hear a leader of the flock use human
language. He seemed surprised. 'block
l )0 d —d !” he remarked. When I had photo-
graphed him in three positions lie explained
that he belonged to Snip and Co., and 1 lie
photographs were to be sent to a distant
client. “We used to send out things like
this on appro.,” he said, “but we found that
frequently they were worn for a few days,
perhaps. ' and then returned. So now’ we
display the goods by photo. It’s just as good
for customers vdio mean business, and it s
better for us.”
CHIFFON’S PRIVATE ARMY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
W hen the British Empire'echoed with the ringing
call to arms,
It was Mister Caesar Chiffon —he that drapes the
female channs —-
Who arose and talked the loudest of “the Lion s
Austral cubs”
And a duty to the Empire nobly done in rifle clubs;
Then, he spoke of marksmen steady and the horror
of a foe
Who by trenches of Australians would be quickly
sent below,
Till the men of Chiffon’s counters, to the very last
galoot,
Were on fire to get some rifles and commence to
drill and shoot.
Now the colonels and the majors were as busy as
could be
Drilling soldiers for the export trade-in soldiers
oversea ;
And the rifle clubs were booming, also banging at
the butts,
Ho that no one paid attention to old Chiffon’s
martial knots ;
Till the smart old advertiser (here you’ll note the
master-rub!)
Formed with grave official sanction “Chiffon’s
Empire Rifle Club.”
Then for patriotic service he was lauded in the
press
Next to all the bleeding cables! What his joy was
you may guess!
But the genius of old Chiffon (in his time he’d
used the Flag
Till that symbol fine was cheaper than a housemaid’s
dusting rag)
Didn’t stop at minor triumphs. While his rivals
gnashed their ads.,
He arranged a morning muster of his valiant
draper-lads;
And he posed before them proudly on the city
training-ground,
While an unsuspecting colonel on inspection strolled
around.
Then, the stern proceedings over, “Captain” Chiffon,
stiff as starch,
Turned his little army shopward and commanded
them to “March!”
Down the streets they promenaded, keeping step
like soldiers true,
And the clerks and early shoppers to the kerbs and
corners flew,
While the drum kept tap-lap-tappuiy very bravely
in the lead ;
’Twould have made a stone-dead city' sit up straight
and take some heed.
“There go Chiffon’s rifle-clubmen,” said the people
as they passed,
And the Chiffon Imgler-eashboy blew a long and
stirring blast
On the brazen thing that dangled from the cord
about his chest,
As the swelling “Captain” muttered, “Well, of
ads., this is my best!”
There were followers in thousands as they drummed
and bugled by ;
“Captain’’ Chiffon breathed in spasms and a tear-
drop dimmed his eye.
“This is splendid (Keep in step there l ) This is
genius ! Lord, it’s fine 1
(Halt!)’ 1 lie stopped them and he dressed them
in a stiff, attentive line,
With their stern young faces staring at the useless
gauds that shone
In the windqws stuck with placards shouting 1
“Chiffon's sale now on.”
There's a moral hero for soldiers who distrust
commercial brag
And the tradesman’s trick of pinning little ads.
upon the Flag.

Cornflour Blancmange.
INGREDIENTS: I>4 tablespoonfuls cornflour, 3 tablespoonfuls
NESTLE’S CONDENSED MILK dissolved in % pint water.
METHOD: Mix the cornflour in a basin, with sufficient cold milk
to make a smooth paste. Put the remaining milk into a saucepan, when
boiling, pour on to the paste, return to the saucepan, boil for eight
minutes, and pour into a wetted mould. Serve with custard sauce.

QUEEN CAKES.
Ingredients —6 ozs. flour, 3 ozs. butter, 2 ozs. candied peel, I lemon rind, 2 tablespoonfuls Nestle’s Con-
densed Milk, mixed with I gill water, 3 ozs. sugar, 3 ozs. currants, 2 eggs, 1 teaspoonful baking powder.
Method —Sift the flour, add grated lemon rind, currants, and the peel chopped fine. Cream the butter and sugar,
and add the eggs and milk alternately with the dry ingredients. Well grease some moulds, and bake 13 minutes.
SULTANA SCONES.
Ingredients —I lb. flour, teaspoonful carbonate of soda, 1 teaspoonful cream of tartar, teaspoonful
salt, 2 ozs. butter, 4 ozs. sultanas, x /i pint Nestle’s Condensed Milk mixed with water (as per label on tin).
Method —Mix all the dry ingredients well together, rub in butter, add sultanas, and mix into a light dough
with the milk. Roll out, cut into scone shapes, brush over with egg, and bake in a hot oven.

ELECTRICITY’S HUNDRED USES.
Using Electricity for lighting only is like using a telephone in the evenings and
neglecting its daytime convenience.
Electricity has a hundred uses in addition to lighting—at the turn of a switch this mar-
vellous force produces a flameless heat in scores of heating and cooking devices like those
shown above. Electrically-heated cereal cookers, frying-pans, grids, boilers, hot-plate
ovens and ranges, are a few more of the many modern electrical conveniences manufac-
tured by the GENERAL ELECTRIC CO.
That the GENERAL ELECTRIC CO. has made it possible for you to cook by electricity
is due primarily to “Calorite,” a metal alloy which transforms electricity into heat as
effectively as the metal filament of the Mazda Lamps turns electricity into light.
“Calorite” only is used in the heating devices manufactured by this Company.
Write us for illustrated Bulletin, “Heating & Cooking.”

IN BANANA LAND
Extract from a letter written by a Bunda-
berg breach-of-promise plaintiff to her faith-
less swain:—
The Governor-General of Australia was here this
afternoon with his wife and party to tea, and he
took such an interest in me and wanted to know if
I were a Dane. He told Mrs. v? he admired my
face. He thought I had such afctfjee face. Of course
he is a married man, and I woirld rather he would not
when he is so much above me. I would
rather he was my own class; but he is not the
only man who thinks I have a pretty face. Mr.
Higgins also said I had a good face. But darn
them, they are no good to me. I don’t really believe
you have ever -told hne I had a nice face; but you
, have told me nicer things than that.
It is satisfactory to know that the
G.-G.’s (.or was it ex-G.-G.’sV) afternoon-tea
compliments, paid through a third party,
didn’t turn the young woman’s head. Re-
membering the : G.-G.’s married state and
aloof position, she bracketed him with
Mr. Higgins, and “darned” them both.
Brisbane jobbed away its street-tramway
rights and thought, because it reserved the
right to buy the works at market price at
the end of a certain period, that it had fully
protected itself. Certainly it is a good thing
that the co. will not be able to squeeze com-
pensation for “disturbance”; all the same,
In a recent Northern breach-of-promise
case the Judge alluded to the plaintiff as a
“bonny, wholesome-looking girl whom any
honest man might feel proud to make his
wife.” Of course she got a verdict. How
it would have gone with a pug-nosed young
woman with freckles and a squint Heaven
Only knows. Yet the bonny, wholesome-
looking girl is clearly less damaged by a
breach — any honest man would be proud to
make her his wife —whereas the freckled
derelict, having missed her one chance,
would probably be a derelict for ever.
W. H. Ryder, most courteous and able of
Queensland’s older Under-Secretaries, is
dead. He came from Prince Edward’s
Island and drifted nearly 50 years ago into
Queensland journalism, thence into the State
Printing Office, and finally into the Civil
Service. At a time when Bananaland’s
superior officials habitually snubbed mem-
bers of the new Labor party, Ryder won
the distinction of being the only Under-
secretary who treated them all with invari-
jable courtesy. If he, too, has not met with
la kindly welcome, something worse is known
of the old man up there than was ever
whispered about him down here.
From an interview with ,T. G. Appel, who
threw up the post of Home Sepretffyy and
Minister for Mines iir the Denham Ministry
last week :
I am not prepared to eat dirt all the days of
my life! It is not my nature to wait to be kicked
out!
The Premier has of late become a very autocratic
individual indeed. He has treated his colleagues
like junior clerks, and I hope in tendering- my resig-
nation that I have set an example to tliose_ .of the
party to which I belong who find themselves similarly
situated. /
Evidently the fear that something heavy
is going to fall on him is fraying' Denham at
the edges.
From London :- |
There are two bishops in Rockhampton—one Angli-
can, the other R.C. —and there’s going to be a row
when they meet again. The Anglican shovel-hat
came to London to see if he could pick up a casual
£30,000. He wanted the money partly to build a
hostel for “girls from the faf-away bush.” These
Church of England flappers come down to Rockhamp-
ton, it seems, for their education, and are dependent
on lodgings in the town; and as some of their
parents are suspicious of such lodgings, they send
their girls to the R.C. convent. Then: “The sisters
of this convent simply lay themselves out to w : ". the
girls over to their religion, and will take them for
less than half the money that is paid by Roman
Catholic parents.” Excusing himself for coming to
London to cadge money for building a boarding-
house for squatters’ daughters, our beloved brother
told a meeting of the Church Home, Westminster,
that Central Queensland produced a large amount of
wealth, but “it all went to England.” The rector
of Mount Morgan, who acted as echo to the Gaiters,
confirmed the statement. Here is another argument
in favor of a thumping absentee tax. With that or
some other means of persuading Australia to keep
for local circulation the wealth it produces, the Com-.
monwealth would be saved the ignominy of sending
its Church of England bishops on perpetual begging
expeditions to London.
The Rockhampton Harbor Board is call-
ing upon the Heavens to witness its abso-
lute stony-brokenness and how much it
deserves extended borrowing powers. The
sum of £832,495 has, it seems, been spent on
“improvements,” and as about half of that
total was borrowed money, fixed charges are
staggering. Interest and redemption cost
£18,500; management and. maintenance,
£10,000; deficiency on the Port Alma rail-
way, £B5OO. As a result of the millstone of
£32,000 a year which the “port” has tied
to its neck, the Fitzroy River has a depth
of 14ft. at low water; Port Alma flourishes
in the midst of a salt-pan where no one can
live; and all labor has to be taken down
from Rockhampton. The consequent cost
of handling cargo, especially if the boat does
not arrive until a couple of hours after it
is expected, soars out of sight, as all labor
has to be paid from the time of leaving
“Rockie” until it returns. The one bright
spot in the whole mad assortment of “har-
bors” owned by Rockhampton is Broad-
mount. At that place there is a splendid site
for a town, which is probably the reason
why it and its first-class wharves are prac-
tically desefted. Within a stone-throw of
this hopeless muddle and clash of money run-
ning to waste is Gladstone, which has the
finest natural harbor in Queensland, and one
of the best in Australia, but given over to
the dismal curlew and the riotous oyster.
Queensland has several such madnesses.
Bowen dreams on the shore of a splendid
natural harbor, while Townsville, next door,
is shovelling good hard coin into the ocean,
in the attempt to make a harbor out of a few
stones end a mudbank.
LUX TENEBRIS.
SHIFT BOSS: “ Here’s a candle, Mick. Thoust'll be wantin' it d’rectly, laad! ”
NEW HAND (about to go below for the first time): “ Share , an' what the divil would I
be a antin' a candle for, and it broad day?"
it is finding out that an arrangement under
which it buys the works at a tair valuation
can be a big curse too. The other day
manager Badger, at the opening of a new
line, frankly adjnitted that half a dozen
others ought to be built; but naturally the
co. will not build them and be out of
interest on the cost during the period of.
construction, and then work up a traffic
(probably at a loss) just for the sake of
handing them over to the “local authority.”
Badger knows he has a grip, and
is squeezing out concessions; and Brisbane
for its original folly will have to make a
bargain with him or go without a lot of
conveniences.
The Brisbane police recently called the
roll at a gambling room in that city, and
went through the pockets of those on parade.
The collection panned out something like
this: Jim, nothing; Sandy, 3d. ; Moseis,
£lB 7s. 9d. ; Bill, Is. 3d.; Tom, 9d.; Israel,
£27 11s. fid. and a two-headed penny.
Providence doth still watch over the Chosen.
There was a smell of a horrible under-
standing the other day when the Brisbane
Water and Sewerage Board considered ten-
ders for the year’s printing and bookbinding
and found no less than eight firms’ esti-
mates identical to a penny. The Govern-
ment Printer’s price was the lowest; but the
Treasurer “desired” that its tenders should
be withdrawn. Also the Government Printer,
having been “directed” to cease doing such
work for this semi-Government Board, told
the members that they would have to get
printing of certain plans (previously en-
trusted to him) done elsewhere. Naturally
the Board wants the biggest value for the
public’s money, so the Treasurer is to
be asked to reconsider the matter and let
the State office do the work. Meanwhile
the tenders are hung up, while Private
Enterprise smiles confidently and hangs
on to the strings.
“Itan”: Several of the members who sit
behind Denham are as wild as March flies
about the way they were dished lately.
At the beginning of the year a meet-
ing of the party was called to decide
whether Digby should continue to hold
the reins. It was just an ordinary
plain caucus. There were 22 malcontents.
Digby promised to reconstruct the Cabinet.
This implied the removal of several portfolio
holders. Hope of getting one or other of
these jobs placated the 22, and Digby was
put on the throne again. It now transpires
that there will be very little, if any, re-
shuffling, and as Digby has the vote of con-
fidence about his person he doesn’t bother.
But, all the same, the day of wrath
approaches. It will take more than _ dis-
qualification of the nomad to save the situa-
tion for the butterman.
“Politicus”: Some worthy people in
Southern Queensland are advocating the
abolition of the fairly numerous German
schools. I’ve lived in so-called German dis-
tricts and can truthfully say that the prin-
cipal effect of the German schools therein
is to breed a holy hatred of the Fatherland’s
language. Children of Deutscher parents
going to and from the local Deutscher school
never talk German, but use good Australian
all the time. When they quarrel their fav-
orite term of insult is “Squarehead” or
“Sausage.” Young Australians of German
parentage will brag of many things, .but
never of their Teuton blood.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, March 5, 1915.
My Bear Thargomindah,—
There has been an epidemic of matrimony
symptoms this week. Io Qualtrough, one
of Brisbane’s popular and ornamental
daughters, has accepted the usual sign of
her future monopolisation by Harry Benson,
a Sydney-sider whose business it is to buy
wool. A similar souvenir has been accepted
by Phyllis Webb, one of Dr. Webb’s many
chicks, at the instigation of Arthur Ward,
a doctor on the staff of Brisbane General
Hospital. The third incident of the kind
signed up Meta McKenny, daughter of tlie
late “handsome Charlie” McKenny (once a
well-known squatter), to Spencer Finlay,
whose father is Dr. Sinclair Finlay, of
Hunter’s Hill, Sydney. The prospective
groom encourages bullocks or sheep to sprout
and multiply in Queensland.
Victor Fitzherbert. for some time a well-
execrated villain at the Theatre Royal, and
now a member of the Buckler-Paget co., was
to-day united in wholly bondage to Dorothy
Gill, a pretty girl-export from Maoriland.
The breathless event of the week, socially
speaking, was the marriage of My Feez, only
child of Feez, K.C., and Mrs. Feez, to Jim
Henry, the mining man, whose sister, by the
way, is the handsome Mrs. Doyle. Veteran
Canon “Tommy” Jones secured the knot
in the little Anglican church at Indooroo-
pilly. The bride viewed the proceedings
from the sanctuary of a delightful fluffy
gown of white and silver and pink, and a
veil which was caught with a wreath of
tiny beb6 roses. Little Alison Cameron (her
cousin) and Miss Jean Ross Munro and
Ella Sydney Jones were the bridesmaids.
They graced pink and white crinoline-y
gowns, while wreaths and veils added to the
general effect or incidental picturesqueness.
Mr. Story and Eric Doyle kept the groom
alert throughout the performance. The
feast Was at the Feez home. Mr. and Mrs.
Henry are going to live in Sydney.
Among the host of well-dressed women at
I lie wedding, Mrs. Ross Munro was among
the best-upholstered. She blossomed in a
creation of gold satin and black lace, with
touches of rose on the corsage. It was the
gown she showed to Queen Mary at Court.
Her coat was of gold and silver brocade,
and her black-plumed hat would have kept
a Belgian refugee in cigars for a month.
Dorothy Curran, who three years ago
packed her fiddle and went to the London
Royal Academy of Music, returned to
Queensland by the Orsova last week. She
won an extension scholarship, but through
the Kaiser’s nastiness there was some hitch
which prevented the studies being gone on
with.
The Arts and Crafts Society of Queens-
land, which has begun its year’s work, will
hold its annual exhibition in September.
One of its aspirations is to be able to do
something in the line of the Women’s Emer-
gency Corps in England, and provide
employment for artistic and needy gentle-
women.
The Methodist Conference has been
shaking its cotton brolly at Satan all the
week, letting up on the Old Gentleman only
twice in order to attend At Homes. Ori
Thursday the kindred-souled W.C.T.U.
dashed to the front during another At Home
and begged all present to refrain from
“shouting” for soldiers. As the gathering
mostly consisted of doleful parsons and
frigid deaconesses there didn’t seem much
danger. Still, you never know.
A bunch of imported domestics arrived
this week, and were met by stranded house
wives struggling to hire them. But the
oversea Mary Anns were not to be caught
easy. They mostly refused to close with
offers until “some more came along.” Wages
of £1 and 255. weekly were demanded, with
a proviso that the miss.us should do the
washing. The sooner someone explains to
the State-subsidised aristocracy of the broom
and the dustpan that what we want out
here are people who don’t mind working for
their pay, the better for all concerned,
The Queensland Red Cross Society has
forwarded £2OOO to Lady Helen Ferguson,
who is stake holder for the British Red
Cross ditto. The week before, £650 was sent
along to buy a motor-ambulance.
President and Mrs. A'. W. Bale enter-
tained a party of guests at the Clayfield
bowling green to-day. Another frivolity was
a basket picnic up the river, organised by
the rowing clubs.
Yours ‘affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JAME.
The taking back of Ken Grant into the
_ , r „ h : np< . i« an awkward not to sav
Denham Cabinet is an awkward, not to say
humiliating, climb-down. Grant, who is
personally a popular fellow, was got rid
ot some years ago on a flimsy pretext
an error of which his re-appointment is the
correction. However on the present look ot
things, the honor will not exactly die of
senile decay.

MR. CRA VEN BURLEIGH.
(From a recent photograph.)
I am not an advertising quack, but am a business man.
My hair began to grow thin about the time I reached the
age of thirty. When I had reached the age of thirty-five
the top of my head was quite bald. I had considerable
pride, and sought for something to cause renewed growth.
I invested in every preparation that 1 could see or hear of,
and here let me say that not only did I try all the English
so-called restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many sover-
eigns and considerable time—yet the hairs came not. I had
given up the idea of ever growing hair again. One after-
noon, while enjoying a brief business respite by an Alpine
trip, I fell in with an old collegiate chemist, whose hair was
particularly plentiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished
he could spare some of his hair for my benefit. This led
him to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a for-
mula and suggested that I have a quantity of the compound
prepared. I did this a few days later. I was astonished
to see the hair coming forth after several applications, and
within six weeks my head was covered with a firm, yet
soft, growth of hair. Since then the hair on my head has
been as luxuriant as when in my school days. Having
arranged for the legal right to do so, I have for some time
been privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people who,
have actually grown new hair through the medium of my
true hair-grower. I do not pretend to say it is the only
hair-grower in the world—l am not interested in decrying
others —but I do assert that it is the most reliable, anrii in
the end by far the cheapest.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
G.J.P.: You’re very dull this morning.
The sketch was a protest against the wealthy who,
with all their money-bags, dropped a mere dole to
the starving Belgians. It was the “tightness” of
Fat that made Lady Strickland’s fund so compara-
tively beggarly.... S. Smokestack: It’s an aged and

lOst. 101 b W.H.: Thanks; we drink to his safe

sequence. Australian ink is available and it is
used; and excellent stuff it is. Now go and exer-
cise your wit on the Labor Government which per-
petuates the low tariff... .Camp: Can’t reproduce
those verses... .A.C.T.: Yes, the Christmas number
was a special and should have been ordered in ad-
vance. ... S.H.M.: (1) The second trial opened
on March 11, 1901 ; (2) first trial was

certain strictures upon the prelate... .Mrs.

of Versification and the Technicalities of Poetry,” by

“Mil”: “Jermyn Street” (B. 25/2/’15)
shows us the real horrors of war. The
vanquished German may pick up a lot of his
trade as people forget the crimes of Hog-
many ; German workshops may be busy
again, and German fields may grow corn.
But “never again in Europe will the elderly
junker, at any rate, taste the best cham-
pagne.” Neither shall he drink the velvet
clarets of France; he may be even exiled
from the best play-houses in Paris; and
Longchamps and Ascot may refuse to let
him see great races, even if he offer real
money (I don’t think). He shall not hunt
in “the Shires,” nor see the Dublin horse
show, nor shall he be one of the climbers at
’Enley or at Cowes. Having no sculptors,
he must buy his sculptures from England—
'which orders them from Rome mostly, and
forges a name such as Jones or Bull to the
statues produced by fifty-shilling-a-week
Italians. He must drink only Johannis-
berger and Marcobrunner —which are wines
not hard to take—and he is done with
gwouse and partwidges, and has seen his last
of pheasants “coming over on a high wind.”
Death and ruin he may bear with that
stolidity, which in the German is stupidity,
though in his cousin the Englishman it is
stubborn courage; but when gwouse and
partwidges are taken from him the iron will
enter his neck. But this is too cold-blooded,
too cruel. The Germans are bad enough,
but only a fiend would threaten to rob them
of hunting in the shires and shooting at
pheasants “coming over” on hot air, or high
wind, or whatever these devils of sportsmen
call it. Practically German life is to be
reduced to the horrible conditions of exist-
ence in Australia—which is 10,000
miles from the shires, and unable to do a
week-end at the Dublin horse show—and
to threaten to reduce Germany to the level
of Australia is “really quite too shocking.”

“5 minutes make the
difference between
victory and defeat!”
—LORD NELSON
THIS is an age of Petrol. Swift action, swift
transport, will be a deciding factor in the
present titanic upheaval in Europe.
In an important engagement it is easily conceivable
that immortal Nelson’s dictum will be signally
exemplified in the present struggle. Motor-
haulage of field-guns has proved very successful in
manoeuvres. How successful it will be in actual
warfare we are yet to see.
In all questions of motor traction the fuel plays a
vital part.
Pratt’s
Motor Spirit
•
is used by War Office and Admiralty for motor-cars, aeroplanes,
seaplanes, and on the waters for petrol-driven craft. The choice of
Pratt’s Spirit for this important work is a significant tribute to its
sheer merit and consistent reliability. Pratt’s also enjoys the
complete confidence of our Allies, who are using it in enormous
quantities.
In Peace as in War, Pratt’s Spirit is the motorist s first and final
choice because of its unrivalled power and purity.
Vacuum Oil Co. Pty. Ltd.

The Inky Way.
J.0’8.”: The first person to have a civil
claim determined in Australia by a jury
was one Hall, editor of the Sydney Monitor.
The Act making this possible came into
operation in March, 1830, and the frequently-
prosecuted Hall at once rushed into the
Supreme Court with a claim for damages
against the magistrates who had convicted
him of harboring convicts. Wentworth
appeared for plaintiff, who secured £lO
damages. Hall’s next case was an action
against Holy, superintendent of convicts,
claiming damages for trespass; and again
the plaintiff got a verdict— £25. His next
flutter before a jury was against Rev. Ralph
Mansfield, editor of the Government
Gazette, and 12 impartial persons awarded
him 40s. Hall’s successes rejoiced “the
rabble,” but “society” was much grieved.
What. “society” thought of it was pretty
well indicated by Judge James Dowling,
who, in directing on the first case, warned
the jury against “the licentiousness of the
Radical press.”
* X- *
From London: Owing to decreased advts.
and increased costs 212 German newspapers
have thus far disappeared, and about a
hundred in England. The statistics of
London book production in 1914 show that
1941 novels were issued,, as compared with
2285 in 1913. But naval and military books
naturally made a big jump—B9 to 295.
Religion and theology also seem to benefit
m war time—B93 to 1020. Poetry and
drama made a slight advance —582 to 590.
Political economy bounded from 394 to 598.
Essays slumped from 576 to 691; children’s
books from 809 to 727 ; biography and his-
tory from 015 to 593, and travel and adven-
ture from 388 to 348. The figures were, of
course, enormously helped by a very buoyant
Spring.
+ * * *
An editor in kilts is a fearsome example of
misplaced humor. The Bulletin’s wildly-
imaginative story, supposed to have been told
in Berlin concerning the attack by a Turk
and an Afghan on the Broken Hill excursion
train, was solemnly reprinted in the Otago
Daily Times (Dunedin, M.L.), as having
emanated from the Wilhelmstrasse, and was
held up to the ridicule of all good Scots as “a
sample of the news the Germans are fed on.”
Next this yarn will be passed off at Balclutha
as a recipe for haggis, or directions for mak-
ing a sporran.
* * * *
The French censorship continues extra-
ordinarily rigorous. Clemenceau, most
caustic and biting of journalists, had his
paper, The Free Man, suppressed for ex-
cessive candor. He promptly brought out
another, The Fettered Man. That, also,
speedily failed to give satisfaction to the
censors. It came out the other day with
two columns completely blank with the ex-
ception of the signature, “Georges Clemen-
ceau’—all that was left of the leading
article when the censor had done with it.
* * * *
The last line in the report of a deputa-
tion to Chief Sec. Murray (Vic.) published
in a Sydney paper quoted an observation
by that sleepy gentleman, “They do some
funny things in Sydney.” The headline fol-
lowing made the next article read on with
great appropriateness. “Most abominable,”
it exclaimed; and proceeded to give details
of the extent to which abortion is practised
on the shores of the finest harbor in the
world. The stone-hand does the right thing
sometimes.
* * * *
The art-critic of London Times, in his
notice of Bill Dyson’s collection of war car-
toons in the Leicester Square Gallery,
acclaims William as the “best of living
English cartoonists.” The critic (Humphrey
husband of the novelist) emphasises
Dyson’s power of “drawing in epigrams,”
and thinks the artist’s new victim, the
militarist, suits him better than his old one,
the capitalist. Ward’s idea is that it is the
quieter form of irony that hits Fatman
hardest, whereas nothing is too extravagant
for German militarism —“and Dyson draws
the madness of it as no other English artist
could.”
N.S.W. Education Department has given
encouragement to home-grown versifiers by
including “Selections from Australasian
Poets” in the English set for second year
students in secondary schools. Altogether
there are selections from 46 Australasians,
including eight women (Dorothea Mackellar,
by the way, is spelt Mackella) and most
of the corps of best-known Bulletin
writers.
A self-centred little place, London. When
a German-haunted club there, calling itself
the Athenaeum, was closed by the authorities
early in the war, Sydney Telegraph took
the cable as referring to the Athenaeum, home
of bishops and other respectabilities. This
bloomer is now being read in London, and
has caused howls of delight. One paper
calls it the most “monumental of howlers.”
Of course the Telegraph might have
avoided the mistake; “Jeames” instantly
pinned it down in The Bulletin. But
to confuse a club on the other side of the
world with another club of the same name
in the same city, is a thing that could seem
“monumental” only to a place as self-
centred as London is.
* ■ * * *
“Pica de Vinne”: In Egypt another Aus-
tralian soldiers’ paper is promised—this one
a regimental daily, edited by Private J. W.
A. Bradford, the type being put together by
a comp, formerly of Petersburg Times
(S.A. V. Lance-Corporal W. A. Fry, late
editor of the Hevtesbury Reformer, and
Private A. B. Glasson, part-proprietor of
Warracknabeal Post, are to be “the staff.”
They have an assortment of type and an old
machine. The chief difficulty is to secure
paper. Bradford was formerly editor of
Quorn Mercury and other S. Aus. country
papers.
* * -X- *
“Megatherium”: In the day when what
Hilaire Belloc calls “the noble army of the
misprints” shall receive recognition, let not
the cable mutilation miss its meed of praise.
As in all things else, the simplest is the
best, and Morning Pest for Morning
Post still holds its own for neatness and
despatch.
