A Tale of Wet Enthusiasm and
an Over-modest Mermaid,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
is the law of air and sea
By ancient constitution set:
However frolicsome she be,
The pinkest mermaid must keep wet.
She lifts a happy,. laughing head,
And gazes at the iand and sky;
at she would instantly be dead
If once the whole of her got dry.
The selfsame law has made it plain.
While waves shall beat and seaweeds
wreathe,
A man can never long remain
In water without air to breathe;
And this is why a love affair
Is short, though often damp and sweet,
When persons of the upper air
With dimpled ocean maidens meet.
One evening, sitting in the sea,
just as the pleasant moon arose,
A pretty mermaid came to me
And pinched me oil my strong, wet toes.
She was a giri with happy hair,
All greenish gold about the tlilmat,
As exquisitely pink and fair
As anything that keeps afloat.
We had a most delightful hoUh
We sported round the weedy rocks.
I kissed her lips beneath the shoWer
Tossed from her dripping moon-bright
locks.
She was a thing so much alive,
Stich Wonder-laughter lit her face,
I gambolled with her, dive for dive,
Ahd Won the prize in every race.
Thereafter, frequently we met
Oil morns or nights or afternoons.
Together we beheld the set
Of many stars and suns and moons ;
We saw the same utensils rise,
And often, sitting in the spi-ay,
The wild love dawning in her eyes
With rapture took my breath away.
She told me stories of her home,
Of seafldngs set on corai thrones,
Of weed and shell and blanching foam
And rainbow iights o’er seamen’s botteS.
And once she told me of a ship
Nine fathoms deep at lowest tide,
Where minted coinage Used to drip
At seasons through the broken side.
I thought a lot about that tale.
I knew those coins were good to own,
But ail that liquid green and pale
Above the treasure made me groan.
Nine fathoms is too steep a dive
In any ordinary way
For one who cares to keep aiive,
Or hankers for the light of day.
And yet I dreamed that practice might
Make me so used to hold my breath
That I could get that gold, despite
The ancient law of life and death.
So Kate —that was my mermaid’s name—-
Taught me all diving tricks she knew,
Till, as a master of the game,
I hoped my skill would pull me through.
Then, on an evening calm and cool,
1 deemed the prize was in my reach.
Kate met me in a small, blue pool,
Just half a mile from Bondi beach.
The merry dimple on her chin
Played come-and-catch-me as she smiled,
And through the wave we sped to win
The gold some old, lost Spaniard piled.
Down, down the sea-slope sliding green,
Hand linked in hand, we swiftly fell;
Dim through the soft wet twilight seen
Were floating weed and wrinkled shell.
She led me slantwise to my prey
I vaguely felt the metal round,
Crusted with barnacles it lay
In canvas bags with leather bound.
Coins broken from the larger store
Seemed scattered thickly all around,
Discolored on the ocean’s floor
They glimmered softly pound on pound.
But time was short. We could not stay,
Lest, scant of breath, my life should cease ;
We rose to greet the garish day
Bearing a mighty bag apiece.
I filled my tortured lungs with air.
’Twas good to look on sky and land ;
Kate splashing ’mid her soft wet hair
Balanced her treasure in her hand.
Then arm o’er arm we raced ashore,
Parting the pliant evening wave,
To hide the plunder that we bore
High in a small sea guarded cave.
Almost we reached the chosen place,
When, suddenly, I chanced to note
Upon the shoreward water’s face
The swift appearance of a boat.
A large boat driven mad with oil,
It rushed across the seascape fair,
Sent by some evil fate to spoil
Our ready landing anywhere.
For how could we who swam so far
From haunts of men at once explain,
A lady naked as a star,
Bearing the wealth of ancient Spain?
Clad though I was from neck to knee,
I scarce could give a reason fair
Why I should thus come in from sea
Beside a girl with greenish hair.
We lay and floated with the tide,
Trusting Our forms would pass for weed ;
But that confounded boat espied
Our toes and thought we were in need.
So, till the darkness gathered round,
We had to race and duck and dive,
Till I was very nearly drowned
And little more than half alive.
But in the end We gained the shore,
Two miles from where we meant to land,
And that large treasure that we bore,
In spite of all, was still in hand.
We ran across a strip of beach
All soft and warm beneath our feet ;
Then two girls with a boy for each
Came down and cut off our retreat.
A keen breeze stirred the pulse of night,
Along the shore we heard it sigh,
And soon that girl of my delight
From rosy head to foot got dry .
Without some clothes she would not fgce
Those eight keen eyes upon the sand,
And so to save her from disgrace
I simply sat and held her hand.
The wavelets made a gentle swish,
But that was fifty yards away,
She faded like a jelly-fish
That short of moisture cannot stay.
Oh, heavy was this heart of mine
To see her wilt and melt and go,
My sweet companion of the brine
That lived and kissed an hour ago.
Still, there was all that treasure left
To give me comfort as I lay
Of love and decent clothes bereft—
I hugged it till the break of day
Tlleh in the time of dawn and dew .
I said one loud explosive “damn,”
For morning only brought tp view
Brass buttons made ill Birmingham.
N.S.W,
PAT O'MAORI.
Colonial Experience,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was a blazing hot day on the open downs,
and Dick Wells, crouching under the
scanty shade afforded by his saddle and
cloth hung on the wire fence cursed the
station, the flies, the heat, the whole of
Australia with fine impartiality. He had
just finished his lunch, which consisted of
dry bread and meat, and a huge hunk of
brownie, washed down with a quart of tea.
Ilis horse stood fastened to the fence,
stamping his feet at the flies, and chewing
the top of the post in a dispirited way.
Dick had only partially finished his work
of repairing and straightening up a section
of the six-wire fence, and was waiting for
the heat to abate a little before he resumed
the job.
He gave way to a fresh outburst of pro*
fanity on discovering that he had lost his
pipe; and peevishly flung his tobacco and
knife as far as he could in the long grass.
The motion of throwing shifted him slightly
from where he sat, and he recognised some-
thing hard under him. It proved to be the
missing pipe.
Feeling that nothing mattered much, and
that the situation could not be exm-egged in
mere words, he scrambled slowly to his feet,
and went in search of the tobacco and knife.
After a quarter of an hour’s search he found
the knife, but his best efforts could not bring
the tobacco to light again.
Disconsolately sucking the empty pipe he
was returning to the shade, when he spied
a horseman riding towards him along the
fence. The newcomer was a tough, wiry,
ginger-whiskered man of about 50, who
greeted him with a brief “Goo’-day. Hot,
isn’t it? Got any water to spare?”
Dick returned his salute with, “Goo’-day.
It’s a fair cow. There’s the water-bag.
You’ll find a drop in it.”
“Wot possessed you to pull up here fur a
camp?” said the newcomer. “There’s a
good shady creek about four miles on ; and
plenty of water. I wuz just makin’ fur it
meself.”
“I didn’t camp here for a sunbath,” said
Dick. “I’m repairin’ the blanked fence.
The overseer says the boundary riders on
your place mus’ ride round with their eyes
shut, as you never seem to do any repairs to
the boundary.”
“Oh, ’im,” said the ginger man. “’E
thinks a boundary rider ort to carry a crow-
bar and shovel in ’is saddle pouch. I
worked fur ’im fur two munce, but ’is col-
lars wuz too tight a fit fur me. ’E give me
one ’undred and forty miles of fence to ride
one day, and told me to make a good job of
it. 1 wuz a week doin’ it; and w’en I
finished it I give ’im notice. ’Ee’s mad as
a snake.”
“Got any tobacco?” said Dick, “I lost
mine somewhere along the fence.”
Ginger threw him his plug, and proceeded
to make a fire to boil his quartpot with bark
pulled off the posts. He also pulled off his
saddle and cloth and hung them on the fence
for shade, but hobbled his horse out.
“Been on Wyandra long?” he qderied.
“Three munce,” said Dick. “It ain’t
much of a place to work on, but it’s about as
good as most others. I’m tryin’ to save up
a few bob, and I’m goin’ down to see the
nex’ Cup run, and if I can make a bit of a
punch I’m off to England again. This
flamin’ country ain’t fit fur a white man to
live in. I’ve been in it seven years, and I
hates it worse every day.”
“I’ve been in it all my life,” said Ginger,
“and me father before me, and I don’t see
nuthin’ wrong with it. You blanked new-
chums think becos the beer ain’t laid on to
every paddock on a stashun that the country
is a fair dog ’ole of a place. Wot wuz you
doin’ in England?”
“Nuthin, ” said Dick, “but me old man
wuz a butcher in Manchester, and ’e ’ad a
pile of money, and a fellow ’ad a good time.”
“Umph,” grunted Ginger. “I ain’t s’prised
at you wantin’ to go back. You wuz ■ a
blanky ijit to leave it in the fust instance.”
“I ’ad to,” said Dick. “Wuz goin’ into
(Continued on next page.)

Urgently WANT MORE ZAM-BUK
for their Cuts, Wounds and Sore Feet
REMARKABLE LETTERS FROM THE FRONT
“A pot of Zam-Buk out here is like a loaf of bread to
a starving man. On the retreat from Mons we had a
forced march of 37 miles, and not a man who used
Zain-Buk on his feet fell out.”
In these graphic terms a Distinguished Conduct Medallist, Lance-
Corporal J. P. Delaney, 2nd Royal Irish Regiment, expresses his
warm appreciation of Zam-Buk, in a letter dated Nov. 24. He
adds: “I can safely recommend Zam-Buk to everyone as being the
best healer on the market. P.S.—Please excuse writing and
pencil, as I am writing this from the trenches, and I cannot
tell when or where the next shell is going to drop. The
Germans are sky-larking again with their ‘Coal-Boxes.’ ”
In my opinion every soldier should have a pot of
Zam-Buk in his kit when ‘trekking,’ as Zam-Buk
is the most valuable addition to
his marching powers.”
Thus writes another Distinguished Con-
duct Medallist, Private A. Mutlow, of the
2nd South Staffs Regiment. In his pen-
cilled note, dated Dec. sth, from “Some-
where in France,” the gallant
private adds: —
“I am a constant user of your
Zam-Buk for chafed legs (from
which I suffer on long marches).
I brought a pot out here from
Aldershot, and I may
mention it is in great de-
mand among my comrades
who are likewise afflicted.
Zam-Buk is also very
effective in the case of
galled feet and blistered
heels.”
“We soldiers are al-
ways glad to see a pot
of Zam-Buk, for it is
the best of all ‘First-
aids.’ Zam-Buk is
one of our best friends
out here.”

The flagic Art of Beauty Culture.
SOME HOME RECIPES.
By RITA MOYA.
A comnlexion that appears clear, fresh and natural is as necessary to the smart woman
as a modish gown. More attention should be given to keeping the skin ‘fat than m u
details of dress. The face, constantly exposed to wind, dust, fatigue and strain, requires
regular and watchful care. One great cause of complexion troubles is the frequen u.
greasy, inactive preparations which clog the pores and prevent the natural throwing
of waste matter. My repeated advice is to avoid made-up cosmetics, and to use only
ingredients. The various aids to beauty which I recommend are simple, and if not alieac >
at hand, can be procured from any reliable chemist. If he has not. what you require, ,
can easily obtain it for you from his wholesaler. Only let me advise you to insist o
ing the original ingredients, and not to accept some made-up preparation insteau.
About Shampoos. —To quote an eminent
London beauty specialist: “The slimy egg
cannot dissolve the scalp impurities, but only
adds to the trouble by completely choking the
pores with animal matter.” The substance
sticks and decomposes, thereby causing the
hair to assume a dead, “dull color.” I heart-
ily endorse every word. Avoid eggs (and
soap, too) on your hair. Try the delightful pre-
paration made by stirring a teaspoonful of
stallax in a cup of hot. water. It will bring
out new beauty possibilities in your hair, will
clear and prevent dandruff, and leave that
flue, fluffy effect so much sought after. It
also removes excess oil or greasiness.
Complexion Secrets of an Actress. —In a re-
cently issued volume bearing the above title
the author says: “Continual use of grease
paints, rouge and the like, has ruined my
comnlexion. My skin -was colorless, wrinkled,
coarse and punctured with large pores. In
America I heard of the virtues of mercolised
wax; my first experience with this marvellous
substance convinced me it was more valuable
than all the cosmetics combined. Now, when-
ever my complexion begins to go wrong, I get
a small quantity of mercolised wax at the
chemist’s, spread on a thin layer of it before
retiring, washing it off in the morning. The
wax, after a few such applications, seems
literally to absorb the worn-out cuticle, when
a brighter, healthier, younger looking skin
appears.
Face Fuzz. —Many women know how to re-
move superfluous hair tempdrarily, but to
banish it for ever is quite another matter. As
regards depilatories, I must say that there are
very few good ones. They nearly all irritate
the skin, and even then only give temporary
relief. Powdered pheminol acts in a wonder-
ful manner, and the recommended treatment
is designed not only to immediately remove
the ugly hairs, but to also permanently des-
troy the roots.
Scanty Eyebrows and Fashes. —What a
wealth of expression can be given to an other-
wise plain face by fine arching eyebrow's end
long curling lashes! Much care should be
exercised, however, in choosing a P?. m . a<^®.^° r
promoting the growth of either, as ft is pi a■ -
tically impossible to keep the lids tightly cl°._
when treating the lashes. Mennalme has t e
advantage of being perfectly harmless, and at
the same time a healthy stimulant to the la
follicles. Its use tends to darken the new
growth, which presumably is what most
women desire.
Is Powder Necessary?—l say emphatically
No! There is a simple lotion which can be
easily and cheaply made at home, and it is
at the same time both effective and beneficial
to the complexion. Cleminite is a splendid
substitute for face powder, which is at tne
bottom of many complexion troubles, tmt
about an ounce from the chemist s and dis-
solve in four tablespoonsful of water, the.
result is a fine clear liquid, which instantly
gives the face, neck or arms that peach-liKe
bloom of perfect health. There is nothing to
equal it for greasy skins, and the result lasts
all day long under the most trying conditions.
Try it for the next dance.
Falling Hair.—How often one hears the
lament: “I have tried everything on the
market, and my hair comes out in handfuls.
Not so surprising either yhen you come to
think it over. Hair tonics to be effective must
be fresh, and there is no earthly reason why
every woman should not make her own lotion
at home. The finest vegetable tonic I know
of is made by mixing a packet of boranium
with 1-pint of bay rum and adding sufficient
water to fill a half-pint bottle. This lotion
rubbed briskly into the scalp sets the hair
roots tingling with new life, and will, if per-
severed with, give you back your crowning
glory.”
For Pale Faces.—Some folks are naturally
pale, and I see no harm whatever in adding a
little color to the cheeks if so desired. Rouge,
however, is always obvious, and to people or
refinement somewhat vulgar. There is a sub-
stance, however, known as powdered colli-
andum, which gives a perfectly natural color,
and at the same time defies detection. Apply
a little with the finger tips. You will be
pleased with the result I feel sure. [4

gallopin’ consumpshun, and an interferin’
fool of a doctor told the old man it was
hrough drink and ’e ’ad better send me to
a dry climate or I’d peg out inside a year.
There ain’t nuthin’ to complain of on the
score of dryness in this dam’ place. It’s
been mostly as dry as ’Ell everywhere I’ve
been, but I’d rather ’ave died of gallopin’
consumshun than ’ave come ’ere if I’d only
’ave known as much then as I do now.”
Ginger rose and made his quart of tea and
returned to the shade.
“Is this, the on’y game you’ve taken on
since you come to the country?” he inquired.
“No,” replied Dick; “it ain’t. Up to a
few weeks ago it wuz the on’y game I ’adn’t
taken on. I’ve ’ad a go at everything;
from runnin’ a billiard table to offsidin* fur
a Chinaman cook; from tea tastin’ to bul-
lock-driving. I put in three munce cane-
cuttin’ near Bundaberg once, and made
better money there than I made anywhere,
and then they’ll tell you that a white man
can’t cut cane. I wuz on’y a chummy, and
not too robustious, but I made 15 bob a day
and tucker easy. I ’ad enuff saved up that
time to take me back to Manchester, but
done it all in, in one night, at a two-up
school. It’s a rotten country, all right.”
“W’y, wot are you growlin’ about the
country fur? You say you made good wages
—more in one day than you’d make in a week
in England, and then you blames the conn-'
try becos you wuz bass enuff to lose it all
gaffin’. Strike me fat, some of you imported
blokes is the limit. You live on ’oof parin’
soup and ’ominy in England, and go into
gallopin’ consumshun frum starvashun; and
then you come out ’ere w’ere the mutton is
fairly snappin’ at you as you ride along, and
you ain’t got a good word fur the country.
I wouldn’t mind bettin’ if you went to
’Eaven, w’ich as old Euclid says is ridic’l’us,
you’d complain becos there wuzn’t any
devils there fur you to ’sociate with. Wot
else wuz you at?”
“Fust thing I took on w’en I come out
wuz a job of waitin’ in a resterong in Syd-
ney,” said Dick. “I wuz told that ’arf-crown
tips to waiters wuz quite common in Orstra-
liar. I wuz there three weeks, and I didn’t
get more than ’arf a crown altogether in tips
the fust week. The next week I got five
bob, and the next week I got the sack, and
ten bob wuz deducted frum me wages fur
breakin’ a pile of crockery. After that I
wuz pencillin’ fur a booky, but I think ’e
mistook me fur one of those ’ere phenomenil
litinin’ calculators. ’E wuz the most sar-
castik man ever I met. Then I got a job
drivin’ a milk cart in the subbubs. Wuz
gettin’ on all right at that until the ’orse
bolted one morning and upset the cart,
breakin’ off both sharves. ’E wuz headed fur
the Blue Mountains w’en I seen ’im last.
Of course the boss blamed me fur it, instead
of the mongrel dog wot ’eeled the ’orse. I
tried to explain, but ’e wouldn’t listen. I
’ad to summons ’im to get me week’s wages.
’E wuz a fair cow.”
“But,” interrupted Ginger, “do you mean
to say your old man sent you out without
any dough?”
“Oh, I ’ad five ’undred quid w’en I landed,
w’ich I wuz supposed to buy a dairy farm
with ; but I mostly done it in on the little
’eifers in Sydney in the fust month, and ’ad
a slap-up time, too.”
He was silent for a while as he dreamily
pictured some of the Sydney “ ’eifers.” and
the good time he had had. Then he re-
sumed :
“After gettin’ the kick-out as milk-o I
come to Queensland as rouseabout fur
Young & 00. to Milo. Went wool snatchin’
on the board. I reckon I run 40 miles a
day fur three weeks at Milo shed. I wuz
too tired some nights to go to sleep, but
the tucker wuz good. I’d ’ave seen the shed
out, on’y my gallopin’ consumshun didn’t
like opposisliun. and I ’ad to quit gallopin’
up and down the board. I told the boss of
the shed I wuz too delicate fur a long
distance runner, and asked ’im to put me
at something easier. ’E said ’e ’ad ’is eye
on me fur some time, and reckoned it wuz
more laziness than delicacy wot I wuz
troubled with, and paid me off. I wuzn’t
much upset, as shearers are a lot of blanky
savages anyway. After that I ’ad a trip on
the road with sheep. If it ’ad been anybody
else I’d ’ave laughed at myself offen at that
game. Fust thing w’en I got the job the
drover asked me if I could ride; and me
thinkin’ ’e meant sit on a nuiet ’orse I said I
wuz a good rider. ’E told me to jump on
’is old moke and run the mob of ’orses back
to the camp frum w’ere they wuz feed in’.
I knew how to climb up, as I ’ad offen
scrambled on old Milk-0 in Sydney. After
I got on I knew wot the boss and the other
blokes wuz smilin’ about. That pig of a
’orse seemed to bunch ’is four legs up in a
heap, and put ’is ’ead down w’ere ’is front
feet ought to be. Then ’e sprung about five
feet in the air. snuealin’ like a hog. The
girth broke, and I airyplaned off with the
saddle. I started to rouse some about rotten
gear, until I seen the boss fittin’ a new
girth. Then I told ’im I ’ad jinked me
back, so ’e gave me a quiet old moke wot
used to reach round and grab me by the
leg if I sat on ’im w’en ’e wuz stamlin’
still. About the second or third night we
wuz camped on a little, narrow creek with
only a little water in it. The boss told
me w’en I went on watch to be careful no
sheep got out. It wuz about a ’undred
yards frum one calico wing to the other,
and I’m walkin’ up and down along the
front with a whip ready to chase back any
sheep wot would try to break away, till
the boss sang out that I needn’t be doin’
so much blanky trampin’ about keepin’
everyone awake. I ’ad to watch two hours,
and towards the finish I could hear sheep
bleatin’ like fun the other side of the creek,
but didn’t think nothin’ of it till I called
the nex’ man. ’E wuz generally pretty
’ard to wake, and I ’ad to call ’im three
times and in the finish pull the blankets
away frum ’im before ’e seemed to wake
properly. ’E sat up and wuz pullin’ on ’is
boots grumblin’ to ’imself about ’ow cold
it wuz. Then all of a sudden ’e listened
and give a yell of, ‘All aboard. All the
blanky sheep are out.’
“The boss and all ’ands jumned up like
mad and off. There wuz on’y about a
’undred sheen left on camp out of about ten
thousand. The rest of them ’ad been cross-
in’ the water all the time I wuz on watch,
and it wuz them I could ’ear bleatin’ up the
paddick. We got them all again that night
and about a thousand of the stashun sheep
with them, and we ’ad to lose a day and
take them to a yard and draft them.”
“I s’pose the boss chased you right oiu
west over that lot,” said Ginger.
“ ’E surged a bit at fust, and the other
blokes cliiaeked me a lot, but the boss said
afterwards it wuz ’is own silly fault fur
not tliinkin’ to tell a dam’ importation that
w’en sheep wuz bleatin’ it wuz a pretty good
sign they wuz gettin’ out somew’ere. I done
three trips with ’im altogether and ’e wuz
pretty good. I might ’ave been with ’im
yet on’y fur a strike fur ’igher wages on
the last trip, w’ich the other cows made me
join in; and I wuz fool enuff to let them
persuade me to be the spokesman. The
strike didn’t last more than as long as it
took the boss to tell us wot ’e thought of us,
and the other cows all went on again at the
same rate of wages, and poor Pilgarlic got
the kick out as an agitator, an ungrateful
sw r ab, and a galvanized Cockney. I wrote
to me old man then and asked ’im to send
me a ’undred at once, as a feller ’ad asked
me to join ’im in a big butcherin’ business
in Rock’ampton, and I ’adn’t quite enuff
money fur a ’alf share in it. ’E sent me
the money too, and also sent me some books
on butcherin’. I swapped the books in Long-
reach fur some of Nat Gould’s yarns, and ’ad a
bonzer time in Rocky fur a month or more
on the cash. W’en it wuz done I went down
to the cane-cuttin’. After I ’ad been taken
down fur my sugar money I wrote to the
old chap again for another ’undred. I told
’im my partner ’ad got killed in a railway
accident, and I ’ad bought some ’eifers with
the cash, but they ’ad all died in a drought,
and I was a rooined man again. ’E wuz
evidently gettin’ advice frum ’is brother wot
wuz always sayin’ I wuz a useless young
dog, and wouldn’t come to no good ; becos ’e
wrote and told me ’e wouldn’t send me any
more money, but if I wuz well and strong,
and wanted to go ’ome ’e would send the
money fur my passage to the manager of a
bank in Brisbane, and ’e would see me on
the boat and buy my ticket.”
“Well,” said Ginger, “if your old man ’ad
sense enuff to be a butcher in Manchester
I should think ’e would ’ave ’ad sense enuff
of ’is own, without drawin’ on ’is brother’s
lot, to reckon you wanted a kickin’-strap
pretty badly. Do you booze?”
“Well, I generally ’as a feed of beer fur a
few days w’en I ’its a town fust,” said Dick.
“Once w’en I wuz in the sugar country I
palled up with a railway man ’oo wuz frum
Manchester. ’E wuz porter at a small
stashun on the North Coast line. I used
to often go doAvn to the stashun w’en ’e
wuz on night duty, and take a bottle of
whisky with me. One night we started
drinkin’ whisky and playin’ euchre w’ile ’e
wuz waitin’ fur the train w’ich wuz due
about two o’clock. We both got full as
ticks, and rolled under the table in the office.
The train came along, and, of course, the
signal wuz against ’er. She blew ’er whistle
till she nearly busted ’er boiler, and waited,
and waited, blowin’ like smoke every few
minits. Presently the guard came along
carryin’ a lamp and swearin’ like mad. ’E
fust tried to wake the night officer, ’oo told
’im ’e ’ad made it spades, and not to be
lookin’ at ’is ’and. Then ’e tried to wake
me, but I told ’im to go to the Devil, as I
wuz seasick, and ’oped the ship would sink
with all ’ands before mornin’.
“’E left us then, and went over and
rooted out the stashun-master, ’oo wuz in
bed at ’is own place. ’E wuz a big, angry-
lookin’ Scotchman with such a short temper
that it wuz ’ardly worth callin’ a temper at
all.
“ ’E let the train through fust, and then ’e
come to the office and poured a bucket of
water over the porter, and shook ’im until
’is boots flew off. ’Avin’ got ’im on ’is feet
’e come to me and took me by the ’eels and
dragged me out of the office. ’E poured a
bucket of freezin’ cold water over me stum-
mick, and w’en I got up ’e booted me out of
the stashun yard, liftin’ me over the fence at
the finish. Until ’e put me down and I ’ad time
to look round. I thought it wuz an escaped
elefant wot wuz bellerin’ and kickin’ be’ind
me. ’E 'ad the nerve then to tell me to
come and see ’im in the mornin’ w’en I wuz
sober, as ’e ’ad a conshense wot wouldent
allow ’im to maul a drunken man about as
much as ’ed_ like to.”
“Did you'go back?” inquired Ginger.
“Go back ! Did you ever ’ear of Daniel
breakin’ ’is way into the lions’ den again
after ’e got out? The porter was suspended,
and ’e wanted me to go and explain to old
McDougal ’ow it wuz all my fault. But ’e
could ’ave sacked the whole of the railway
staff, and I wouldn’t ’ave gone near ’im
again. After that I wuz billiard-markin’ in
Maryborough, but that game wuz too mono-
tonous. and I found I wuz gettin’ too fond
of suckin’ at the beer pump. Then I got a
job with a blacksmith, and ’ad only been
three ’ours with ’im, w’en the ’and ’ammer
I wuz usin’ knockin’ old bolts out of a
waggin slipped and ’it ’im a crack be’ind
the ear and outed ’im. I stayed long enuff
to see ’e wuzn’t killed, but I didn’t wait fur
my cheque. You see, I ’ad ’it ’im on the ’and
earlier in the day, and ’e made a grab to
’eave the anvil at me ; so there weren’t no
sense in waitin’ to apologise to ’im.
“After that I done some wharf-lumpin’
in Rocky, and wuz cleanin’ out tram lines fur
a w’ile. Then I got a job to come out to
Longreach with a mob of rams on the train
and prod them with a long stick w’enever
they wuz lyin’ down. They give me a lot of
trouble, and I poked one feller’s eye out.
There wuz one wrinkly-necked, concertina
old chap amung them wot insisted in lyin’
down. ’E ’ad ’orns as thick as me leg and
a cunnin’ look in ’is eye. ’E knew me again
w’en ’e saw me, as ’e nearly drove me
through the end of the truck w’en we wuz
gettin’ them out at Longreach. ’E broke
two ribs and put me in the ’ospital fur a
week. I got this job soon after, and ’ere
I’m stoppin’ by the grace of God and the
goodwill of the boss and overseer until nex’
October, w’en I’m off to Melbourne to see
the Cup ; and now, if you’ll stop and give me
a ’and fur an hour or two we’ll finish that
fence to-night.”
“It seems to me,” said Ginger, thought-
fully, “that your life’s been a bit crowded.”
MANWELL DAVIS.
